CHAPTER XXXVII  CHAPTER XXXVIII  CHAPTER XXXIX  CHAPTER XL  CHAPTER XLI  CHAPTER XLII  CHAPTER XLIII  CHAPTER XLIV  CHAPTER XLV     THE BLUE CASTLE     CHAPTER I   

If it had not rained on a certain May morning Valancy Stirling’s whole life would have been entirely different.

She would have gone, with the rest of her clan, [clan - клан]  to Aunt Wellington’s engagement  [engagement - помолвка] picnic and Dr. Trent would have gone to Montreal.

But it did rain and you shall hear what happened to her because of it.  

Valancy wakened early, in the lifeless, [lifeless - безжизненный]  hopeless hour just preceding  [preceding - предшествующий] dawn. [dawn - рассвет] 

She had not slept very well.

One does not sleep well, sometimes, when one is twenty-nine on the morrow, and unmarried, in a community  [community - сообщество] and connection where the unmarried are simply those who have failed to get a man.  

Deerwood and the Stirlings had long since relegated Valancy to hopeless old maidenhood.

But Valancy herself had never quite relinquished  [relinquished - покинутый] a certain pitiful, shamed, little hope that Romance  [Romance - романтика] would come her way yet—never, until this wet, horrible morning, when she wakened to the fact that she was twenty-nine and unsought by any man.  

Ay, _there_ lay the sting.

Valancy did not mind so much being an old maid.

After all, she thought, being an old maid couldn’t possibly be as dreadful  [dreadful - ужасный] as being married to an Uncle Wellington or an Uncle Benjamin, or even an Uncle Herbert.

What hurt her was that she had never had a chance to be anything but an old maid.

No man had ever desired  [desired - желанный] her.  

The tears came into her eyes as she lay there alone in the faintly  [faintly - слабо] greying darkness. [darkness - тьма] 

She dared not let herself cry as hard as she wanted to, for two reasons.

She was afraid that crying might bring on another attack of that pain around the heart.

She had had a spell  [spell - заклинание] of it after she had got into bed—rather worse than any she had had yet.

And she was afraid her mother would notice her red eyes at breakfast and keep at her with minute, persistent, [persistent - настойчивый]  mosquito-like questions regarding the cause  [cause - причина] thereof.  

But of course appearances should be kept up.

“It is not,” Valancy could hear her mother’s prim, dictatorial voice asserting, [asserting - утверждающий]  “it is not _maidenly_ to think about _men_.”  

The thought of her mother’s expression made Valancy laugh—for she had a sense of humour  [humour - юмор] nobody in her clan  [clan - клан] suspected. [suspected - подозреваемый] 

For that matter, there were a good many things about Valancy that nobody suspected. [suspected - подозреваемый] 

But her laughter was very superficial  [superficial - поверхностный] and presently  [presently - в настоящее время] she lay there, a huddled, futile little figure, listening to the rain pouring down outside and watching, with a sick distaste, the chill, [chill - холод]  merciless light creeping  [creeping - ползучий] into her ugly, sordid room.  

She knew the ugliness of that room by heart—knew it and hated it.

There were only two pictures that were not of relatives.

One, an old chromo of a puppy sitting on a rainy doorstep. [doorstep - порог] 

That picture always made Valancy unhappy.

That forlorn little dog crouched on the doorstep  [doorstep - порог] in the driving rain!

Why didn’t _some one_ open the door and let him in?

The other picture was a faded, passe-partouted engraving of Queen Louise coming down a stairway, [stairway - лестница]  which Aunt Wellington had lavishly given her on her tenth birthday.

For nineteen years she had looked at it and hated it, beautiful, smug, [smug - самодовольный]  self-satisfied Queen Louise.

But she never dared  [dared - смеется] destroy it or remove it.

Mother and Cousin Stickles would have been aghast, or, as Valancy irreverently expressed it in her thoughts, would have had a fit. [fit - подходит]   

Every room in the house was ugly, of course.

But downstairs appearances were kept up somewhat. [somewhat - несколько] 

There was no money for rooms nobody ever saw.

Valancy sometimes felt that she could have done something for her room herself, even without money, if she were permitted.

But her mother had negatived every timid  [timid - робкий] suggestion and Valancy did not persist. [persist - настойчивый] 

Valancy never persisted. [persisted - настойчивый] 

She was afraid to.

Her mother could not brook opposition.

Mrs. Stirling would sulk for days if offended, [offended - обиженный]  with the airs of an insulted duchess.  

The only thing Valancy liked about her room was that she could be alone there at night to cry if she wanted to.  

But, after all, what did it matter if a room, which you used for nothing except sleeping and dressing in, were ugly?

Valancy was never permitted to stay alone in her room for any other purpose.

People who wanted to be alone, so Mrs. Frederick Stirling and Cousin Stickles believed, could only want to be alone for some sinister  [sinister - зловещий] purpose.

But her room in the Blue Castle was everything a room should be.  

Valancy, so cowed and subdued and overridden and snubbed in real life, was wont to let herself go rather splendidly in her day-dreams.

Nobody in the Stirling clan, [clan - клан]  or its ramifications, suspected  [suspected - подозревается] this, least of all her mother and Cousin Stickles.

They never knew that Valancy had two homes—the ugly red brick  [brick - кирпич] box of a home, on Elm Street, and the Blue Castle in Spain.

Valancy had lived spiritually in the Blue Castle ever since she could remember.

She had been a very tiny child when she found herself possessed of it.

Everything wonderful and beautiful was in that castle.

Jewels that queens might have worn; robes of moonlight  [moonlight - лунный свет] and fire; couches of roses  [roses - розы] and gold; long flights of shallow marble steps, with great, white urns, and with slender, mist- [mist - туман] clad maidens going up and down them; courts, marble-pillared, where shimmering fountains fell and nightingales sang among the myrtles; halls of mirrors that reflected only handsome knights and lovely women—herself the loveliest of all, for whose glance men died.

All that supported her through the boredom of her days was the hope of going on a dream spree at night.

Most, if not all, of the Stirlings would have died of horror if they had known half the things Valancy did in her Blue Castle.  

For one thing she had quite a few lovers in it.

Oh, only one at a time.

One who wooed  [wooed - сватались] her with all the romantic ardour of the age of chivalry and won her after long devotion and many deeds  [deeds - деяния] of derring-do, and was wedded  [wedded - поженились] to her with pomp and circumstance  [circumstance - обстоятельство] in the great, banner- [banner - знамя] hung chapel of the Blue Castle.  

At twelve, this lover was a fair lad  [lad - парень] with golden  [golden - золотые] curls  [curls - кудри] and heavenly blue eyes.

At fifteen, he was tall and dark and pale, but still necessarily  [necessarily - необходимо] handsome.

At twenty, he was ascetic, dreamy, [dreamy - мечтательный]  spiritual.

At twenty-five, he had a clean-cut jaw, [jaw - челюсть]  slightly grim, [grim - мрачный]  and a face strong and rugged rather than handsome.

I don’t say Valancy deliberately  [deliberately - нарочито] murdered these lovers as she outgrew  [outgrew - перерос] them.

One simply faded  [faded - блек] away as another came.

Things are very convenient in this respect in Blue Castles.  

But, on this morning of her day of fate, [fate - судьба]  Valancy could not find the key of her Blue Castle.

Reality pressed on her too hardly, barking  [barking - лающий] at her heels like a maddening little dog.

She was twenty-nine, lonely, undesired, ill-favoured—the only homely  [homely - некрасивый] girl in a handsome clan, [clan - клан]  with no past and no future.

As far as she could look back, life was drab and colourless, [colourless - бесцветный]  with not one single crimson or purple spot anywhere.

As far as she could look forward it seemed certain to be just the same until she was nothing but a solitary, little withered leaf clinging  [clinging - цепляющийся] to a wintry bough.

The moment when a woman realises that she has nothing to live for—neither love, duty, purpose nor hope—holds for her the bitterness of death.  

“And I just have to go on living because I can’t stop.

I may have to live eighty years,” thought Valancy, in a kind of panic. [panic - паника] 

“We’re all horribly long-lived.

It sickens me to think of it.”  

She was glad it was raining—or rather, she was drearily satisfied that it was raining.

There would be no picnic that day.

This annual picnic, whereby Aunt and Uncle Wellington—one always thought of them in that succession— [succession - преемственность] inevitably  [inevitably - неизбежно] celebrated their engagement  [engagement - помолвка] at a picnic thirty years before, had been, of late years, a veritable nightmare  [nightmare - кошмар] to Valancy.

There seemed to be nothing of the revolutionary in her nature.

And she knew exactly what every one would say to her at the picnic.

Uncle Wellington, whom she disliked and despised  [despised - презираемый] even though he had fulfilled  [fulfilled - исполненный] the highest Stirling aspiration, [aspiration - стремление]  “marrying money,” would say to her in a pig’s whisper, “Not thinking of getting married yet, my dear? [dear - дорогой] ” and then go off into the bellow of laughter with which he invariably  [invariably - неизменно] concluded his dull  [dull - скучный] remarks. [remarks - замечания] 

Aunt Wellington, of whom Valancy stood in abject awe, would tell her about Olive’s new chiffon dress and Cecil’s last devoted  [devoted - преданный] letter.

Valancy would have to look as pleased and interested as if the dress and letter had been hers or else Aunt Wellington would be offended. [offended - обиженный] 

And Valancy had long ago decided that she would rather offend  [offend - обиженный] God than Aunt Wellington, because God might forgive her but Aunt Wellington never would.  

Aunt Alberta, enormously  [enormously - безмерно] fat, with an amiable habit of always referring to her husband as “he,” as if he were the only male creature in the world, who could never forget that she had been a great beauty in her youth, would condole with Valancy on her sallow skin—  “I don’t know why all the girls of today are so sunburned.

When _I_ was a girl my skin was roses  [roses - розы] and cream.

I was counted the prettiest girl in Canada, my dear. [dear - дорогой] ”  

Perhaps Uncle Herbert wouldn’t say anything—or perhaps he would remark jocularly, “How fat you’re getting, Doss!”

And then everybody would laugh over the excessively humorous idea of poor, scrawny little Doss getting fat.  

And Uncle Benjamin would ask some of his abominable conundrums, between wheezy chuckles, and answer them himself.  

“What is the difference between Doss and a mouse?  

“The mouse wishes to harm the cheese and Doss wishes to charm  [charm - обаяние] the he’s.”  

Valancy had heard him ask that riddle fifty times and every time she wanted to throw something at him.

But she never did.

In the first place, the Stirlings simply did not throw things; in the second place, Uncle Benjamin was a wealthy  [wealthy - богатый] and childless  [childless - бездетный] old widower  [widower - вдовец] and Valancy had been brought up in the fear and admonition of his money.

If she offended  [offended - обиженный] him he would cut her out of his will—supposing she were in it.

Valancy did not want to be cut out of Uncle Benjamin’s will.

She had been poor all her life and knew the galling bitterness of it.

So she endured  [endured - перетерпевший] his riddles  [riddles - загадки] and even smiled tortured  [tortured - истязаемый] little smiles over them.  

Aunt Isabel, downright and disagreeable  [disagreeable - неприятный] as an east wind, would criticise her in some way—Valancy could not predict just how, for Aunt Isabel never repeated a criticism— [criticism - критика] she found something new with which to jab you every time.

Aunt Isabel prided herself on saying what she thought, but didn’t like it so well when other people said what _they_ thought to _her_.

Valancy never said what _she_ thought.  

Cousin Georgiana—named after her great-great-grand- [grand - грандиозный] mother, who had been named after George the Fourth—would recount dolorously the names of all relatives and friends who had died since the last picnic and wonder “which of us will be the first to go next.”  

Oppressively competent, [competent - компетентный]  Aunt Mildred would talk endlessly  [endlessly - бесконечно] of her husband and her odious prodigies of babies to Valancy, because Valancy would be the only one she could find to put up with it.

For the same reason, Cousin Gladys—really First Cousin Gladys once removed, according to the strict way in which the Stirlings tabulated relationship—a tall, thin lady who admitted she had a sensitive  [sensitive - чувствительный] disposition, [disposition - нрав]  would describe minutely the tortures of her neuritis.

There would be none of all this today.

And there would be no packing up of teaspoons. [teaspoons - чайные ложки] 

The packing up was always left for Valancy and Cousin Stickles.

And once, six years ago, a silver teaspoon  [teaspoon - чайная ложка] from Aunt Wellington’s wedding set had been lost.

Valancy never heard the last of that silver teaspoon. [teaspoon - чайная ложка] 

Its ghost appeared Banquo-like at every subsequent  [subsequent - последующие] family feast.  

Oh, yes, Valancy knew exactly what the picnic would be like and she blessed  [blessed - благословенные] the rain that had saved her from it.

There would be no picnic this year.

If Aunt Wellington could not celebrate on the sacred  [sacred - священные] day itself she would have no celebration at all.

Thank whatever gods  [gods - боги] there were for that.  

Since there would be no picnic, Valancy made up her mind that, if the rain held up in the afternoon, she would go up to the library and get another of John Foster’s books.

Valancy was never allowed to read novels, but John Foster’s books were not novels.

They were “nature books”—so the librarian told Mrs. Frederick Stirling—“all about the woods and birds and bugs  [bugs - жуки] and things like that, you know.”

So Valancy was allowed to read them—under protest, for it was only too evident  [evident - очевидные] that she enjoyed them too much.

It was permissible, even laudable, to read to improve your mind and your religion, but a book that was enjoyable  [enjoyable - приятные] was dangerous.

Valancy did not know whether her mind was being improved or not; but she felt vaguely that if she had come across John Foster’s books years ago life might have been a different thing for her.

They seemed to her to yield  [yield - урожай] glimpses  [glimpses - проблески] of a world into which she might once have entered, though the door was forever barred to her now.

It was only within the last year that John Foster’s books had been in the Deerwood library, though the librarian told Valancy that he had been a well-known writer for several years.  

“Where does he live?”

Valancy had asked.  

“Nobody knows.

From his books he must be a Canadian, but no more information can be had.

His publishers won’t say a word.

Quite likely John Foster is a nom de plume.

His books are so popular we can’t keep them in at all, though I really can’t see what people find in them to rave over.”  

“I think they’re wonderful,” said Valancy, timidly.  

“Oh—well—” Miss Clarkson smiled in a patronising  [patronising - покровительственно] fashion that relegated Valancy’s opinions to limbo, “I can’t say I care much for bugs  [bugs - жуки] myself.

But certainly Foster seems to know all there is to know about them.”  

Valancy didn’t know whether she cared much for bugs  [bugs - жуки] either.

It was not John Foster’s uncanny knowledge of wild creatures and insect life that enthralled her.

Yes, she would get a new Foster book.

It was a month since she had _Thistle Harvest_, so surely Mother could not object. [object - объект] 

Valancy had read it four times—she knew whole passages off by heart.  

And—she almost thought she would go and see Dr. Trent about that queer pain around the heart.

It had come rather often lately, [lately - в последнее время]  and the palpitations were becoming annoying, not to speak of an occasional  [occasional - случайный] dizzy moment and a queer  [queer - странный] shortness  [shortness - краткость] of breath.

But could she go to see him without telling any one?

It was a most daring  [daring - дерзкий] thought.

None of the Stirlings ever consulted  [consulted - посоветовался] a doctor without holding a family council and getting Uncle James’ approval.

_

Then_, they went to Dr. Ambrose Marsh of Port Lawrence, who had married Second Cousin Adelaide Stirling.  

But Valancy disliked Dr. Ambrose Marsh.

And, besides, she could not get to Port Lawrence, fifteen miles away, without being taken there.

She did not want any one to know about her heart.

There would be such a fuss made and every member of the family would come down and talk it over and advise her and caution her and warn her and tell her horrible tales of great-aunts and cousins forty times removed who had been “just like that” and “dropped dead without a moment’s warning, my dear. [dear - дорогой] ”  

Aunt Isabel would remember that she had always said Doss looked like a girl who would have heart trouble—“so pinched and peaked always”; and Uncle Wellington would take it as a personal insult, [insult - оскорбление]  when “no Stirling ever had heart disease before”; and Georgiana would forebode in perfectly audible asides that “poor, dear little Doss isn’t long for this world, I’m afraid”; and Cousin Gladys would say, “Why, _my_ heart has been like that for _years_,” in a tone that implied  [implied - подразумеваемый] no one else had any business even to have a heart; and Olive—Olive would merely  [merely - просто] look beautiful and superior  [superior - превосходный] and disgustingly  [disgustingly - отвратительно] healthy, as if to say, “Why all this fuss over a faded  [faded - выцветший] superfluity like Doss when you have _me_?”  

Valancy felt that she couldn’t tell anybody unless she had to.

Dr. Trent was over seventy and there had been rumours  [rumours - слухи] that he meant to retire soon.

None of the Stirling clan  [clan - клан] had ever gone to him since he had told Cousin Gladys, ten years before, that her neuritis was all imaginary and that she enjoyed it.

You couldn’t patronise a doctor who insulted  [insulted - оскорбленный] your first-cousin—once-removed like that—not to mention that he was a Presbyterian when all the Stirlings went to the Anglican church.

But Valancy, between the devil  [devil - дьявол]  [devil - дьявол] of disloyalty to clan  [clan - клан] and the deep  [deep - глубокий] sea of fuss and clatter and advice, thought she would take a chance with the devil.     

CHAPTER II   When Cousin Stickles knocked at her door, Valancy knew it was half-past seven and she must get up.

As long as she could remember, Cousin Stickles had knocked at her door at half-past seven.

Cousin Stickles and Mrs. Frederick Stirling had been up since seven, but Valancy was allowed to lie abed half an hour longer because of a family tradition that she was delicate. [delicate - деликатный] 

Valancy got up, though she hated getting up more this morning than ever she had before.

What was there to get up for?

Another dreary day like all the days that had preceded  [preceded - предшествующий] it, full of meaningless  [meaningless - бессмысленный] little tasks, joyless and unimportant, that benefited  [benefited - выгодный] nobody.

But if she did not get up at once she would not be ready for breakfast at eight o’clock.

Hard and fast times for meals were the rule in Mrs. Stirling’s household.

Breakfast at eight, dinner at one, supper at six, year in and year out.

No excuses for being late were ever tolerated. [tolerated - терпимый] 

So up Valancy got, shivering.  

The room was bitterly  [bitterly - горько] cold with the raw, penetrating  [penetrating - проникающий] chill  [chill - холод] of a wet May morning.

The house would be cold all day.

It was one of Mrs. Frederick’s rules that no fires were necessary after the twenty-fourth of May.

On the twenty-first of October Mrs. Frederick began cooking over the kitchen range and lighted a fire in the sitting-room stove in the evenings.

It was whispered  [whispered - прошептал] about in the connection that the late Frederick Stirling had caught the cold which resulted in his death during Valancy’s first year of life because Mrs. Frederick would not have a fire on the twentieth of October.

She lighted it the next day—but that was a day too late for Frederick Stirling.  

Valancy took off and hung up in the closet her nightdress of coarse, [coarse - грубо]  unbleached cotton, with high neck and long, tight sleeves.

She put on undergarments of a similar nature, a dress of brown gingham, thick, black stockings and rubber-heeled boots.

Of late years she had fallen into the habit of doing her hair with the shade of the window by the looking-glass pulled down.

The lines on her face did not show so plainly  [plainly - прямо] then.

But this morning she jerked the shade to the very top and looked at herself in the leprous mirror with a passionate  [passionate - страстно] determination  [determination - решительно] to see herself as the world saw her.  

The result was rather dreadful. [dreadful - ужасно] 

Even a beauty would have found that harsh, [harsh - резко]  unsoftened side-light trying.

Valancy saw straight black hair, short and thin, always lustreless despite  [despite - несмотря на] the fact that she gave it one hundred strokes  [strokes - поглаживания] of the brush, neither more nor less, every night of her life and faithfully rubbed  [rubbed - потер] Redfern’s Hair Vigor into the roots, more lustreless than ever in its morning roughness; fine, straight, black brows; a nose she had always felt was much too small even for her small, three-cornered, white face; a small, pale mouth that always fell open a trifle over little, pointed white teeth; a figure thin and flat- [flat - плоско] breasted, rather below the average height.

She had somehow escaped the family high cheek- [cheek - щеку] bones, and her dark-brown eyes, too soft and shadowy to be black, had a slant that was almost Oriental. [Oriental - восточное] 

Apart from her eyes she was neither pretty nor ugly—just insignificant-looking, she concluded bitterly. [bitterly - горько] 

How plain the lines around her eyes and mouth were in that merciless light!

And never had her narrow, white face looked so narrow and so white.  

She did her hair in a pompadour.

Pompadours had long gone out of fashion, but they had been in when Valancy first put her hair up and Aunt Wellington had decided that she must always wear her hair so.  

“It is the _only_ way that becomes you.

But then, there were so many things Valancy never dared  [dared - осмелился] do.  

All her life she had been afraid of something, she thought bitterly. [bitterly - горько] 

From the very dawn  [dawn - рассвет] of recollection, [recollection - воспоминание]  when she had been so horribly afraid of the big black bear that lived, so Cousin Stickles told her, in the closet under the stairs.  

“And I always will be—I know it—I can’t help it.

I don’t know what it would be like not to be afraid of something.”  

Afraid of her mother’s sulky fits—afraid of offending  [offending - оскорбление]  [offending - оскорбление] Uncle Benjamin—afraid of becoming a target for Aunt Wellington’s contempt— [contempt - презрение] afraid of Aunt Isabel’s biting comments—afraid of Uncle James’ disapproval— [disapproval - неодобрение] afraid of offending the whole clan’ [clan - род] s opinions and prejudices— [prejudices - предубеждения] afraid of not keeping up appearances—afraid to say what she really thought of  anything—afraid of poverty in her old age.

Fear—fear—fear—she could never escape from it.

It bound  [bound - связанный] her and enmeshed her like a spider’s web of steel.

Only in her Blue Castle could she find temporary release.

And this morning Valancy could not believe she had a Blue Castle.

She would never be able to find it again.

Twenty-nine, unmarried, undesired—what had she to do with the fairy-like chatelaine of the Blue Castle?

She would cut such childish  [childish - ребячество] nonsense  [nonsense - бред] out of her life forever and face reality unflinchingly.  

She turned from her unfriendly mirror and looked out.

Valancy _had_ kept her schoolgirl complexion. [complexion - цвет лица] 

That was just the trouble.

There was not a gleam  [gleam - блеск] of beauty anywhere—“exactly like my life,” thought Valancy drearily.

Her brief bitterness had passed.

She accepted facts as resignedly as she had always accepted them.

She was one of the people whom life always passes by.

There was no altering  [altering - изменяющийся] that fact.  

In this mood Valancy went down to breakfast.     

CHAPTER III   Breakfast was always the same.

Oatmeal porridge, which Valancy loathed, toast and tea, and one teaspoonful  [teaspoonful - чайная ложка] of marmalade.

Mrs. Frederick thought two teaspoonfuls extravagant—but that did not matter to Valancy, who hated marmalade, too.

The chilly, [chilly - зябкий]  gloomy  [gloomy - мрачный] little dining-room was chillier and gloomier  [gloomier - мрачнее] than usual; the rain streamed down outside the window; departed  [departed - ушедший] Stirlings, in atrocious, gilt frames, wider than the pictures, glowered down from the walls.

And yet Cousin Stickles wished Valancy many happy returns of the day!  

“Sit up straight, Doss,” was all her mother said.  

Valancy sat up straight.

She talked to her mother and Cousin Stickles of the things they always talked of.

She never wondered what would happen if she tried to talk of something else.

She knew.

Therefore she never did it.  

Mrs. Frederick was offended  [offended - обиженный] with Providence for sending a rainy day when she wanted to go to a picnic, so she ate her breakfast in a sulky silence for which Valancy was rather grateful.

But Christine Stickles whined endlessly  [endlessly - бесконечно] on as usual, complaining about everything—the weather, the leak  [leak - протекающий] in the pantry, the price of oatmeal and butter—Valancy felt at once she had buttered her toast too lavishly—the epidemic  [epidemic - эпидемический] of mumps in Deerwood.  

“Doss will be sure to ketch them,” she foreboded.  

“Doss must not go where she is likely to catch mumps,” said Mrs. Frederick shortly. [shortly - коротко]   

Valancy had never had mumps—or whooping cough— [cough - кашель] or chicken-pox—or measles—or anything she should have had—nothing but horrible colds every winter.

Doss’ winter colds were a sort of tradition in the family.

Nothing, it seemed, could prevent her from catching them.

Mrs. Frederick and Cousin Stickles did their heroic best.

“The Stirlings seldom  [seldom - редко] take colds,” said Cousin Stickles resentfully.

_

She_ had been a Stirling.  

“I think,” said Mrs. Frederick, “that if a person makes up her mind _not_ to have colds she will not _have_ colds.”  

So that was the trouble.

It was all Valancy’s own fault.  

But on this particular  [particular - особый] morning Valancy’s unbearable  [unbearable - невыносимый] grievance was that she was called Doss.

She had endured [endure - терпящий]   [endured - переносимый] it for twenty-nine years, and all at once she felt she could not endure it any longer.

Her full name was Valancy Jane.

Valancy Jane was rather terrible, but she liked Valancy, with its odd, [odd - странный]  out-land tang.

It was always a wonder to Valancy that the Stirlings had allowed her to be so christened.

She had been told that her maternal  [maternal - материнский] grandfather, old Amos Wansbarra, had chosen the name for her.

Her father had tacked  [tacked - прикрепленный] on the Jane by way of civilising it, and the whole connection got out of the difficulty by nicknaming her Doss.

She never got Valancy from any one but outsiders. [outsiders - посторонние]   

“Mother,” she said timidly, “would you mind calling me Valancy after this?

Doss seems so—so—I don’t like it.”  

Mrs. Frederick looked at her daughter in astonishment. [astonishment - удивление] 

She wore glasses with enormously  [enormously - чрезвычайно] strong lenses that gave her eyes a peculiarly disagreeable  [disagreeable - неприятный] appearance.  

“What is the matter with Doss?”  “It—seems so childish, [childish - ребяческий] ” faltered  [faltered - нерешительный] Valancy.  

“Oh!”

Mrs. Frederick had been a Wansbarra and the Wansbarra smile was not an asset. [asset - актив] 

“I see.

Well, it should suit _you_

then.

“I am twenty-nine,” said the dear child desperately. [desperately - отчаянно]   

“I wouldn’t proclaim  [proclaim - провозглашать] it from the house-tops if I were you, dear,” said Mrs. Frederick.

“Twenty-nine!

_

I_ had been married nine years when I was twenty-nine.”  

“_I_ was married at seventeen,” said Cousin Stickles proudly. [proudly - гордо]   

Valancy looked at them furtively.

Mrs. Frederick, except for those terrible glasses and the hooked nose that made her look more like a parrot than a parrot itself could look, was not ill-looking.

At twenty she might have been quite pretty.

But Cousin Stickles!

And yet Christine Stickles had once been desirable  [desirable - желанное] in some man’s eyes.

Valancy felt that Cousin Stickles, with her broad, flat, wrinkled  [wrinkled - морщинистое]  [wrinkled - морщинистое] face, a mole right on the end of her dumpy nose, bristling hairs on her chin, wrinkled yellow neck, pale, protruding eyes, and thin, puckered mouth, had yet this advantage over her—this right to look down on her.

And even yet Cousin Stickles was necessary to Mrs. Frederick.

Valancy wondered pitifully what it would be like to be wanted by some one—needed by some one.

No one in the whole world needed her, or would miss anything from life if she dropped suddenly out of it.

She was a disappointment  [disappointment - разочарование] to her mother.

No one loved her.

She had never so much as had a girl friend.  

“I haven’t even a gift for friendship,” she had once admitted to herself pitifully.  

“Doss, you haven’t eaten your crusts,” said Mrs. Frederick rebukingly.  

It rained all the forenoon without cessation. [cessation - прекращение] 

Valancy pieced a quilt.

Valancy hated piecing quilts.

And there was no need  [need - нужда] of it.

The house was full of quilts.

There were three big chests, packed with quilts, in the attic. [attic - чердак] 

Mrs. Frederick had begun storing  [storing - хранение]  [storing - хранение] away  [away - прочь] quilts when Valancy was seventeen and she kept on storing them, though it did not seem likely that Valancy would ever need them.

But Valancy must be at work and fancy work materials were too expensive.

Idleness was a cardinal sin  [sin - грех] in the Stirling household.

When Valancy had been a child she had been made to write down every night, in a small, hated, black notebook, [notebook - тетрадь]  all the minutes she had spent in idleness that day.

On Sundays her mother made her tot them up and pray over them.  

At least, Mrs. Frederick and Cousin Stickles would have called it idleness.

She went to her room to get a better thimble and she opened _Thistle Harvest_ guiltily  [guiltily - виновато] at random. [random - случайно]   

“The woods are so human,” wrote John Foster, “that to know them one must live with them.

An occasional  [occasional - случайно] saunter through them, keeping to the well-trodden paths, will never admit us to their intimacy.

If we wish to be friends we must seek them out and win them by frequent, [frequent - часто]  reverent visits at all hours; by morning, by noon, [noon - полдень]  and by night; and at all seasons, in spring, in summer, in autumn, in winter.

Otherwise we can never really know them and any pretence we may make to the contrary will never impose  [impose - навязывать] on them.

They have their own effective way of keeping aliens  [aliens - инопланетяне] at a distance and shutting their hearts to mere  [mere - просто] casual  [casual - случайно] sightseers.

It is of no use to seek the woods from any motive  [motive - мотив] except sheer  [sheer - откровенно] love of them; they will find us out at once and hide all their sweet, old-world secrets from us.

But if they know we come to them because we love them they will be very kind to us and give us such treasures  [treasures - сокровища] of beauty and delight as are not bought or sold in any market-place.

For the woods, when they give at all, give unstintedly and hold nothing back from their true worshippers.

Then the immortal  [immortal - бессмертный] heart of the woods will beat against ours and its subtle  [subtle - тонкий] life will steal into our veins  [veins - вены] and make us its own forever, so that no matter where we go or how widely we wander  [wander - блуждать] we shall yet be drawn back to the forest to find our most enduring  [enduring - непреходящий] kinship.”  

“Doss,” called her mother from the hall below, “what are you doing all by yourself in that room?”  Valancy dropped _Thistle Harvest_ like a hot coal and fled  [fled - сбежал] downstairs to her patches; [patches - пятна]  but she felt the strange exhilaration  [exhilaration - волнение] of spirit that always came momentarily  [momentarily - на мгновение] to her when she dipped  [dipped - окунулся] into one of John Foster’s books.

Valancy did not know much about woods—except the haunted groves of oak and pine  [pine - сосна] around her Blue Castle.

But she had always secretly hankered after them and a Foster book about woods was the next best thing to the woods themselves.  

At noon  [noon - полдень] it stopped raining, but the sun did not come out until three.

Then Valancy timidly said she thought she would go uptown.  

“What do you want to go uptown for?” demanded her mother.  

“I want to get a book from the library.”  

“You got a book from the library only last week.”  

“No, it was four weeks.”  

“Four weeks.

Nonsense! [Nonsense - ерунда] ”  “Really it was, Mother.”  

“You are mistaken.

It cannot possibly have been more than two weeks.

I dislike contradiction. [contradiction - противоречие] 

And I do not see what you want to get a book for, anyhow.

You waste too much time reading.”  

“Of what value is my time?” asked Valancy bitterly. [bitterly - горько]   

“Doss!

Don’t speak in that tone to _me_.”  

“We need some tea,” said Cousin Stickles.

“She might go and get that if she wants a walk—though this damp weather is bad for colds.”  

They argued the matter for ten minutes longer and finally Mrs. Frederick agreed rather grudgingly that Valancy might go.     

CHAPTER IV   “Got your rubbers on?” called Cousin Stickles, as Valancy left the house.  

Christine Stickles had never once forgotten to ask that question when Valancy went out on a damp day.  

“Yes.”  “Have you got your flannel petticoat on?” asked Mrs. Frederick.  

“No.”  “Doss, I really do not understand you.

Do you want to catch your death of cold _again_?”

But Olive’s father had “married money” and Olive never had bronchitis.

So there you were.  

“Are you sure you didn’t leave the soap in the water?” demanded Mrs. Frederick.

But Valancy was gone.

She turned at the corner and looked back down the ugly, prim, respectable street where she lived.

The Stirling house was the ugliest on it—more like a red brick  [brick - кирпичный] box than anything else.

Too high for its breadth, and made still higher by a bulbous glass cupola on top.

About it was the desolate, [desolate - пустынный]  barren  [barren - бесплодный] peace of an old house whose life is lived.  

There was a very pretty little house, with leaded casements and dubbed  [dubbed - дублированный] gables, just around the corner—a new house, one of those houses you love the minute you see them.

Clayton Markley had built it for his bride.

He was to be married to Jennie Lloyd in June.

The little house, it was said, was furnished from attic  [attic - чердак] to cellar, [cellar - погреб]  in complete readiness for its mistress.  

“I don’t envy  [envy - зависть] Jennie the man,” thought Valancy sincerely—Clayton Markley was not one of her many ideals—“but I _

do_

envy  [envy - зависть] her the house.

It’s such a nice young house.

Oh, if I could only have a house of my own—ever so poor, so tiny—but my own!

But then,” she added bitterly, [bitterly - горько]  “there is no use in yowling for the moon when you can’t even get a tallow candle. [candle - свеча] ”  

In dreamland nothing would do Valancy but a castle of pale sapphire.

In real life she would have been fully satisfied with a little house of her own.

She envied  [envied - завидовал] Jennie Lloyd more fiercely  [fiercely - люто] than ever today.

Jennie was not so much better looking than she was, and not so very much younger.

Yet she was to have this delightful house.

And the nicest little Wedgwood teacups—Valancy had seen them; an open fireplace, [fireplace - камин]  and monogrammed linen; [linen - белье]  hemstitched tablecloths, [tablecloths - скатерти]  and china-closets.

Why did _everything_ come to some girls and _nothing_ to others?

It wasn’t fair.  

Motors were still rather a novelty  [novelty - новинка] in Deerwood, though they were common in Port Lawrence, and most of the summer residents  [residents - жители] up at Muskoka had them.

In Deerwood only some of the smart set had them; for even Deerwood was divided into sets.

There was the smart set—the intellectual  [intellectual - интеллектуальный] set—the old-family set—of which the Stirlings were members—the common run, and a few pariahs.

Not one of the Stirling clan  [clan - клан] had as yet condescended to a motor, though Olive was teasing  [teasing - дразнящий] her father to have one.

Valancy had never even been in a motorcar.

But she did not hanker after this.

In truth, she felt rather afraid of motorcars, especially at night.

They seemed to be too much like big purring beasts  [beasts - звери] that might turn and crush  [crush - давка] you—or make some terrible savage  [savage - дикарь] leap  [leap - прыжок] somewhere.

On the steep mountain trails around her Blue Castle only gaily caparisoned steeds might proudly  [proudly - гордо] pace; [pace - шаг]  in real life Valancy would have been quite contented  [contented - довольный] to drive in a buggy  [buggy - багги] behind a nice horse.

She got a buggy  [buggy - багги] drive only when some uncle or cousin remembered to fling her “a chance,” like a bone to a dog.     

CHAPTER V   Of course she must buy the tea in Uncle Benjamin’s grocery- [grocery - продуктовый] store.

To buy it anywhere else was unthinkable.

Yet Valancy hated to go to Uncle Benjamin’s store on her twenty-ninth birthday.

There was no hope that he would not remember it.  

“Why,” demanded Uncle Benjamin, leeringly, as he tied up her tea, “are young ladies like bad grammarians?”  Valancy, with Uncle Benjamin’s will in the background of her mind, said meekly, “I don’t know.

On the first day Claude Bertram had seen her in the store she had heard him whisper  [whisper - шепот] to Joe, “Who is that?”

And Joe had said, “Valancy Stirling—one of the Deerwood old maids.”

“Curable or incurable? [incurable - неизлечимый] ”

Claude had asked with a snicker, evidently  [evidently - очевидно] thinking the question very clever.

Valancy smarted anew  [anew - снова] with the sting of that old recollection. [recollection - воспоминание]   

“Twenty-nine,” Uncle Benjamin was saying.

“Dear me, Doss, you’re dangerously near the second corner and not even thinking of getting married yet.

Twenty-nine.

It seems impossible.”  

Then Uncle Benjamin said an original thing.

Uncle Benjamin said, “How time does fly!”  

“_I_ think it _crawls_ [crawls - ползет] ,” said Valancy passionately. [passionately - страстно] 

Passion was so alien  [alien - чужое] to Uncle Benjamin’s conception  [conception - зачатие] of Valancy that he didn’t know what to make of her.

To cover his confusion, [confusion - путаница]  he asked another conundrum as he tied up her beans—Cousin Stickles had remembered at the last moment that they must have beans.

Beans were cheap and filling.  

“What two ages are apt  [apt - меткое] to prove illusory?” asked Uncle Benjamin; and, not waiting for Valancy to “give it up,” he added, “Mir-age and marriage.”  

“M-i-r-a-g-e  [e - е] is pronounced _mirazh_,” said Valancy shortly, [shortly - коротко]  picking up her tea and her beans.

For the moment she did not care whether Uncle Benjamin cut her out of his will or not.

She walked out of the store while Uncle Benjamin stared after her with his mouth open.

Then he shook his head.  

“Poor Doss is taking it hard,” he said.  

Valancy was sorry by the time she reached the next crossing.

Why had she lost her patience  [patience - терпение] like that?

Uncle Benjamin would be annoyed and would likely tell her mother that Doss had been impertinent—“to _me_!”—and her mother would lecture her for a week.  

“I’ve held my tongue for twenty years,” thought Valancy.

“Why couldn’t I have held it once more?”  

Yes, it was just twenty, Valancy reflected, since she had first been twitted with her loverless condition.

She remembered the bitter moment perfectly.

She was just nine years old and she was standing alone on the school playground while the other little girls of her class were playing a game in which you must be chosen by a boy as his partner before you could play.

Nobody had chosen Valancy—little, pale, black-haired Valancy, with her prim, long-sleeved apron and odd, [odd - странное]  slanted eyes.  

“Oh,” said a pretty little girl to her, “I’m so sorry for you.

You haven’t got a beau.”  

Valancy had said defiantly, as she continued to say for twenty years, “I don’t _want_ a beau.”

But this afternoon Valancy once and for all stopped saying that.  

“I’m going to be honest with myself anyhow,” she thought savagely.

She felt sure that Rev. Dr. Stalling, who passed her at this moment, read her thoughts and disapproved  [disapproved - неодобрено] of them thoroughly. [thoroughly - тщательно] 

Valancy was afraid of Dr. Stalling—had been afraid of him ever since the Sunday, twenty-three years before, when he had first come to St. Albans’.

Valancy had been too late for Sunday School that day and she had gone into the church timidly and sat in their pew.

No one else was in the church—nobody except the new rector, Dr. Stalling.

Dr. Stalling stood up in front of the choir  [choir - хор] door, beckoned to her, and said sternly, “Little boy, come up here.”  

Valancy had stared around her.

There was no little boy—there was no one in all the huge church but herself.

This strange man with the blue glasses couldn’t mean her.

She was not a boy.  

“Little boy,” repeated Dr. Stalling, more sternly still, shaking his forefinger fiercely  [fiercely - жестоко] at her, “come up here at once!”  

Valancy arose as if hypnotised and walked up the aisle.

She was too terrified to do anything else.

What dreadful  [dreadful - ужасно] thing was going to happen to her?

What _had_ happened to her?

Had she actually turned into a boy?

She came to a stop in front of Dr. Stalling.

Dr. Stalling shook his forefinger—such a long, knuckly forefinger—at her and said:  “Little boy, take off your hat.”  

Valancy took off her hat.

She had a scrawny little pigtail hanging down her back, but Dr. Stalling was short-sighted and did not perceive  [perceive - воспринимать] it.  

“Little boy, go back to your seat and _always_ take off your hat in church.

_Remember_!”  

Valancy went back to her seat carrying her hat like an automaton.

Presently  [Presently - в настоящее время] her mother came in.  

“Doss,” said Mrs. Stirling, “what do you mean by taking off your hat?

Put it on instantly! [instantly - мгновенно] ”  

Valancy put it on instantly. [instantly - мгновенно] 

She was cold with fear lest Dr. Stalling should immediately summon  [summon - вызывать] her up front again.

She would have to go, of course—it never occurred to her that one could disobey the rector—and the church was full of people now.

Oh, what would she do if that horrible, stabbing  [stabbing - колоть] forefinger were shaken at her again before all those people?

Valancy sat through the whole service in an agony  [agony - агонию] of dread and was sick for a week afterwards.

Nobody knew why—Mrs. Frederick again bemoaned herself of her delicate  [delicate - деликатно] child.  

Dr. Stalling found out his mistake and laughed over it to Valancy—who did not laugh.

She never got over her dread of Dr. Stalling.

And now to be caught by him on the street corner, thinking such things!  

Valancy got her John Foster book—_Magic of Wings_.

“His latest—all about birds,” said Miss Clarkson.

She had almost decided that she would go home, instead of going to see Dr. Trent.

Her courage had failed her.

She was afraid of offending  [offending - обидно] Uncle James—afraid of angering her mother—afraid of facing gruff, shaggy-browed old Dr. Trent, who would probably tell her, as he had told Cousin Gladys, that her trouble was entirely imaginary and that she only had it because she liked to have it.

No, she would not go; she would get a bottle of Redfern’s Purple Pills instead.

Valancy always felt very sceptical  [sceptical - скептический] concerning  [concerning - относительно] the virtues  [virtues - добродетелей] of the Purple Pills; but there _might_ be something in them; and it was easier to take them than to face Dr. Trent alone.

She would glance over the magazines in the reading-room a few minutes and then go home.  

Valancy tried to read a story, but it made her furious. [furious - яростный] 

On every page was a picture of the heroine  [heroine - героиня] surrounded by adoring  [adoring - обожающий] men.

And here was she, Valancy Stirling, who could not get a solitary beau!

Valancy slammed  [slammed - хлопнул] the magazine shut; [shut - затворил]  she opened _Magic of Wings_.

Her eyes fell on the paragraph that changed her life.  

“_Fear is the original sin_ [sin - грех] ,” wrote John Foster.

“_Almost all the evil  [evil - злой] in the world has its origin in the fact that some one is afraid of something_.

It is a cold, slimy serpent coiling about you.

It is horrible to live with fear; and it is of all things degrading. [degrading - унижающий] ”  

Valancy shut _Magic of Wings_ and stood up.

She would go and see Dr. Trent.     

CHAPTER VI   

The ordeal was not so dreadful, [dreadful - ужасный]  after all.

Dr. Trent was as gruff and abrupt as usual, but he did not tell her ailment was imaginary.

After he had listened to her symptoms and asked a few questions and made a quick examination, he sat for a moment looking at her quite intently.

Valancy thought he looked as if he were sorry for her.

She caught her breath for a moment.

Was the trouble serious?

Oh, it couldn’t be, surely—it really hadn’t bothered her _much_—only lately  [lately - в последнее время] it had got a little worse.  

Dr. Trent opened his mouth—but before he could speak the telephone at his elbow  [elbow - локоть] rang sharply. [sharply - резко] 

She heard him rushing madly  [madly - безумно] about overhead, barking  [barking - лай] out a few remarks  [remarks - замечания] to somebody—presumably  [presumably - предположительно] his housekeeper. [housekeeper - экономка] 

Then he came tearing downstairs with a club bag in his hand, snatched his hat and coat from the rack, [rack - стеллаж]  jerked open the street door and rushed down the street in the direction of the station.  

Valancy sat alone in the little office, feeling more absolutely foolish than she had ever felt before in her life.

Foolish—and humiliated. [humiliated - униженный] 

So this was all that had come of her heroic determination  [determination - решимость] to live up to John Foster and cast  [cast - отброшенный] fear aside. [aside - в сторону] 

Not only was she a failure as a relative and non-existent as a sweetheart or friend, but she was not even of any importance as a patient.

Dr. Trent had forgotten her very presence in his excitement over whatever message had come by the telephone.

She had gained nothing by ignoring Uncle James and flying in the face of family tradition.  

For a moment she was afraid she was going to cry.

It _was_ all so—ridiculous. [ridiculous - нелепый] 

Then she heard Dr. Trent’s housekeeper  [housekeeper - экономка] coming down the stairs.

Valancy rose and went to the office door.  

“The doctor forgot all about me,” she said with a twisted  [twisted - искривленный] smile.  

“Well, that’s too bad,” said Mrs. Patterson sympathetically.

“But it wasn’t much wonder, poor man.

That was a telegram they ’phoned over from the Port.

His son has been terribly  [terribly - ужасно] injured in an auto  [auto - авто] accident in Montreal.

The doctor had just ten minutes to catch the train.

Valancy went home by the short-cut of Lover’s Lane.

She did not often go through Lover’s Lane—but it was getting near supper-time and it would never do to be late.

Lover’s Lane wound back of the village, under great elms and maples, and deserved its name.

It was hard to go there at any time and not find some canoodling couple—or young girls in pairs, arms intertwined, earnestly talking over their little secrets.

Valancy didn’t know which made her feel more self-conscious and uncomfortable.  

This evening she encountered  [encountered - столкнулся] both.

She met Connie Hale and Kate Bayley, in new pink organdy dresses with flowers stuck coquettishly in their glossy, bare  [bare - голый] hair.

Valancy had never had a pink dress or worn flowers in her hair.

Then she passed a young couple she didn’t know, dandering along, oblivious  [oblivious - забывчивый] to everything but themselves.

The young man’s arm was around the girl’s waist  [waist - талия] quite shamelessly.

Valancy had never walked with a man’s arm about her.

She felt that she ought to be shocked— [shocked - шокированный] they might leave that sort of thing for the screening  [screening - экранирование] twilight, at least—but she wasn’t shocked.

In another flash  [flash - вспышка] of desperate, stark  [stark - абсолютная] honesty  [honesty - честность] she owned to herself that she was merely  [merely - просто] envious. [envious - завидующий] 

When she passed them she felt quite sure they were laughing at her—pitying her—“there’s that queer  [queer - педик] little old maid, Valancy Stirling.

They say she never had a beau in her whole life”—Valancy fairly ran to get out of Lover’s Lane.

Never had she felt so utterly  [utterly - совершенно] colourless  [colourless - бесцветный] and skinny and insignificant.  

Just where Lover’s Lane debouched on the street, an old car was parked.

Valancy knew that car well—by sound, at least—and everybody in Deerwood knew it.

This was before the phrase “tin Lizzie” had come into circulation— [circulation - тираж] in Deerwood, at least; but if it had been known, this car was the tinniest of Lizzies—though it was not a Ford but an old Grey Slosson.

Valancy gave him a swift, [swift - стремительный]  furtive look as she hurried by.

This was only the second time she had ever seen the notorious  [notorious - пресловутый] Barney Snaith, though she had heard enough about him in the five years that he had been living “up back” in Muskoka.

The first time had been nearly a year ago, on the Muskoka road.

He had been crawling  [crawling - ползающий] out from under his car then, too, and he had given her a cheerful grin  [grin - ухмылка]  [grin - ухмылка] as she went by—a little, whimsical grin that gave him the look of an amused gnome.

He didn’t look bad—she didn’t believe he was bad, in spite  [spite - злоба] of the wild yarns that were always being told of him.

Of course he went tearing in that terrible old Grey Slosson through Deerwood at hours when all decent  [decent - порядочный] people were in bed—often with old “Roaring  [Roaring - ревущий] Abel,” who made the night hideous with his howls—“both of them dead drunk, my dear. [dear - дорогой] ”

And every one knew that he was an escaped convict  [convict - зек] and a defaulting bank clerk  [clerk - клерк] and a murderer in hiding and an infidel and an illegitimate son of old Roaring  [Roaring - ревущий] Abel Gay and the father of Roaring Abel’s illegitimate grandchild and a counterfeiter and a forger  [forger - фальсификатор] and a few other awful things.

But still Valancy didn’t believe he was bad.

Nobody with a smile like that could be bad, no matter what he had done.  

But he still retained something a little grim  [grim - мрачный] about the jaw. [jaw - челюсть]   

Barney Snaith looked even more disreputable than usual just now.

It was very evident  [evident - очевидный] that he hadn’t shaved for days, and his hands and arms, bare  [bare - голый] to the shoulders, were black with grease.

But he was whistling  [whistling - свистящий] gleefully to himself and he seemed so happy that Valancy envied  [envied - завидный] him.

She envied  [envied - завидный] him his light-heartedness and his irresponsibility and his mysterious little cabin  [cabin - кабина] up on an island in Lake Mistawis—even his rackety old Grey Slosson.

Neither he nor his car had to be respectable and live up to traditions.

When he rattled past her a few minutes later, bareheaded, leaning  [leaning - прислоненный] back in his Lizzie at a raffish angle, his longish hair blowing in the wind, a villainous-looking old black pipe in his mouth, she envied  [envied - завидный] him again.

Men had the best of it, no doubt about that.

This outlaw happy, whatever he was or wasn’t.

She, Valancy Stirling, respectable, well-behaved to the last degree, was unhappy and had always been unhappy.

So there you were.  

Valancy was just in time for supper.

The sun had clouded over, and a dismal, [dismal - мрачный]  drizzling rain was falling again.

Cousin Stickles had the neuralgia.

Valancy had to do the family darning and there was no time for _Magic of Wings_.  

“Can’t the darning wait till tomorrow?”

she pleaded. [pleaded - умоляющий]   

“Tomorrow will bring its own duties,” said Mrs. Frederick inexorably.  

Valancy darned all the evening and listened to Mrs. Frederick and Cousin Stickles talking the eternal, [eternal - вечный]  niggling gossip of the clan, [clan - клан]  as they knitted drearily at interminable  [interminable - бесконечный] black stockings.

On the whole, they approved.

Second Cousin Lilian was doing well for herself.  

“Though she hasn’t hurried,” said Cousin Stickles.

“She must be twenty-five.”  

“There have not—fortunately—been many old maids in our connection,” said Mrs. Frederick bitterly. [bitterly - горько]   

Valancy flinched.

She had run the darning needle into her finger.  

Third Cousin Aaron Gray had been scratched  [scratched - царапал] by a cat and had blood-poisoning  [poisoning - отравление] in his finger.

“Cats are most dangerous animals,” said Mrs. Frederick.

“I would never have a cat about the house.”  

She glared significantly  [significantly - значительно] at Valancy through her terrible glasses.

Once, five years ago, Valancy had asked if she might have a cat.

She had never referred to it since, but Mrs. Frederick still suspected  [suspected - подозревал] her of harbouring the unlawful  [unlawful - неправомерное] desire in her heart of hearts.  

Once Valancy sneezed. [sneezed - чихнул] 

Now, in the Stirling code, it was very bad form to sneeze  [sneeze - чихнуть] in public.  

“You can always repress  [repress - подавить] a sneeze by pressing your finger on your upper lip,” said Mrs. Frederick rebukingly.  

Half-past nine o’clock and so, as Mr. Pepys would say, to bed.

But First Cousin Stickles’ neuralgic back must be rubbed  [rubbed - натер] with Redfern’s Liniment.

Valancy did that.

Valancy always had to do it.

She hated the smell of Redfern’s Liniment—she hated the smug, [smug - самодовольно]  beaming, portly, be-whiskered, be-spectacled picture of Dr. Redfern on the bottle.

Her fingers smelled of the horrible stuff after she got into bed, in spite  [spite - назло] of all the scrubbing she gave them.  

Valancy’s day of destiny  [destiny - судьба] had come and gone.

She ended it as she had begun it, in tears.     

CHAPTER VII   

There was a rosebush on the little Stirling lawn, [lawn - лужайка]  growing beside the gate.

It was called “Doss’s rosebush.”

Cousin Georgiana had given it to Valancy five years ago and Valancy had planted it joyfully. [joyfully - радостно] 

That was her luck.

Valancy did everything she could think of and took the advice of everybody in the clan, [clan - клан]  but still the rosebush would not bloom. [bloom - цветение] 

It throve and grew luxuriantly, with great leafy branches untouched  [untouched - нетронутый] of rust or spider; but not even a bud  [bud - бутон] had ever appeared on it.

Valancy, looking at it two days after her birthday, was filled with a sudden, overwhelming  [overwhelming - подавляющий] hatred  [hatred - ненависть] for it.

The thing wouldn’t bloom: [bloom - цветение]  very well, then, she would cut it down.

She marched to the tool-room in the barn  [barn - амбар] for her garden knife and she went at the rosebush viciously. [viciously - злобно] 

A few minutes later horrified Mrs. Frederick came out to the verandah  [verandah - веранда] and beheld her daughter slashing  [slashing - рубящий] insanely among the rosebush boughs.

Half of them were already strewn on the walk.

The bush looked sadly dismantled.  

“Doss, what on earth are you doing?

Have you gone crazy?”  

“No,” said Valancy.

She meant to say it defiantly, but habit was too strong for her.

She said it deprecatingly.

“I—I just made up my mind to cut this bush down.

It is no good.

It never blooms [bloom - цветение] — [blooms - цветы] never will bloom.”  

“That is no reason for destroying it,” said Mrs. Frederick sternly.

“It was a beautiful bush and quite ornamental. [ornamental - декоративный] 

You have made a sorry-looking thing of it.”  

“Rose trees should _bloom_ [bloom - цветок] ,” said Valancy a little obstinately.  

“Don’t argue with _me_, Doss.

Clear up that mess and leave the bush alone.

“The bush is mine,” muttered  [muttered - пробормотал] Valancy.  

“What’s that?

What did you say, Doss?”  

“I only said the bush was mine,” repeated Valancy humbly.  

Mrs. Frederick turned without a word and marched back into the house.

The mischief was done now.

Valancy knew she had offended  [offended - обиженный] her mother deeply and would not be spoken to or noticed in any way for two or three days.

Cousin Stickles would see to Valancy’s bringing-up but Mrs. Frederick would preserve the stony silence of outraged majesty.  

Valancy sighed  [sighed - вздохнул] and put away her garden knife, hanging it precisel [precise - точный] y  [precisely - точно] on its precise nail in the tool-shop.

She cleared away  [away - прочь] the severed branches and swept  [swept - метал] up the leaves.

Her lips twitched  [twitched - дернул] as she looked at the straggling bush.

It had an odd  [odd - странно] resemblance to its shaken, scrawny donor, [donor - донор]  little Cousin Georgiana herself.  

“I certainly have made an awful-looking thing of it,” thought Valancy.  

But she did not feel repentant—only sorry she had offended  [offended - обиделся] her mother.

Things would be so uncomfortable until she was forgiven.

Mrs. Frederick was one of those women who can make their anger felt all over a house.

Walls and doors are no protection from it.  

“You’d better go uptown and git the mail,” said Cousin Stickles, when Valancy went in.

“_I_ can’t go—I feel all sorter peaky and piny this spring.

I want you to stop at the drugstore and git me a bottle of Redfern’s Blood Bitters.

There’s nothing like Redfern’s Bitters for building a body up.

Cousin James says the Purple Pills are the best, but I know better.

My poor dear husband took Redfern’s Bitters right up to the day he died.

Don’t let them charge you more’n ninety cents.

I kin git it for that at the Port.

And what _have_

you been saying to your poor mother?

Do you ever stop to think, Doss, that you kin only have one mother?”  

“One is enough for me,” thought Valancy undutifully, as she went uptown.  

She got Cousin Stickles’ bottle of bitters and then she went to the post-office and asked for her mail at the General Delivery.

Her mother did not have a box.

They got too little mail to bother with it.

Valancy did not expect any mail, except the _Christian Times_, which was the only paper they took.

They hardly ever got any letters.

But Valancy rather liked to stand in the office and watch Mr. Carewe, the grey-bearded, [bearded - бородатый]  Santa-Clausy old clerk, [clerk - клерк]  handing out letters to the lucky people who did get them.

He did it with such a detached, impersonal, [impersonal - безличный]  Jove-like air, as if it did not matter in the least to him what supernal joys or shattering  [shattering - сокрушительный] horrors might be in those letters for the people to whom they were addressed.

In her Blue Castle exciting epistles, bound  [bound - связанный] with silk and sealed with crimson, were always being brought to her by pages in livery of gold and blue, but in real life her only letters were occasional  [occasional - случайный] perfunctory notes from relatives or an advertising circular.  

Consequently  [Consequently - Поэтому] she was immensely  [immensely - безмерно] surprised when Mr. Carewe, looking even more Jovian than usual, poked a letter out to her.

Yes, it was addressed to her plainly, [plainly - явно]  in a fierce, [fierce - жестокий]  black hand: “Miss Valancy Stirling, Elm Street, Deerwood”—and the postmark was Montreal.

Valancy picked it up with a little quickening of her breath.

Montreal!

It must be from Doctor Trent.

He had remembered her, after all.  

Valancy met Uncle Benjamin coming in as she was going out and was glad the letter was safely in her bag.  

“What,” said Uncle Benjamin, “is the difference between a donkey  [donkey - осел] and a postage-stamp?”  

“I don’t know.

What?” answered Valancy dutifully. [dutifully - послушно]   

“One you lick  [lick - лизать] with a stick  [stick - палку] and the other you stick with a lick.

Ha, ha!”  

Uncle Benjamin passed in, tremendously  [tremendously - чрезвычайно] pleased with himself.  

Cousin Stickles pounced  [pounced - набросился] on the _Times_ when Valancy got home, but it did not occur to her to ask if there were any letters.

Mrs. Frederick would have asked it, but Mrs. Frederick’s lips at present were sealed.

Valancy was glad of this.

If her mother had asked if there were any letters Valancy would have had to admit there was.

Then she would have had to let her mother and Cousin Stickles read the letter and all would be discovered.  

Her heart acted strangely on the way upstairs, and she sat down by her window for a few minutes before opening her letter.

She felt very guilty and deceitful.

She had never before kept a letter secret from her mother.

Every letter she had ever written or received had been read by Mrs. Frederick.

That had never mattered.

Valancy had never had anything to hide.

But this _did_

matter.

She could not have any one see this letter.

But her fingers trembled with a consciousness  [consciousness - сознание] of wickedness and unfilial conduct as she opened it—trembled a little, too, perhaps, with apprehension. [apprehension - предчувствие] 

She felt quite sure there was nothing seriously wrong with her heart but—one never knew.  

Dr. Trent’s letter was like himself—blunt, abrupt, concise, wasting no words.

Dr. Trent never beat about the bush.

“Dear Miss Sterling”—and then a page of black, positive writing.

Valancy seemed to read it at a glance; she dropped it on her lap, [lap - на коленях]  her face ghost-white.  

He said, without mincing matters, that nothing could be done for her.

If she took great care of herself she might live a year—but she might also die at any moment—Dr. Trent never troubled himself about euphemisms.

She must be careful to avoid all excitement and all severe muscular  [muscular - мускулистый] efforts.

She must eat and drink moderately, she must never run, she must go upstairs and uphill with great care.

Any sudden jolt or shock might be fatal. [fatal - смертельный] 

She was to get the prescription  [prescription - рецепт] he enclosed filled and carry it with her always, taking a dose  [dose - доза] whenever her attacks came on.

And he was hers truly, H. B. Trent.  

Valancy sat for a long while by her window.

Outside was a world drowned  [drowned - утонул] in the light of a spring afternoon—skies entrancingly blue, winds perfumed and free, lovely, soft, blue hazes at the end of every street.

Over at the railway station a group of young girls was waiting for a train; she heard their gay laughter as they chattered and joked.

The train roared  [roared - заревел]  [roared - заревел] in and roared out again.

But none of these things had any reality.

Nothing had any reality except the fact that she had only another year to live.  

When she was tired of sitting at the window she went over and lay down on her bed, staring at the cracked, [cracked - треснул]  discoloured ceiling. [ceiling - потолок] 

The curious  [curious - любопытный] numbness that follows on a staggering  [staggering - ошеломляющий] blow  [blow - удар] possessed her.

She did not feel anything except a boundless surprise and incredulity—behind which was the conviction  [conviction - осуждение] that Dr. Trent knew his business and that she, Valancy Stirling, who had never lived, was about to die.  

When the gong  [gong - гонг] rang for supper Valancy got up and went downstairs mechanically, [mechanically - механически]  from force of habit.

She wondered that she had been let alone so long.

But of course her mother would not pay any attention to her just now.

Valancy was thankful for this.

Valancy forced  [forced - вынужденный] herself to swallow  [swallow - глотать] a cup of tea and then sat and watched the others eat, with an odd  [odd - странный] feeling that years had passed since she had sat with them at the dinner-table.

She found herself smiling inwardly to think what a commotion  [commotion - переполох] she could make if she chose.

Let her merely  [merely - просто] tell them what was in Dr. Trent’s letter and there would be as much fuss made as if—Valancy thought bitterly— [bitterly - горько] they really cared two straws about her.  

“Dr. Trent’s housekeeper  [housekeeper - домработница] got word from him today,” said Cousin Stickles, so suddenly that Valancy jumped guiltily. [guiltily - виновно] 

Was there anything in thought waves?

“Mrs. Judd was talking to her uptown.

They think his son will recover, but Dr. Trent wrote that if he did he was going to take him abroad as soon as he was able to travel and wouldn’t be back here for a year at least.”  

“That will not matter much to _us_,” said Mrs. Frederick majestically.

“He is not _our_ doctor.

I would not”—here

she looked or seemed to look accusingly right through Valancy—“have _him_ to doctor  [doctor - доктор] a sick cat.”  

“May I go upstairs and lie down?” said Valancy faintly. [faintly - слабо] 

“I—I have a headache.”  

“What has given you a headache?” asked Cousin Stickles, since Mrs. Frederick would not.

The question had to be asked.

Valancy could not be allowed to have headaches without interference. [interference - вмешательство]   

“You ain’t in the habit of having headaches.

I hope you’re not taking the mumps.

Here, try a spoonful of vinegar. [vinegar - уксус] ”  

“Piffle!” said Valancy rudely, getting up from the table.

She did not care just then if she were rude.

She had had to be so polite all her life.  

If it had been possible for Cousin Stickles to turn pale she would have.

As it was not, she turned yellower.  

“Are you sure you ain’t feverish, [feverish - лихорадочный]  Doss?

You sound like it.

she said.  

Cousin Stickles stared and gasped.

“What—what do you mean?”  “I said I wouldn’t be rubbed  [rubbed - натер] with Redfern’s Liniment,” repeated Valancy.

“Horrid, [Horrid - Ужасный]  sticky stuff!

And it has the vilest smell of any liniment I ever saw.

It’s no good.

I want to be left alone, that’s all.”  

Valancy went out, leaving Cousin Stickles aghast.  

“She’s feverish— [feverish - лихорадочный]  [feverish - лихорадочный] she _must_ be feverish,” ejaculated Cousin Stickles.  

Mrs. Frederick went on eating her supper.

It did not matter whether Valancy was or was not feverish. [feverish - лихорадочный] 

Valancy had been guilty of impertinence to _her_.     

CHAPTER VIII   Valancy did not sleep that night.

She lay awake all through the long dark hours—thinking—thinking.

She made a discovery that surprised her: she, who had been afraid of almost everything in life, was not afraid of death.

It did not seem in the least terrible to her.

And she need not now be afraid of anything else.

Why had she been afraid of things?

Because of life.

Afraid of Uncle Benjamin because of the menace of poverty in old age.

But now she would never be old—neglected—tolerated. [tolerated - терпит] 

Afraid of being an old maid all her life.

But now she would not be an old maid very long.

Afraid of offending  [offending - оскорбляющий] her mother and her clan  [clan - клан] because she had to live with and among them and couldn’t live peaceably if she didn’t give in to them.

But now she hadn’t.

Valancy felt a curious  [curious - любопытный] freedom.  

But she was still horribly afraid of one thing—the fuss the whole jamfry of them would make when she told them.

Valancy shuddered at the thought of it.

She couldn’t endure  [endure - терпит] it.

Oh, she knew so well how it would be.

First there would be indignation—yes, indignation on the part of Uncle James because she had gone to a doctor—any doctor—without consulting  [consulting - консультирует] HIM.

Indignation on the part of her mother for being so sly and deceitful—“to your own mother, Doss.”

Indignation on the part of the whole clan  [clan - клан] because she had not gone to Dr. Marsh.  

Then would come the solicitude.

She would be taken to Dr. Marsh, and when Dr. Marsh confirmed Dr. Trent’s diagnosis  [diagnosis - диагноз] she would be taken to specialists in Toronto and Montreal.

the doctors had given up”—and her mother would insist on Redfern’s Blood Bitters, and Cousin Stickles would insist on rubbing  [rubbing - тереть] her over the heart every night with Redfern’s Liniment on the grounds that it _might_ do good  [good - добро] and _couldn’t_ do harm; [harm - вред]  and everybody else would have some pet dope for her to take.

Dr. Stalling would come to her and say solemnly, “You are very ill.

Are you prepared for what may be before you?”—almost as if he were going to shake his forefinger at her, the forefinger that had not grown any shorter or less knobbly with age.

And she would be watched and checked like a baby and never let do anything or go anywhere alone.

Perhaps she would not even be allowed to sleep alone lest she die in her sleep.

Cousin Stickles or her mother would insist on sharing her room and bed.

Yes, undoubtedly  [undoubtedly - несомненно] they would.  

It was this last thought that really decided Valancy.

She could not put up with it and she wouldn’t.

As the clock in the hall below struck twelve Valancy suddenly and definitely made up her mind that she would not tell anybody.

She had always been told, ever since she could remember, that she must hide her feelings.

“It is not ladylike  [ladylike - дамский] to have feelings,” Cousin Stickles had once told her disapprovingly.

Well, she would hide them with a vengeance.  

But though she was not afraid of death she was not indifferent  [indifferent - безразличный] to it.

She found that she _resented_ [resented - обиженный]  it; it was not fair that she should have to die when she had never lived.

Rebellion  [Rebellion - Восстание] flamed up in her soul as the dark hours passed by—not because she had no future but because she had no past.  

“I’m poor—I’m ugly—I’m a failure—and I’m near death,” she thought.

She could see her own obituary  [obituary - некролог] notice in the Deerwood _Weekly Times_, copied into the Port Lawrence _Journal_.

“A deep  [deep - глубокий] gloom  [gloom - мрак] was cast  [cast - бросок] over Deerwood, etc., etc.”—“leaves

a large circle of friends to mourn, etc., etc., etc.”—lies, all lies.

Gloom, forsooth!

Nobody would miss her.

Her death would not matter a straw to anybody.

Not even her mother loved her—her mother who had been so disappointed that she was not a boy—or at least, a pretty girl.  

Valancy reviewed  [reviewed - пересмотренный] her whole life between midnight and the early spring dawn. [dawn - рассвет] 

These incidents were all unpleasant in one way or another.

Nothing really pleasant had ever happened to Valancy.  

“I’ve never had one wholly  [wholly - целиком] happy hour in my life—not one,” she thought.

“I’ve just been a colourless  [colourless - бесцветный] nonentity.

I remember reading somewhere once that there is an hour in which a woman might be happy all her life if she could but find it.

I’ve never found my hour—never, never.

And I never will now.

If I could only have had that hour I’d be willing  [willing - готовый] to die.”  

Those significant incidents kept bobbing up in her mind like unbidden ghosts, without any sequence of time or place.

For instance, that time when, at sixteen, she had blued a tubful of clothes too deeply.

And the time when, at eight, she had “stolen” some raspberry jam from Aunt Wellington’s pantry.

Valancy never heard the last of those two misdemeanours.

At almost every clan  [clan - клан] gathering they were raked up against her as jokes.

Uncle Benjamin hardly ever missed re-telling the raspberry jam incident—he had been the one to catch her, her face all stained  [stained - запятнанный] and streaked.  

“I have really done so few bad things that they have to keep harping on the old ones,” thought Valancy.

“Why, I’ve never even had a quarrel  [quarrel - ссора] with any one.

I haven’t an enemy.

What a spineless thing I must be not to have even one enemy!”  

There was that incident of the dust-pile at school when she was seven.

Valancy always recalled it when Dr. Stalling referred to the text, “To him that hath shall be given and from him that hath not shall be taken even that which he hath.”

Other people might puzzle  [puzzle - загадка] over that text but it never puzzled  [puzzled - озадаченный] Valancy.

The whole relationship between herself and Olive, dating from the day of the dust-pile, was a commentary  [commentary - комментарий] on it.  

She had been going to school a year, but Olive, who was a year younger, had just begun and had about her all the glamour  [glamour - гламур] of “a new girl” and an exceedingly pretty girl at that.

It was at recess  [recess - перерыв] and all the girls, big and little, were out on the road in front of the school making dust-piles.

The aim of each girl was to have the biggest pile.

Valancy was good at making dust-piles—there was an art in it—and she had secret hopes of leading.

But Olive, working off by herself, was suddenly discovered to have a larger dust-pile than anybody.

Valancy felt no jealousy.

Her dust-pile was quite big enough to please her.

Then one of the older girls had an inspiration. [inspiration - вдохновение]   

“Let’s put all our dust on Olive’s pile and make a tremendous  [tremendous - огромный] one,” she exclaimed. [exclaimed - воскликнул]   

Valancy turned away  [away - прочь] resolutely  [resolutely - решительно] and began building another dust-pile.

Again a bigger girl pounced  [pounced - набросился] on it.

Valancy stood before it, flushed, indignant, arms outspread.  

“Don’t take it,” she pleaded. [pleaded - умолял] 

“Please don’t take it.”  

“But _why_?” demanded the older girl.

“Why won’t you help to build Olive’s bigger?”  

“I want my own little dust-pile,” said Valancy piteously.  

Her plea  [plea - умолял] went unheeded.

While she argued with one girl another scraped up her dust-pile.

Valancy turned away, her heart swelling, her eyes full of tears.  

“Jealous—you’re jealous!” said the girls mockingly.  

“You were very selfish,” said her mother coldly, when Valancy told her about it at night.

That was the first and last time Valancy had ever taken any of her troubles to her mother.  

Valancy was neither jealous nor selfish.

It was only that she wanted a dust-pile of her own—small or big mattered not.

A team of horses came down the street—Olive’s dust pile was scattered over the roadway—the bell rang—the girls trooped into school and had forgotten the whole affair before they reached their seats.

Valancy never forgot it.

To this day she resented  [resented - возмущался] it in her secret soul.

But was it not symbolical of her life?  

“I’ve never been able to have my own dust-pile,” thought Valancy.  

The enormous red moon she had seen rising right at the end of the street one autumn evening of her sixth year.

She had been sick and cold with the awful, uncanny horror of it.

So near to her.

So big.

She had run in trembling to her mother and her mother had laughed at her.

She had gone to bed and hidden her face under the clothes in terror  [terror - ужас] lest she might look at the window and see that horrible moon glaring in at her through it.  

The boy who had tried to kiss her at a party when she was fifteen.

She had not let him—she had evaded him and run.

He was the only boy who had ever tried to kiss her.

Now, fourteen years later, Valancy found herself wishing that she had let him.  

The time she had been made to apologise to Olive for something she hadn’t done.

Olive had said that Valancy had pushed her into the mud and spoiled  [spoiled - испорченный] her new shoes _on purpose_.

Valancy knew she hadn’t.

It had been an accident—and even that wasn’t her fault—but nobody would believe her.

She had to apologise—and kiss Olive to “make up.”

The injustice  [injustice - несправедливость] of it burned in her soul tonight.  

That summer when Olive had the most beautiful hat, trimmed with creamy  [creamy - сливочный] yellow net, with a wreath of red roses  [roses - розы] and little ribbon bows under the chin.

Valancy had wanted a hat like that more than she had ever wanted anything.
