Behind the house was a long stretch of useless  [useless - бесполезный] barrens, full of scrub pines  [pines - сосны] and spruces, with here and there a blossoming bit of wild cherry, [cherry - вишня]  running back to a belt of timber  [timber - бревен] on the shores  [shores - берега] of Lake Mistawis, two miles away.

Roaring  [Roaring - Ревущий] Abel met Valancy at the door.  

“So you’ve come,” he said incredulously.

“I never s’posed that ruck of Stirlings would let you.”  

Valancy showed all her pointed teeth in a grin. [grin - ухмылка]   

“They couldn’t stop me.”  

“I didn’t think you’d so much spunk,” said Roaring  [Roaring - Ревущий] Abel admiringly. [admiringly - восхищенно] 

“And look at the nice ankles of her,” he added, as he stepped aside  [aside - в сторону] to let her in.  

If Cousin Stickles had heard this she would have been certain that Valancy’s doom, [doom - гибель]  earthly and unearthly, [unearthly - неземной]  was sealed.

But Abel’s superannuated gallantry did not worry Valancy.

Besides, this was the first compliment she had ever received in her life and she found herself liking it.

She sometimes suspected  [suspected - подозреваемый] she had nice ankles, but nobody had ever mentioned it before.

In the Stirling clan  [clan - клан] ankles were among the unmentionables.  

Roaring  [Roaring - Ревущий] Abel took her into the kitchen, where Cissy Gay was lying on the sofa, breathing quickly, with little scarlet spots on her hollow  [hollow - впалые] cheeks. [cheeks - щеки] 

Valancy had not seen Cecilia Gay for years.

Then she had been such a pretty creature, a slight, [slight - легкий]  blossom- [blossom - цветок] like girl, with soft, golden  [golden - золотой] hair, clear-cut, almost waxen features, and large, beautiful blue eyes.

She was shocked at the change in her.

Could this be sweet Cissy—this pitiful little thing that looked like a tired, broken flower?

She had wept all the beauty out of her eyes; they looked too big—enormous—in her wasted face.

The contrast was so terrible that Valancy’s own eyes filled with tears.

She knelt  [knelt - встал на колени] down by Cissy and put her arms about her.  

“Cissy dear, I’ve come to look after you.

I’ll stay with you till—till—as long as you want me.”  

“Oh!”

Cissy put her thin arms about Valancy’s neck.

“Oh—_will_ you?

It’s been so—lonely.

I can wait on myself—but it’s been so _lonely_.

It—would just be like—heaven—to have some one here—like you.

You were always—so sweet to me—long ago.”  

Valancy held Cissy close.

She was suddenly happy.

Here was some one who needed her—some one she could help.

She was no longer a superfluity.

Old things had passed away; everything had become new.  

“Most things are predestinated, but some are just darn sheer  [sheer - отвесный] luck,” said Roaring Abel, complacently smoking his pipe in the corner.     

CHAPTER XVII   When Valancy had lived for a week at Roaring Abel’s she felt as if years had separated  [separated - отделенный] her from her old life and all the people she had known in it.

They were beginning to seem remote—dream-like—far-away—and as the days went on they seemed still more so, until they ceased  [ceased - прекратившийся] to matter altogether. [altogether - совсем]   

She was happy.

Nobody ever bothered her with conundrums or insisted on giving her Purple Pills.

Nobody called her Doss or worried her about catching cold.

There were no quilts to piece, no abominable rubber-plant to water, no ice-cold maternal  [maternal - материнский] tantrums  [tantrums - истерики] to endure. [endure - терпит] 

She could be alone whenever she liked, go to bed when she liked, sneeze  [sneeze - чих] when she liked.

Sometimes of an afternoon, when Cissy was strong enough, the two girls went into the barrens and looked at the wood-flowers.

But they did not pick any.

Valancy had read to Cissy the gospel thereof according to John Foster: “It is a pity  [pity - жалость] to gather wood-flowers.

They lose half their witchery away from the green and the flicker. [flicker - мерцание] 

The way to enjoy wood-flowers is to track  [track - трек] them down to their remote haunts—gloat over them—and then leave them with backward glances, taking with us only the beguiling  [beguiling - обманчивый] memory of their grace  [grace - изящество] and fragrance. [fragrance - аромат] ”  

Valancy was in the midst  [midst - середина] of realities after a lifetime  [lifetime - время жизни] of unrealities.

And busy—very busy.

The house had to be cleaned.

Not for nothing had Valancy been brought up in the Stirling habits of neatness and cleanliness. [cleanliness - чистота] 

If she found satisfaction  [satisfaction - удовлетворение] in cleaning dirty rooms she got her fill of it there.

Roaring  [Roaring - ревущий] Abel thought she was foolish to bother doing so much more than she was asked to do, but he did not interfere  [interfere - мешать] with her.

He was very well satisfied with his bargain. [bargain - сделка] 

Valancy was a good cook.

Abel said she got a flavour  [flavour - аромат] into things.

The only fault he found with her was that she did not sing at her work.  

“Folks should always sing at their work,” he insisted.

“Sounds cheerful-like.”  

“Not always,” retorted Valancy.

“Fancy a butcher singing at his work.

Or an undertaker.”  

Abel burst  [burst - взрыв] into his great broad laugh.  

“There’s no getting the better of you.

You’ve got an answer every time.

I should think the Stirlings would be glad to be rid of you.

_

They_ don’t like being sassed back.”  

During the day Abel was generally away from home—if not working, then shooting or fishing with Barney Snaith.

He generally came home at nights—always very late and often very drunk.

The first night they heard him come howling into the yard, Cissy had told Valancy not to be afraid.  

“Father never does anything—he just makes a noise.”  

For the most part, Abel was a good-natured creature, but occasionally he had a temper.

Once Valancy asked him coolly:  “What is the use of getting in a rage? [rage - ярость] ”  “It’s such a d——d relief, [relief - облегчение] ” said Abel.  

They both burst  [burst - взрыв] out laughing together.  

“You’re a great little sport,” said Abel admiringly. [admiringly - восхищенно] 

“Don’t mind my bad French.

I don’t mean a thing by it.

Jest habit.

Say, I like a woman that ain’t afraid to speak up to me.

Sis there was always too meek—too meek.

That’s why she got adrift.

I like you.”  

“All the same,” said Valancy determinedly, “there is no use in sending things to hell  [hell - ад] as you’re always doing.

And I’m _not_ going to have you tracking  [tracking - отслеживание] mud all over a floor I’ve just scrubbed.

You _must_

use the scraper whether you consign it to perdition or not.”  

Cissy loved the cleanness and neatness.

She had kept it so, too, until her strength failed.

She was very pitifully happy because she had Valancy with her.

It had been so terrible—the long, lonely days and nights with no companionship  [companionship - общение] save those dreadful  [dreadful - ужасный] old women who came to work.

Cissy had hated and feared  [feared - страшный] them.

She clung  [clung - цепляющийся] to Valancy like a child.  

There was no doubt that Cissy was dying.

Yet at no time did she seem alarmingly ill.

She did not even cough a great deal.

Most days she was able to get up and dress—sometimes even to work about in the garden or the barrens for an hour or two.

For a few weeks after Valancy’s coming  [coming - приближающийся] she seemed so much better that Valancy began to hope she might get well.

But Cissy shook her head.  

“No, I can’t get well.

My lungs are almost gone.

And I—don’t want to.

I’m so tired, Valancy.

Only dying can rest me.

But it’s lovely to have you here—you’ll never know how much it means to me.

But Valancy—you work too hard.

You don’t need to—Father only wants his meals cooked.

I don’t think you are strong yourself.

You turn so pale sometimes.

And those drops you take.

_Are_ you well, dear?”  

“I’m all right,” said Valancy lightly.

She would not have Cissy worried.

“And I’m not working hard.

I’m glad to have some work to do—something that really wants to be done.”  

“Then”—Cissy slipped her hand wistfully into Valancy’s—“don’t let’s talk any more about my being sick.

Let’s just forget it.

Let’s pretend I’m a little girl again—and you have come here to play with me.

I used to wish that long ago—wish that you could come.

I knew you couldn’t, of course.

But how I did wish it!

You always seemed so different from the other girls—so kind and sweet—and as if you had something in yourself nobody knew about—some dear, pretty secret.

_

Had_ you, Valancy?”  

“I had my Blue Castle,” said Valancy, laughing a little.

She was pleased that Cissy had thought of her like this.

She had never suspected  [suspected - подозреваемый] that anybody liked or admired or wondered about her.

She told Cissy all about her Blue Castle.

She had never told any one about it before.  

“Every one has a Blue Castle, I think,” said Cissy softly.

“Only every one has a different name for it.

_

I_ had mine—once.”  

She put her two thin little hands over her face.

She did not tell Valancy—then—who had destroyed her Blue Castle.

But Valancy knew that, whoever it was, it was not Barney Snaith.     

CHAPTER XVIII   Valancy was acquainted with Barney by now—well acquainted, it seemed, though she had spoken to him only a few times.

But then she had felt just as well acquainted with him the first time they had met.

She had been in the garden at twilight, hunting for a few stalks of white narcissus for Cissy’s room when she heard that terrible old Grey Slosson coming down through the woods from Mistawis—one could hear it miles away.

Valancy did not look up as it drew near, thumping over the rocks in that crazy lane. [lane - полоса] 

She had never looked up, though Barney had gone racketting past every evening since she had been at Roaring  [Roaring - ревущий] Abel’s.

This time he did not racket past.

The old Grey Slosson stopped with even more terrible noises than it made going.

Valancy was conscious that Barney had sprung from it and was leaning  [leaning - наклоняющийся] over the ramshackle gate.

Neither of his eyebrows  [eyebrows - брови] looked like the other.

He was thin—too thin—she wished she could feed him up a bit—she wished she could sew the buttons on his coat—and make him cut his hair—and shave every day.

There was _something_ in his face—one hardly knew what it was.

Tiredness? [Tiredness - Усталость] 

Sadness?

Disillusionment? [Disillusionment - Разочарование] 

He had dimples in his thin cheeks  [cheeks - щеки] when he smiled.

All these thoughts flashed  [flashed - вспыхнули] through Valancy’s mind in that one moment while his eyes looked into hers.  

“Good-evening, Miss Stirling.”  

Nothing could be more commonplace  [commonplace - банальность] and conventional. [conventional - условные] 

Any one might have said it.

But Barney Snaith had a way of saying things that gave them poignancy.

When he said good-evening you felt that it _was_ a good evening and that it was partly his doing that it was.

Also, you felt that some of the credit was yours.

Valancy felt all this vaguely, but she couldn’t imagine why she was trembling from head to foot—it _must_ be her heart.

If only he didn’t notice it!  

“I’m going over to the Port,” Barney was saying.

“Can I acquire merit  [merit - заслуги] by getting or doing anything there for you or Cissy?”  “Will you get some salt codfish for us?” said Valancy.

It was the only thing she could think of.

Roaring  [Roaring - Рев] Abel had expressed a desire that day for a dinner of boiled salt codfish.

When her knights came riding to the Blue Castle, Valancy had sent them on many a quest, [quest - квест]  but she had never asked any of them to get her salt codfish.  

“Certainly.

You’re sure there’s nothing else?

Lots of room in Lady Jane Grey Slosson.

And she always gets back _some_ time, does Lady Jane.”  

“I don’t think there’s anything more,” said Valancy.

She knew he would bring oranges for Cissy anyhow—he always did.  

Barney did not turn away at once.

He was silent for a little.

Then he said, slowly and whimsically:  “Miss Stirling, you’re a brick! [brick - кирпич] 

You’re a whole cartload of bricks. [bricks - кирпичи] 

To come here and look after Cissy—under the circumstances. [circumstances - обстоятельства] ”  

“There’s nothing so bricky about that,” said Valancy.

“I’d nothing else to do.

And—I like it here.

I don’t feel as if I’d done anything specially meritorious.

Mr. Gay is paying me fair wages. [wages - зарплата] 

I never earned any money before—and I like it.”

It seemed so easy to talk to Barney Snaith, someway—this terrible Barney Snaith of the lurid tales and mysterious past—as easy and natural as if talking to herself.  

“All the money in the world couldn’t buy what you’re doing for Cissy Gay,” said Barney.

“It’s splendid and fine of you.

And if there’s anything I can do to help you in any way, you have only to let me know.

If Roaring  [Roaring - Рев] Abel ever tries to annoy you——”  “He doesn’t.

He’s lovely to me.

“So do I.

But there’s one stage of his drunkenness—perhaps you haven’t encountered  [encountered - столкнулась] it yet—when he sings ribald songs——”  “Oh, yes.

He came home last night like that.

Cissy and I just went to our room and shut ourselves in where we couldn’t hear him.

He apologised this morning.

I’m not afraid of any of Roaring  [Roaring - ревущая] Abel’s stages.”  

“Well, I’m sure he’ll be decent  [decent - приличная] to you, apart from his inebriated yowls,” said Barney.

“And I’ve told him he’s got to stop damning things when you’re around.”  

“Why?” asked Valancy slily, with one of her odd, [odd - нечетная]  slanted glances and a sudden flake of pink on each cheek, [cheek - щека]  born of the thought that Barney Snaith had actually done so much for _her_.

“I often feel like damning things myself.”  

For a moment Barney stared.

Was this elfin girl the little, old-maidish creature who had stood there two minutes ago?

Surely there was magic and devilry going on in that shabby, [shabby - потрепанная]  weedy old garden.  

Then he laughed.  

“It will be a relief  [relief - облегчение] to have some one to do it for you, then.

So you don’t want anything but salt codfish?”  

“Not tonight.

But I dare  [dare - смелость] say I’ll have some errands for you very often when you go to Port Lawrence.

I can’t trust Mr. Gay to remember to bring all the things I want.”  

Barney had gone away, then, in his Lady Jane, and Valancy stood in the garden for a long time.  

Since then he had called several times, walking down through the barrens, whistling.

How that whistle of his echoed through the spruces on those June twilights!

Valancy caught herself listening for it every evening—rebuked herself—then let herself go.

Why shouldn’t she listen for it?  

He always brought Cissy fruit and flowers.

Once he brought Valancy a box of candy—the first box of candy she had ever been given.

It seemed sacrilege  [sacrilege - кощунство] to eat it.  

She found herself thinking of him in season and out of season.

She wanted to know if he ever thought about her when she wasn’t before his eyes, and, if so, what.

She wanted to see that mysterious house of his back on the Mistawis island.

Cissy had never seen it.

Cissy, though she talked freely  [freely - свободно] of Barney and had known him for five years, really knew little more of him than Valancy herself.  

“But he isn’t bad,” said Cissy.

“Nobody need  [need - потребность] ever tell me he is.

He _can’t_ have done a thing to be ashamed of.”  

“Then why does he live as he does?” asked Valancy—to hear somebody defend him.  

“I don’t know.

He’s a mystery.

And of course there’s something behind it, but I _know_

it isn’t disgrace. [disgrace - позор] 

Barney Snaith simply couldn’t do anything disgraceful, Valancy.”  

Valancy was not so sure.

Barney must have done _something_—sometime.

He was a man of education and intelligence.

She had soon discovered that, in listening to his conversations and wrangles with Roaring Abel—who was surprisingly well read and could discuss any subject under the sun when sober. [sober - трезвый] 

Such a man wouldn’t bury himself for five years in Muskoka and live and look like a tramp  [tramp - бродяга] if there were not too good—or bad—a reason for it.

But it didn’t matter.

All that mattered was that she was sure now that he had never been Cissy Gay’s lover.

There was nothing like _that_ between them.

Though he was very fond  [fond - любящая] of Cissy and she of him, as any one could see.

But it was a fondness that didn’t worry Valancy.  

“You don’t know what Barney has been to me, these past two years,” Cissy had said simply.

“_

Barney told weird  [weird - странный] tales of his adventures with “shacks” on trains while hoboing it across the continent.

Valancy thought she ought to think his stealing rides quite dreadful, [dreadful - ужасный]  but didn’t.

The story of his working his way to England on a cattle- [cattle - скот] ship sounded more legitimate. [legitimate - законный] 

And his yarns of the Yukon enthralled her—especially the one of the night he was lost on the divide between Gold Run and Sulphur Valley.

He had spent two years out there.

Where in all this was there room for the penitentiary and the other things?  

If he were telling the truth.

But Valancy knew he was.  

“Found no gold,” he said.

“Came away poorer than when I went.

But such a place to live!

Those silences at the back of the north wind _got_ me.

I’ve never belonged to myself since.”  

Yet he was not a great talker.

He told a great deal in a few well-chosen words—how well-chosen Valancy did not realise.

And he had a knack of saying things without opening his mouth at all.  

“I like a man whose eyes say more than his lips,” thought Valancy.  

But then she liked everything about him—his tawny hair—his whimsical smiles—the little glints of fun in his eyes—his loyal  [loyal - верный] affection  [affection - привязанность] for that unspeakable Lady Jane—his habit of sitting with his hands in his pockets, his chin sunk on his breast, looking up from under his mismated eyebrows. [eyebrows - брови] 

She liked his nice voice which sounded as if it might become caressing or wooing  [wooing - ухаживание] with very little provocation.

She was at times almost afraid to let herself think these thoughts.

They were so vivid that she felt as if the others _must_

know what she was thinking.  

“I’ve been watching a woodpecker all day,” he said one evening on the shaky  [shaky - шаткий] old back verandah. [verandah - веранда] 

His account of the woodpecker’s doings was satisfying.

He had often some gay  [gay - веселая] or cunning little anecdote of the wood folk to tell them.

The barrens lay before her in a white moon splendour, where dozens of little rabbits frisked.

Barney, when he liked, could sit down on the edge of the barrens and lure  [lure - приманка] those rabbits right to him by some mysterious sorcery he possessed.

Valancy had once seen a squirrel leap  [leap - прыжок] from a scrub pine  [pine - сосна] to his shoulder and sit there chattering to him.

It reminded her of John Foster.  

It was one of the delights of Valancy’s new life that she could read John Foster’s books as often and as long as she liked.

She could read them in bed if she wanted to.

She read them all to Cissy, who loved them.

She also tried to read them to Abel and Barney, who did not love them.

Abel was bored and Barney politely refused to listen at all.  

“Piffle,” said Barney.     

CHAPTER XIX   Of course, the Stirlings had not left the poor maniac alone all this time or refrained from heroic efforts to rescue her perishing soul and reputation.

Uncle James, whose lawyer had helped him as little as his doctor, came one day and, finding Valancy alone in the kitchen, as he supposed, gave her a terrible talking- [talking - говорение] to—told her she was breaking her mother’s heart and disgracing her family.  

“But _why_?” said Valancy, not ceasing  [ceasing - прекращение] to scour her porridge pot decently.

“I’m doing honest work for honest pay.

What is there in that that is disgraceful?”  “Don’t quibble, Valancy,” said Uncle James solemnly.

“This is no fit place for you to be, and you know it.

Why, I’m told that jail-bird, Snaith, is hanging around here every evening.”  

“Not _every_ evening,” said Valancy reflectively.

“No, not quite every evening.”  

“It’s—it’s insufferable!” said Uncle James violently.

“Valancy, you _must_ come home.

We won’t judge your harshly.

I assure  [assure - уверить] you we won’t.

We will overlook  [overlook - пропустить] all this.”  

“Thank you,” said Valancy.  

“Have you no sense of shame?” demanded Uncle James.  

“Oh, yes.

But the things _I_ am ashamed of are not the things _you_ are ashamed of.”

Valancy proceeded  [proceeded - прошел] to rinse her dishcloth meticulously.  

Still was Uncle James patient.

He gripped  [gripped - захватил] the sides of his chair and ground  [ground - землю] his teeth.  

“We know your mind isn’t just right.

We’ll make allowances. [allowances - пособия] 

But you _must_ come home.

You shall not stay here with that drunken, [drunken - пьяный]  blasphemous  [blasphemous - кощунственный] old scoundrel——”  “Were you by any chance referring to _me_, _Mister_ Stirling?” demanded Roaring Abel, suddenly appearing in the doorway  [doorway - проем] of the back verandah  [verandah - веранда] where he had been smoking a peaceful pipe and listening to “old Jim Stirling’s” tirade with huge enjoyment!

Roaring  [Roaring - Ревущий] Abel crossed the kitchen at a bound, [bound - связанный]  caught him by his collar and his trousers, and hurled him through the doorway  [doorway - дверной проем] and over the garden paling with as little apparent effort as he might have employed  [employed - занятый] in whisking a troublesome  [troublesome - проблемный] kitten out of the way.  

“The next time you come back here,” he bellowed, [bellowed - ревел]  “I’ll throw you through the window—and all the better if the window is shut!

Coming here, thinking yourself God to put the world to rights!”  

Valancy candidly  [candidly - откровенно] and unashamedly  [unashamedly - нагло] owned to herself that she had seen few more satisfying sights than Uncle James’ coat-tails  [tails - хвосты] flying out into the asparagus bed.

She had once been afraid of this man’s judgment. [judgment - суд] 

Now she saw clearly that he was nothing but a rather stupid little village tin-god. [god - бог]   

Roaring  [Roaring - Ревущий] Abel turned with his great broad laugh.  

“He’ll think of that for years when he wakes up in the night.

The Almighty made a mistake in making so many Stirlings.

But since they are made, we’ve got to reckon  [reckon - считаться] with them.

Too many to kill out.

But if they come here bothering you I’ll shoo ’em off before a cat could lick  [lick - лизать] its ear.”  

The next time they sent Dr. Stalling.

Surely Roaring  [Roaring - Ревущий] Abel would not throw him into asparagus beds.

He, Dr. Stalling, had never been able to understand her.

Therefore, beyond doubt, she was queer. [queer - странный] 

She was only just a little queerer than usual now.

And Dr. Stalling had his own reasons for disliking Roaring Abel.

When Dr. Stalling had first come to Deerwood he had had a liking  [liking - нравящийся] for long hikes around Mistawis and Muskoka.

On one of these occasions he had got lost and after much wandering had fallen in with Roaring  [Roaring - Ревущий] Abel with his gun over his shoulder.  

Dr. Stalling had contrived to ask his question in about the most idiotic manner possible.

He said, “Can you tell me where I’m going?”  

“How the devil  [devil - дьявол] should I know where you’re going, gosling?” retorted Abel contemptuously.  

Dr. Stalling was so enraged that he could not speak for a moment or two and in that moment Abel had disappeared in the woods.

Dr. Stalling had eventually found his way home, but he had never hankered to encounter  [encounter - встреча] Abel Gay again.  

Nevertheless he came now to do his duty.

Valancy greeted him with a sinking heart.

She had to own to herself that she was terribly  [terribly - ужасно] afraid of Dr. Stalling still.

She had a miserable  [miserable - жалкий] conviction  [conviction - убеждение] that if he shook his long, bony  [bony - костяной] finger at her and told her to go home, she dared not disobey.  

“Mr. Gay,” said Dr. Stalling politely and condescendingly, “may I see Miss Stirling alone for a few minutes?”  

Roaring  [Roaring - Ревущий] Abel was a little drunk—just drunk enough to be excessively polite and very cunning.

He had been on the point of going away when Dr. Stalling arrived, but now he sat down in a corner of the parlour  [parlour - гостиная] and folded his arms.  

“No, no, mister,” he said solemnly.

“That wouldn’t do—wouldn’t do at all.

I’ve got the reputation of my household to keep up.

I’ve got to chaperone this young lady.

Can’t have any sparkin’ going on here behind my back.”  

Outraged Dr. Stalling looked so terrible that Valancy wondered how Abel could endure  [endure - терпеть] his aspect.

But Abel was not worried at all.  

“D’ye know anything about it, anyway?”

he asked genially.  

“About _what_?”  “Sparking,” said Abel coolly.  

Poor Dr. Stalling, who had never married because he believed in a celibate clergy, would not notice this ribald remark. [remark - замечание] 

He turned his back on Abel and addressed himself to Valancy.  

“Miss Stirling, I am here in response to your mother’s wishes.

She begged me to come.

I am charged with some messages from her.

Will you”—he wagged his forefinger—“will you hear them?”  

“Yes,” said Valancy faintly, [faintly - слабо]  eyeing the forefinger.

It had a hypnotic effect on her.  

“The first is this.

Back of her terror  [terror - террор] Valancy smiled in secret.

Uncle James must indeed regard the matter as desperate when he would loosen his purse-strings like that.

At any rate, her clan  [clan - клан] no longer despised  [despised - презираемый] her or ignored her.

She had become important to them.  

“That’s _my_ business, Mister,” said Abel.

“Miss Stirling can go if she pleases, or stay if she pleases.

I made a fair bargain  [bargain - сделка] with her, and she’s free to conclude it when she likes.

She gives me meals that stick to my ribs. [ribs - ребра] 

She don’t forget to put salt in the porridge.

She never slams  [slams - хлопает] doors, and when she has nothing to say she don’t talk.

That’s uncanny in a woman, you know, Mister.

I’m satisfied.

If she isn’t, she’s free to go.

But no woman comes here in Jim Stirling’s pay.

If any one does”—Abel’s voice was uncannily bland and polite—“I’ll spatter the road with her brains.

Tell him that with A. Gay’s compliments.”  

“Dr. Stalling, a nurse is not what Cissy needs,” said Valancy earnestly.

“She isn’t so ill as that, yet.

What she wants is companionship— [companionship - товарищество] somebody she knows and likes just to live with her.

You can understand that, I’m sure.”  

“I understand that your motive  [motive - мотив] is quite—ahem—commendable.”

Dr. Stalling felt that he was very broad-minded  [minded - мыслящий] indeed—especially as in his secret soul he did not believe Valancy’s motive  [motive - мотив] _was_ commendable.

He hadn’t the least idea what she was up to, but he was sure her motive  [motive - мотив] was not commendable.

When he could not understand a thing he straightway condemned  [condemned - осужденный] it.

Simplicity  [Simplicity - простота] itself!

“But your first duty is to your mother.

_

She_ needs you.

She implores you to come home—she will forgive everything if you will only come home.”  

“That’s a pretty little thought,” remarked Abel meditatively, as he ground  [ground - земля] some tobacco  [tobacco - табак] up in his hand.  

Dr. Stalling ignored him.  

“She entreats, but I, Miss Stirling,”—Dr.

Get your hat and coat and come _now_.”  

Dr. Stalling shook his finger at Valancy.

Before that pitiless finger she drooped and wilted visibly. [visibly - зримо]   

“She’s giving in,” thought Roaring Abel.

“She’ll go with him.

Beats  [Beats - Удары] all, the power these preacher fellows have over women.”  

Valancy _was_ on the point of obeying  [obeying - повинуясь] Dr. Stalling.

She must go home with him—and give up.

She would lapse  [lapse - падению] back to Doss Stirling again and for her few remaining days or weeks be the cowed, futile creature she had always been.

It was her fate— [fate - судьбе] typified by that relentless, [relentless - неустанно]  uplifted forefinger.

She could no more escape from it than Roaring  [Roaring - Рев] Abel from his predestination.

She eyed it as the fascinated  [fascinated - очарованный] bird eyes the snake.

Another moment—  “_Fear is the original sin_ [sin - грех] ,” suddenly said a still, small voice away back—back—back of Valancy’s consciousness. [consciousness - сознание] 

“_Almost all the evil  [evil - зло] in the world has its origin in the fact that some one is afraid of something_.”  

Valancy stood up.

She was still in the clutches  [clutches - хватки] of fear, but her soul was her own again.

She would not be false to that inner voice.  

“Dr. Stalling,” she said slowly, “I do not at present owe _any_ duty to my mother.

She is quite well; she has all the assistance  [assistance - помощь] and companionship  [companionship - товарищество] she requires; she does not need me at all.

I _am_ needed here.

I am going to stay here.”  

Do you remember your childhood days——”  “Perfectly.

And hate them.”  

“Do you realise what people will say?

What they _are_ saying?”  

“I can imagine it,” said Valancy, with a shrug of her shoulders.

She was suddenly free of fear again.

“I haven’t listened to the gossip of Deerwood teaparties and sewing  [sewing - шитье] circles twenty years for nothing.

But, Dr. Stalling, it doesn’t matter in the least to me what they say—not in the least.”  

Dr. Stalling went away then.

A girl who cared nothing for public opinion!

Over whom sacred  [sacred - священное] family ties had no restraining influence!

Who hated her childhood memories!  

Then Cousin Georgiana came—on her own initiative, [initiative - инициатива]  for nobody would have thought it worth while to send her.

She found Valancy alone, weeding the little vegetable garden she had planted, and she made all the platitudinous pleas  [pleas - просьбы] she could think of.

Valancy heard her patiently. [patiently - терпеливо] 

Cousin Georgiana wasn’t such a bad old soul.

Then she said:  “And now that you have got all that out of your system, Cousin Georgiana, can you tell me how to make creamed codfish so that it will not be as thick as porridge and as salt as the Dead Sea?”  *

* *

*

* * *  “We’ll just have to _wait_,” said Uncle Benjamin.

“After all, Cissy Gay can’t live long.

Dr. Marsh tells me she may drop off any day.”  

Mrs. Frederick wept.

It would really have been so much easier to bear if Valancy had died.

She could have worn mourning then.     

CHAPTER XX   When Abel Gay paid Valancy her first month’s wages— [wages - заработная плата] which he did promptly, [promptly - незамедлительно]  in bills reeking with the odour of tobacco  [tobacco - табак] and whiskey— [whiskey - виски] Valancy went into Deerwood and spent every cent of it.

She got a pretty green crêpe  [crêpe - креп] dress with a girdle of crimson beads, at a bargain  [bargain - сделка] sale, a pair of silk stockings, to match, and a little crinkled green hat with a crimson rose in it.

She even bought a foolish little beribboned and belaced nightgown.  

She passed the house on Elm Street twice—Valancy never even thought about it as “home”—but saw no one.

No doubt her mother was sitting in the room this lovely June evening playing solitaire—and cheating.

Valancy knew that Mrs. Frederick always cheated. [cheated - обманул] 

She never lost a game.

Most of the people Valancy met looked at her seriously and passed her with a cool nod.

Nobody stopped to speak to her.  

Valancy put on her green dress when she got home.

Then she took it off again.

She felt so miserably  [miserably - жалко] undressed in its low neck and short sleeves.

And that low, crimson girdle around the hips  [hips - бедра] seemed positively indecent.

She hung it up in the closet, feeling flatly that she had wasted her money.

She would never have the courage to wear that dress.

John Foster’s arraignment of fear had no power to stiffen  [stiffen - напрягся] her against this.

In this one thing habit and custom were still all-powerful.

She wished she could have left it on.

But there were some things John Foster did not know.  

Every Sunday evening Valancy went to the little Free Methodist church in a valley on the edge of “up back”—a spireless little grey building among the pines, [pines - сосны]  with a few sunken graves and mossy gravestones  [gravestones - надгробия] in the small, paling-encircled, grass-grown square beside it.

She liked the minister  [minister - министр] who preached  [preached - проповедовал] there.

He was so simple and sincere. [sincere - искренний] 

An old man, who lived in Port Lawrence and came out by the lake in a little disappearing propeller boat to give a free service to the people of the small, stony farms back of the hills, who would otherwise never have heard any gospel message.

She liked the simple service and the fervent singing.

She liked to sit by the open window and look out into the pine woods.

The congregation  [congregation - собрание] was always small.

The Free Methodists were few in number, poor and generally illiterate.

But Valancy loved those Sunday evenings.

For the first time in her life she liked going to church.

The rumour  [rumour - слух] reached Deerwood that she had “turned Free Methodist” and sent Mrs. Frederick to bed for a day.

But Valancy had not turned anything.

She went to the church because she liked it and because in some inexplicable  [inexplicable - необъяснимый] way it did her good.

Old Mr. Towers believed exactly what he preached  [preached - проповедовал] and somehow it made a tremendous  [tremendous - огромный] difference.  

Oddly  [Oddly - странно] enough, Roaring  [Roaring - ревущий] Abel disapproved  [disapproved - неодобренный] of her going to the hill church as strongly as Mrs. Frederick herself could have done.

He had “no use for Free Methodists.

He was a Presbyterian.”

But Valancy went in spite  [spite - злоба] of him.  

“We’ll hear something worse than _that_ about her soon,” Uncle Benjamin predicted gloomily.  

They did.  

Valancy could not quite explain, even to herself, just why she wanted to go to that party.

It was a dance “up back” at Chidley Corners; and dances at Chidley Corners were not, as a rule, the sort of assemblies  [assemblies - собрания] where well-brought-up young ladies were found.

But the idea of going had never occurred to her until Roaring  [Roaring - Ревущий] Abel himself broached it at supper.  

“You come with me to the dance,” he ordered.

“It’ll do you good—put some colour in your face.

You look peaked—you want something to liven you up.”  

Valancy found herself suddenly wanting to go.

She knew nothing at all of what dances at Chidley Corners were apt  [apt - меткий] to be like.

Her idea of dances had been fashioned on the correct affairs that went by that name in Deerwood and Port Lawrence.

Of course she knew the Corners’ dance wouldn’t be just like them.

Much more informal, [informal - неформальный]  of course.

But so much the more interesting.

Why shouldn’t she go?

Cissy was in a week of apparent health and improvement.

She wouldn’t mind staying alone in the least.

She entreated Valancy to go if she wanted to.

And Valancy _did_ want to go.  

She went to her room to dress.

A rage  [rage - ярость] against the snuff-brown silk seized  [seized - захваченный] her.

Wear that to a party!

Never.

She pulled her green crêpe  [crêpe - креп] from its hanger and put it on feverishly.

It was nonsense  [nonsense - бред] to feel so—so—naked— [naked - голый] just because her neck and arms were bare. [bare - голый] 

That was just her old maidishness.

She would not be ridden by it.

On went the dress—the slippers.  

It was the first time she had worn a pretty dress since the organdies of her early teens.

And _they_ had never made her look like this.  

If she only had a necklace or something.

She wouldn’t feel so bare  [bare - голый] then.

She ran down to the garden.

There were clovers there—great crimson things growing in the long grass.

Valancy gathered handfuls of them and strung them on a cord. [cord - шнур] 

Fastened above her neck they gave her the comfortable sensation  [sensation - сенсация] of a collar and were oddly  [oddly - странно] becoming.

Another circlet of them went round her hair, dressed in the low puffs that became her.

Excitement brought those faint pink stains  [stains - пятна] to her face.

She flung on her coat and pulled the little, twisty hat over her hair.  

“You look so nice and—and—different, dear,” said Cissy.

“Like a green moonbeam with a gleam  [gleam - блеск] of red in it, if there could be such a thing.”  

Valancy stooped to kiss her.  

“I don’t feel right about leaving you alone, Cissy.”  

“Oh, I’ll be all right.

I feel better tonight than I have for a long while.

I’ve been feeling badly to see you sticking here so closely on my account.

I hope you’ll have a nice time.

I never was at a party at the Corners, but I used to go sometimes, long ago, to dances up back.

We always had good times.

And you needn’t be afraid of Father being drunk tonight.

He never drinks when he engages to play for a party.

But—there may be—liquor.

What will you do if it gets rough?”  

“Nobody would molest me.”  

“Not seriously, I suppose.

Father would see to that.

But it _might_ be noisy and—and unpleasant.”  

“I won’t mind.

I’m only going as a looker-on.

I don’t expect to dance.

I just want to _see_

what a party up back is like.

I’ve never seen anything except decorous Deerwood.”  

Cissy smiled rather dubiously.

She knew much better than Valancy what a party “up back” might be like if there should be liquor.

But again there mightn’t be.  

“I hope you’ll enjoy it, dear,” she repeated.  

Valancy enjoyed the drive there.

It wound  [wound - рана] through beautiful, purring pines  [pines - сосны] that were ranks of enchantment in the June sunset, and over the curious  [curious - любопытный] jade-green rivers of Muskoka, fringed by aspens that were always quivering with some supernal joy.  

Roaring  [Roaring - Ревущий] Abel was excellent company, too.

He knew all the stories and legends  [legends - легенды] of the wild, beautiful “up back,” and he told them to Valancy as they drove along.

Valancy had several fits  [fits - припадки] of inward laughter over what Uncle Benjamin and Aunt Wellington, _et al._, would feel and think and say if they saw her driving with Roaring  [Roaring - Ревущий] Abel in that terrible buggy  [buggy - багги] to a dance at Chidley Corners.  

At first the dance was quiet enough, and Valancy was amused  [amused - позабавленный] and entertained.

She even danced twice herself, with a couple of nice “up back” boys who danced beautifully and told her she did, too.  

Another compliment came her way—not a very subtle  [subtle - тонкий] one, perhaps, but Valancy had had too few compliments in her life to be over-nice on that point.

She overheard  [overheard - подслушанный] two of the “up back” young men talking about her in the dark “lean-to” behind her.  

“Know who that girl in green is?”  “Nope.

Guess she’s from out front.

The Port, maybe.

Got a stylish look to her.”  

“No beaut but cute- [cute - милый] looking, I’ll say.

‘Jever see such eyes?”  

The big room was decorated  [decorated - украшенный] with pine  [pine - сосна] and fir boughs, and lighted by Chinese lanterns.

The “up back” girls were pretty and prettily dressed.

Valancy thought it the nicest party she had ever attended.  

By eleven o’clock she had changed her mind.

A new crowd had arrived—a crowd unmistakably  [unmistakably - безошибочно] drunk.

Whiskey  [Whiskey - Виски] began to circulate  [circulate - циркулировать] freely. [freely - свободно] 

Very soon almost all the men were partly drunk.

Those in the porch  [porch - крыльцо] and outside around the door began howling “come-all-ye’s” and continued to howl them.

The room grew noisy and reeking.

Quarrels  [Quarrels - Ссоры] started up here and there.

Bad language and obscene songs were heard.

The girls, swung  [swung - замахнулись] rudely in the dances, became dishevelled and tawdry.

Valancy, alone in her corner, was feeling disgusted  [disgusted - отвращение] and repentant.

Why had she ever come to such a place?

Freedom and independence  [independence - независимость] were all very well, but one should not be a little fool. [fool - дурачок] 

She might have known what it would be like—she might have taken warning from Cissy’s guarded  [guarded - стерег] sentences.

Her head was aching— [aching - болит] she was sick of the whole thing.

But what could she do?

She must stay to the end.

Abel could not leave till then.

And that would probably be not till three or four in the morning.  

The new influx of boys had left the girls far in the minority and partners were scarce. [scarce - скудно] 

Valancy was pestered with invitations to dance.

She refused them all shortly, [shortly - коротко]  and some of her refusals  [refusals - отказы] were not well taken.

Across the room she saw a group of the strangers talking together and glancing meaningly at her.

What were they plotting?  

It was at this moment that she saw Barney Snaith looking in over the heads of the crowd at the doorway. [doorway - дверной проем] 

Valancy had two distinct  [distinct - отчетливые] convictions— [convictions - убеждения] one was that she was quite safe now; the other was that _this_ was why she had wanted to come to the dance.

It had been such an absurd  [absurd - абсурдные] hope that she had not recognised it before, but now she knew she had come because of the possibility that Barney might be there, too.

She thought that perhaps she ought to be ashamed for this, but she wasn’t.

After her feeling of relief  [relief - облегчение] her next feeling was one of annoyance with Barney for coming there unshaved.

Surely he might have enough self-respect to groom himself up decently when he went to a party.

There he was, bareheaded, bristly-chinned, in his old trousers and his blue homespun shirt.

Not even a coat.

Valancy could have shaken him in her anger.

No wonder people believed everything bad of him.  

But she was not afraid any longer.

One of the whispering group left his comrades and came across the room to her, through the whirling couples that now filled it uncomfortably. [uncomfortably - неудобно] 

He was a tall, broad-shouldered fellow, not ill-dressed or ill-looking but unmistakably  [unmistakably - безошибочно] half drunk.

He asked Valancy to dance.

Valancy declined  [declined - отказался] civilly.

His face turned livid.

He threw his arm about her and pulled her to him.

His hot, whiskied breath burned her face.  

“We won’t have fine-lady airs here, my girl.

If you ain’t too good to come here you ain’t too good to dance with us.

Me and my pals have been watching you.

You’ve got to give us each a turn and a kiss to boot.”  

Valancy tried desperately  [desperately - отчаянно] and vainly to free  [free - свободный] herself.

She was being dragged out into the maze of shouting, stamping, yelling  [yelling - орущий] dancers.

The next moment the man who held her went staggering  [staggering - пошатываясь] across the room from a neatly  [neatly - аккуратно] planted blow  [blow - удар] on the jaw, [jaw - челюсть]  knocking down whirling couples as he went.

Valancy felt her arm grasped.  

“This way—quick,” said Barney Snaith.

Suppose she did!

What a scandal  [scandal - скандал] it would make for her poor people.

For the first time Valancy felt a little sorry for them.

Also, she felt glad that she had escaped from that horrible row.

Also, glad that she was holding tight  [tight - туго] to Barney’s hand.

Her feelings were badly mixed and she had never had so many in such a brief time in her life.  

They finally reached a quiet corner in the pine woods.

The pursuit  [pursuit - преследование] had taken a different direction and the whoops and yells  [yells - вопит] behind them were growing faint.

Valancy, out of breath, with a crazily  [crazily - безумно] beating heart, collapsed  [collapsed - рухнул] on the trunk  [trunk - ствол] of a fallen pine. [pine - сосна]   

“Thanks,” she gasped.  

“What a goose  [goose - гусь] you were to come to such a place!” said Barney.  

“I—didn’t—know—it—would—be like this,” protested  [protested - протестовал] Valancy.  

“You _should_ have known.

Chidley Corners!”  

“It—was—just—a name—to me.”  

Valancy knew Barney could not realise how ignorant  [ignorant - невежественный] she was of the regions “up back.”

She had lived in Deerwood all her life and of course he supposed she knew.

He didn’t know how she had been brought up.

There was no use trying to explain.  

“When I drifted  [drifted - дрейфовал] in at Abel’s this evening and Cissy told me you’d come here I was amazed.

And downright scared.

Cissy told me she was worried about you but hadn’t liked to say anything to dissuade you for fear you’d think she was thinking selfishly about herself.

So I came on up here instead of going to Deerwood.”  

Valancy felt a sudden delightful glow  [glow - светиться] irradiating soul and body under the dark pines. [pines - сосны] 

So he had actually come up to look after her.  

“As soon as they stop hunting for us we’ll sneak  [sneak - подкрасться] around to the Muskoka road.

I left Lady Jane down there.

I’ll take you home.

I suppose you’ve had enough of your party.”  

“Quite,” said Valancy meekly.

The first half of the way home neither of them said anything.

It would not have been much use.

Lady Jane made so much noise they could not have heard each other.

By Barney Snaith, reputed  [reputed - прославленный] jail-breaker, infidel, forger  [forger - фальсификатор] and defaulter.

Valancy’s lips twitched  [twitched - дергался] in the darkness  [darkness - мрак] as she thought of it.

But she _was_ ashamed.  

And yet she was enjoying herself—was full of a strange exultation—bumping over that rough road beside Barney Snaith.

The big trees shot by them.

The tall mulleins stood up along the road in stiff, [stiff - жесткий]  orderly ranks like companies of soldiers.

The thistles looked like drunken  [drunken - пьяный] fairies or tipsy elves as their car-lights passed over them.

This was the first time she had even been in a car.

After all, she liked it.

She was not in the least afraid, with Barney at the wheel.

Her spirits rose rapidly as they tore along.

She ceased  [ceased - прекратил] to feel ashamed.

She ceased  [ceased - прекратил] to feel anything except that she was part of a comet rushing gloriously through the night of space.  

All at once, just where the pine woods frayed out to the scrub barrens, Lady Jane became quiet—too quiet.

Lady Jane slowed down quietly—and stopped.  

Barney uttered  [uttered - произнес] an aghast exclamation. [exclamation - восклицание] 

Got out.

Investigated. [Investigated - расследовал] 

Came apologetically  [apologetically - извиняясь] back.  

“I’m a doddering idiot. [idiot - идиот] 

Out of gas.

I knew I was short when I left home, but I meant to fill up in Deerwood.

Then I forgot all about it in my hurry to get to the Corners.”  

“What can we do?” asked Valancy coolly.  

“I don’t know.

There’s no gas nearer than Deerwood, nine miles away.

And I don’t dare  [dare - смел] leave you here alone.

There were boys there from the Port.

As far as I can see, the best thing to do is for us just to sit patiently  [patiently - терпеливо] here until some car comes along and lends us enough gas to get to Roaring  [Roaring - Ревущий] Abel’s with.”  

“Well, what’s the matter with that?” said Valancy.  

“We may have to sit here all night,” said Barney.  

“I don’t mind,” said Valancy.  

Barney gave a short laugh.

“If you don’t, I needn’t.

I haven’t any reputation to lose.”  

“Nor I,” said Valancy comfortably. [comfortably - удобно]      

CHAPTER XXI   

“We’ll just sit here,” said Barney, “and if we think of anything worth while saying we’ll say it.

Otherwise, not.

Don’t imagine you’re bound  [bound - связанный] to talk to me.”  

“John Foster says,” quoted  [quoted - цитируемый] Valancy, “‘If you can sit in silence with a person for half an hour and yet be entirely comfortable, you and that person can be friends.

If you cannot, friends you’ll never be and you need not waste time in trying.’”  “Evidently  [Evidently - Очевидно] John Foster says a sensible  [sensible - разумный] thing once in a while,” conceded  [conceded - уступивший] Barney.  

They sat in silence for a long while.

Little rabbits hopped across the road.

Once or twice an owl  [owl - сова] laughed out delightfully.

The road beyond them was fringed with the woven shadow lace  [lace - кружево] of trees.

Away off to the southwest the sky was full of silvery little cirrus clouds above the spot where Barney’s island must be.  

Valancy was perfectly happy.

Some things dawn on you slowly.

Some things come by lightning flashes. [flashes - вспышки] 

Valancy had had a lightning flash. [flash - вспышка]   

She knew quite well now that she loved Barney.

Yesterday she had been all her own.

Now she was this man’s.

Yet he had done nothing—said nothing.

He had not even looked at her as a woman.

But that didn’t matter.

Nor did it matter what he was or what he had done.

She loved him without any reservations.

Everything in her went out wholly  [wholly - полностью] to him.

She had no wish to stifle or disown her love.

She seemed to be his so absolutely that thought apart from him—thought in which he did not predominate—was an impossibility. [impossibility - невозможность]   

She had realised, quite simply and fully, that she loved him, in the moment when he was leaning  [leaning - опираясь] on the car door, explaining that Lady Jane had no gas.

Love had cast  [cast - литье] out her last fear.  

Love!

What a searing, torturing, [torturing - мучительное]  intolerably sweet thing it was—this possession of body, soul and mind!

With something at its core  [core - ядро] as fine and remote and purely  [purely - чисто] spiritual as the tiny blue spark  [spark - искра] in the heart of the unbreakable diamond.

No dream had ever been like this.

She was no longer solitary.

She was one of a vast sisterhood—all the women who had ever loved in the world.  

Barney need  [need - нужно] never know it—though she would not in the least have minded his knowing.

But _she_ knew it and it made a tremendous  [tremendous - потрясающе] difference to her.

Just to love!

She did not ask to be loved.

It was rapture enough just to sit there beside him in silence, alone in the summer night in the white splendour of moonshine, with the wind blowing down on them out of the pine woods.

She had always envied  [envied - завидно] the wind.

So free.

Blowing where it listed. [listed - перечислено] 

Through the hills.

Over the lakes.

What a tang, what a zip  [zip - zip] it had!

What a magic of adventure!

Valancy felt as if she had exchanged her shop-worn soul for a fresh one, fire-new from the workshop  [workshop - мастерская] of the gods. [gods - боги] 

As far back as she could look, life had been dull— [dull - уныло] colourless— [colourless - бесцветный] savourless.

Now she had come to a little patch  [patch - патч] of violets, purple and fragrant—hers for the plucking.

No matter who or what had been in Barney’s past—no matter who or what might be in his future—no one else could ever have this perfect hour.

“Ever dream of ballooning?” said Barney suddenly.  

“No,” said Valancy.  

“I do—often.

Dream of sailing  [sailing - парусный спорт] through the clouds—seeing the glories  [glories - слава] of sunset—spending hours in the midst  [midst - посреди] of a terrific  [terrific - потрясающий] storm with lightning playing above and below you—skimming  [skimming - снятие] above a silver cloud floor under a full moon—wonderful!”  

“It does sound so,” said Valancy.

“I’ve stayed on earth in my dreams.”  

She told him about her Blue Castle.

It was so easy to tell Barney things.

One felt he understood everything—even the things you didn’t tell him.

And then she told him a little of her existence before she came to Roaring  [Roaring - ревущий] Abel’s.

She wanted him to see why she had gone to the dance “up back.”  

“You see—I’ve never had any real life,” she said.

“I’ve just—breathed.

Every door has always been shut to me.”  

“But you’re still young,” said Barney.  

“Oh, I know.

Yes, I’m ‘still young’—but

that’s so different from _young_,” said Valancy bitterly. [bitterly - горько] 

For a moment she was tempted  [tempted - соблазненный] to tell Barney why her years had nothing to do with her future; but she did not.

She was not going to think of death tonight.  

“Though I never was really young,” she went on—“until tonight,” she added in her heart.

“I never had a life like other girls.

You couldn’t understand.

Why,”—she had a desperate desire that Barney should know the worst about her—“I didn’t even love my mother.

Isn’t it awful that I don’t love my mother?”  

“Rather awful—for her,” said Barney drily.  

“Oh, she didn’t know it.

She took my love for granted.

And I wasn’t any use or comfort to her or anybody.

I was just a—a—vegetable.

And I got tired of it.

That’s why I came to keep house for Mr. Gay and look after Cissy.”  

“And I suppose your people thought you’d gone mad.”  

“They did—and do—literally, [literally - буквально] ” said Valancy.

“But it’s a comfort to them.

They’d rather believe me mad than bad.

There’s no other alternative.

But I’ve been _living_ since I came to Mr. Gay’s.

It’s been a delightful experience.

I suppose I’ll pay for it when I have to go back—but I’ll have _had_ it.”  

“That’s true,” said Barney.

“If you buy your experience it’s your own.

So it’s no matter how much you pay for it.

Somebody else’s experience can never be yours.

Well, it’s a funny old world.”  

“Do you think it really is old?” asked Valancy dreamily. [dreamily - мечтательно] 

“I never believe _that_ in June.

It seems so young tonight—somehow.

In that quivering moonlight— [moonlight - лунный свет] like a young, white girl—waiting.”  

“Moonlight  [moonlight - лунный свет] here on the verge  [verge - грань] of up back is different from moonlight anywhere else,” agreed Barney.

“It always makes me feel so clean, somehow—body and soul.

And of course the age of gold always comes back in spring.”  

It was ten o’clock now.

A dragon of black cloud ate up the moon.

The spring air grew chill— [chill - холод] Valancy shivered.

“No.

I’m not going to have you catching cold on my hands.”  

“Oh, I won’t catch cold.

I haven’t had a cold since I came to Mr. Gay’s—though I’ve done the foolishest things.

It’s funny, too—I used to have them all the time.

I feel so selfish taking your coat.”  

“You’ve sneezed  [sneezed - чихнул] three times.

No use winding  [winding - обмотка] up your ‘experience’ up back with grippe or pneumonia. [pneumonia - пневмония] ”  

He pulled it up tight  [tight - туго] about her throat  [throat - горло] and buttoned it on her.

Valancy submitted  [submitted - подчиненный] with secret delight.

How nice it was to have some one look after you so!

She snuggled down into the tobaccoey folds and wished the night could last forever.  

Ten minutes later a car swooped down on them from “up back.”

Barney sprang from Lady Jane and waved his hand.

The car came to a stop beside them.

Valancy saw Uncle Wellington and Olive gazing at her in horror from it.  

So Uncle Wellington had got a car!

And he must have been spending the evening up at Mistawis with Cousin Herbert.

Valancy almost laughed aloud at the expression on his face as he recognised her.

The pompous, bewhiskered old humbug!  

“Can you let me have enough gas to take me to Deerwood?”

Barney was asking politely.

But Uncle Wellington was not attending to him.  

“Valancy, how came you _here_!”

he said sternly.  

“By chance or God’s grace, [grace - грация] ” said Valancy.  

“With this jail-bird—at ten o’clock at night!” said Uncle Wellington.  

Valancy turned to Barney.

The moon had escaped from its dragon and in its light her eyes were full of deviltry.  

“_Are_ you a jail-bird?”  “Does it matter?” said Barney, gleams  [gleams - блестки] of fun in _his_ eyes.  

“Not to me.

I only asked out of curiosity, [curiosity - любопытство] ” continued Valancy.  

“Then I won’t tell you.

I never satisfy curiosity. [curiosity - любопытство] ”

He turned to Uncle Wellington and his voice changed subtly. [subtly - тонко]   

“Mr. Stirling, I asked you if you could let me have some gas.

If you can, well and good.

If not, we are only delaying you unnecessarily.”  

Uncle Wellington was in a horrible dilemma. [dilemma - дилемма] 

To give gas to this shameless  [shameless - бесстыдство] pair!

But not to give it to them!

To go away and leave them there in the Mistawis woods—until daylight, likely.

It was better to give it to them and let them get out of sight before any one else saw them.  

“Got anything to get gas in?”

he grunted surlily.  

Barney produced a two-gallon  [gallon - галлон] measure from Lady Jane.

He agreed that her mind was deranged and was exceedingly anxious to find out whence the derangement had been inherited. [inherited - унаследованный] 

It was a serious thing to have in the family—a very serious thing.

One had to think of one’s—descendants.  

“She got it from the Wansbarras,” said Olive positively.

“There’s nothing like that in the Stirlings—nothing!”  

“I hope not—I certainly hope not,” Cecil had responded dubiously.

“But then—to go out as a servant— [servant - слуга] for that is what it practically  [practically - практически] amounts to.

Your cousin!”  

Poor Olive felt the implication. [implication - смысл] 

The Port Lawrence Prices were not accustomed  [accustomed - привычный] to ally themselves with families whose members “worked out.”  

Valancy could not resist temptation. [temptation - соблазн] 

She leaned  [leaned - наклоняющийся] forward.  

“Olive, does it hurt?”  

Olive bit—stiffly.  

“Does _what_ hurt?”  

“Looking like that.”  

For a moment Olive resolved she would take no further notice of Valancy.

Then duty came uppermost.

She must not miss the opportunity.  

“Doss,” she implored, leaning  [leaning - наклоняющийся] forward also, “won’t you come home—come home tonight?”  

Valancy yawned. [yawned - зевовший]   

“You sound like a revival  [revival - возрождение] meeting,” she said.

“You really do.”  

“If you will come back——”  “All will be forgiven.”  

“Yes,” said Olive eagerly. [eagerly - жадно] 

Wouldn’t it be splendid if _she_ could induce  [induce - вынуждающий] the prodigal daughter to return?

“We’ll never cast  [cast - бросать] it up to you.

Doss, there are nights when I cannot sleep for thinking of you.”  

“And me having the time of my life,” said Valancy, laughing.  

“Doss, I can’t believe you’re bad.

I’ve always said you couldn’t be bad——”  “I don’t believe I can be,” said Valancy.

“I’m afraid I’m hopelessly  [hopelessly - безнадежно] proper.

I’ve been sitting here for three hours with Barney Snaith and he hasn’t even tried to kiss me.

I wouldn’t have minded if he had, Olive.”  

Valancy was still leaning  [leaning - наклоняющийся] forward.

Her little hat with its crimson rose was tilted down over one eye.

Olive stared.

Olive drew back.

It was beneath  [beneath - ниже] her dignity  [dignity - достоинство] to say more.

After all, Valancy must be both mad _and_ bad.  

“Thanks—that’s enough,” said Barney behind the car.

“Much obliged, Mr. Stirling.

Two gallons— [gallons - галлоны] seventy cents.

Thank you.”  

Uncle Wellington climbed foolishly  [foolishly - глупо] and feebly into his car.

He wanted to give Snaith a piece of his mind, but dared  [dared - осмелился] not.

Who knew what the creature might do if provoked? [provoked - спровоцировал] 

No doubt he carried firearms. [firearms - огнестрельное оружие]   

Uncle Wellington looked indecisively at Valancy.

But Valancy had turned her back on him and was watching Barney pour the gas into Lady Jane’s maw.  

“Drive on,” said Olive decisively. [decisively - решительно] 

“There’s no use in waiting here.

Let me tell you what she said to me.”  

“The little hussy!

The shameless  [shameless - бесстыдно] little hussy!” said Uncle Wellington.     

CHAPTER XXII   

The next thing the Stirlings heard was that Valancy had been seen with Barney Snaith in a movie theatre in Port Lawrence and after it at supper in a Chinese restaurant there.

This was quite true—and no one was more surprised at it than Valancy herself.

Barney had come along in Lady Jane one dim  [dim - тускло] twilight and told Valancy unceremoniously if she wanted a drive to hop in.  

“I’m going to the Port.

Will you go there with me?”  

His eyes were teasing  [teasing - дразнить] and there was a bit of defiance in his voice.

Valancy, who did not conceal  [conceal - скрыть] from herself that she would have gone anywhere with him to any place, “hopped in” without more ado.

They tore into and through Deerwood.

Mrs. Frederick and Cousin Stickles, taking a little air on the verandah, [verandah - веранду]  saw them whirl by in a cloud of dust and sought comfort in each other’s eyes.

Valancy, who in some dim  [dim - тускло] pre-existence had been afraid of a car, was hatless and her hair was blowing wildly round her face.

She would certainly come down with bronchitis—and die at Roaring  [Roaring - ревущий] Abel’s.

“I will not believe,” said Cousin Stickles solemnly, “that our prayers will not yet be answered.”  

“Who—_who_ will protect that unfortunate  [unfortunate - несчастный] girl when I am gone?” moaned Mrs. Frederick.  

As for Valancy, she was wondering if it could really be only a few weeks since she had sat there with them on that verandah. [verandah - веранда] 

Hating the rubber-plant.

Pestered with teasing  [teasing - дразнящий] questions like black flies.

Always thinking of appearances.

Cowed because of Aunt Wellington’s teaspoons  [teaspoons - ложки] and Uncle Benjamin’s money.

Poverty-stricken.

Afraid of everybody.

Envying  [Envying - завидующий] Olive.

A slave to moth-eaten traditions.

Nothing to hope for or expect.  

And now every day was a gay  [gay - веселый] adventure.  

Lady Jane flew over the fifteen miles between Deerwood and the Port—through the Port.

The way Barney went past traffic policemen was not holy.

The lights were beginning to twinkle out like stars in the clear, lemon-hued twilight air.

This was the only time Valancy ever really liked the town, and she was crazy with the delight of speeding.

Was it possible she had ever been afraid of a car?

She was perfectly happy, riding beside Barney.

Not that she deluded herself into thinking it had any significance. [significance - значительность] 

She knew quite well that Barney had asked her to go on the impulse  [impulse - импульс]  [impulse - импульс] of the moment—an impulse born of a feeling of pity  [pity - жалость] for her and her starved  [starved - голодный] little dreams.

She was looking tired after a wakeful night with a heart attack, followed by a busy day.

She had so little fun.

He’d give her an outing  [outing - вылазка] for once.

Besides, Abel was in the kitchen, at the point of drunkenness where he was declaring he did not believe in God and beginning to sing ribald songs.

It was just as well she should be out of the way for a while.

Barney knew Roaring  [Roaring - ревущий] Abel’s repertoire. [repertoire - репертуар]   

They went to the movie—Valancy had never been to a movie.

Mrs. Frederick gave up going to church altogether. [altogether - совсем] 

She could not endure  [endure - терпеть] her friends’ pitying glances and questions.

But Cousin Stickles went every Sunday.

She said they had been given a cross to bear.     

CHAPTER XXIII   

On one of Cissy’s wakeful nights, she told Valancy her poor little story.

They were sitting by the open window.

Cissy could not get her breath lying down that night.

An inglorious gibbous moon was hanging over the wooded  [wooded - лесистой] hills and in its spectral light Cissy looked frail and lovely and incredibly young.

A child.

It did not seem possible that she could have lived through all the passion and pain and shame of her story.  

“He was stopping at the hotel across the lake.

He used to come over in his canoe  [canoe - каноэ] at night—we met in the pines  [pines - сосны] down the shore. [shore - берег] 

He was a young college student—his father was a rich man in Toronto.

Oh, Valancy, I didn’t mean to be bad—I didn’t, indeed.

But I loved him so—I love him yet—I’ll always love him.

And I—didn’t know—some things.

I didn’t—understand.

Then his father came and took him away.

And—after a little—I found out—oh, Valancy,—I was so frightened.

I didn’t know what to do.

I wrote him—and he came.

He—he said he would marry me, Valancy.”  

“And why—and why?——”  “Oh, Valancy, he didn’t love me any more.

I saw that at a glance.

He—he was just offering to marry me because he thought he ought to—because he was sorry for me.

He wasn’t bad—but he was so young—and what was I that he should keep on loving me?”  

“Never mind making excuses for him,” said Valancy a bit shortly. [shortly - ненадолго] 

“So you wouldn’t marry him?”  

“I couldn’t—not when he didn’t love me any more.

Somehow—I can’t explain—it seemed a worse thing to do than—the other.

He—he argued a little—but he went away.

Do you think I did right, Valancy?”  

“Yes, I do.

_

You_ did right.

But he——”  “Don’t blame him, dear.

Please don’t.

Let’s not talk about him at all.

There’s no need. [need - нуждаться] 

I wanted to tell you how it was—I didn’t want you to think me bad——”  “I never did think so.”  

“Yes, I felt that—whenever you came.

Oh, Valancy, what you’ve been to me!

I can never tell you—but God will bless  [bless - благословить] you for it.

I know He will—‘with what measure ye mete.’”  

Cissy sobbed  [sobbed - рыдать] for a few minutes in Valancy’s arms.

Then she wiped  [wiped - вытирать] her eyes.  

“Well, that’s almost all.

I came home.

I wasn’t really so very unhappy.

I suppose I should have been—but I wasn’t.

Father wasn’t hard on me.

And my baby was so sweet while he lived.

I was even happy—I loved him so much, the dear little thing.

He was so sweet, Valancy—with such lovely blue eyes—and little rings of pale gold hair like silk floss—and tiny dimpled hands.

I used to bite his satin-smooth little face all over—softly, so as not to hurt him, you know——”  “I know,” said Valancy, wincing.

“I know—a woman _always_ knows—and dreams——”  “And he was _all_ mine.

Nobody else had any claim on him.

When he died, oh, Valancy, I thought I must die too—I didn’t see how anybody could endure  [endure - терпеть] such anguish  [anguish - тоску] and live.

To see his dear little eyes and know he would never open them again—to miss his warm little body nestled against mine at night and think of him sleeping alone and cold, his wee face under the hard frozen earth.

It was so awful for the first year—after that it was a little easier, one didn’t keep thinking ‘this day last

year’—but I was so glad when I found out I was dying.”  

“‘Who could endure  [endure - терпеть] life if it were not for the hope of death?’” murmured Valancy softly—it was of course a quotation  [quotation - цитировать] from some book of John Foster’s.  

When Valancy saw the change that had come over Cissy’s face she wanted to telephone for the doctor.

But Cissy wouldn’t let her.  

“Valancy, why should you?

He can do nothing for me.

I’ve known for several days that—this—was near.

Let me die in peace, dear—just holding your hand.

Oh, I’m so glad you’re here.

Tell Father good-bye for me.

He’s always been as good to me as he knew how—and Barney.

Somehow, I think that Barney——”  But a spasm of coughing interrupted and exhausted  [exhausted - измученный] her.

She fell asleep when it was over, still holding to Valancy’s hand.

Valancy sat there in the silence.

She was not frightened—or even sorry.

At sunrise Cissy died.

She opened her eyes and looked past Valancy at something—something that made her smile suddenly and happily.

And, smiling, she died.  

Valancy crossed Cissy’s hands on her breast and went to the open window.

In the eastern sky, amid  [amid - среди] the fires of sunrise, an old moon was hanging—as slender and lovely as a new moon.

Valancy had never seen an old, old moon before.

She watched it pale and fade  [fade - увядший] until it paled and faded  [faded - увядший] out of sight in the living  [living - живой] rose of day.

A little pool in the barrens shone in the sunrise like a great golden  [golden - золотой] lily.  

But the world suddenly seemed a colder place to Valancy.

Again nobody needed her.

She was not in the least sorry Cecilia was dead.

She was only sorry for all her suffering  [suffering - страдающий] in life.

But nobody could ever hurt her again.

Valancy had always thought death dreadful. [dreadful - ужасный] 

But Cissy had died so quietly—so pleasantly.

And at the very last—something—had made up to her for everything.

She was lying there now, in her white sleep, looking like a child.

Beautiful!

All the lines of shame and pain gone.  

Roaring  [Roaring - Ревущий] Abel drove in, justifying  [justifying - оправдывающийся] his name.

Valancy went down and told him.

The shock sobered him at once.

He slumped down on the seat of his buggy, [buggy - глючный]  his great head hanging.  

“Cissy dead—Cissy dead,” he said vacantly.

“I didn’t think it would ‘a’ come so soon.

Dead.

She used to run down the lane  [lane - переулок] to meet me with a little white rose stuck in her hair.

Cissy used to be a pretty little girl.

And a good little girl.”  

“She has always been a good little girl,” said Valancy.     

CHAPTER XXIV   Valancy herself made Cissy ready for burial. [burial - похоронный] 

No hands but hers should touch that pitiful, wasted little body.

The old house was spotless on the day of the funeral. [funeral - похоронный] 

Barney Snaith was not there.

He had done all he could to help Valancy before it—he had shrouded the pale Cecilia in white roses  [roses - розы] from the garden—and then had gone back to his island.

But everybody else was there.

All Deerwood and “up back” came.

They forgave Cissy splendidly at last.

Mr. Bradly gave a very beautiful funeral address.

Mr. Bradly was very tactful. [tactful - тактичный] 

He avoided all dubious  [dubious - сомнительный] points and it was plain to be seen he hoped for the best.

Six reputable citizens of Deerwood bore Cecilia Gay to her grave in decorous Deerwood cemetery. [cemetery - кладбище] 

Among them was Uncle Wellington.  

The Stirlings all came to the funeral, [funeral - похороны]  men and women.

They had had a family conclave over it.

Surely now that Cissy Gay was dead Valancy would come home.

She simply could not stay there with Roaring  [Roaring - Ревущие] Abel.

That being the case, the wisest course—decreed Uncle James—was to attend the funeral— [funeral - похороны] legitimise the whole thing, so to speak—show Deerwood that Valancy had really done a most creditable deed  [deed - дело] in going to nurse poor Cecilia Gay and that her family backed her up in it.

Death, the miracle  [miracle - чудо] worker, suddenly made the thing quite respectable.

If Valancy would return to home and decency while public opinion was under its influence all might yet be well.

Society was suddenly forgetting all Cecilia’s wicked  [wicked - злой] doings and remembering what a pretty, modest  [modest - скромный] little thing she had been—“and motherless, [motherless - безматерь]  [motherless - сирота]  you know—motherless!”

It was the psychological  [psychological - психологический] moment—said Uncle James.  

So the Stirlings went to the funeral. [funeral - похороны] 

Even Cousin Gladys’ neuritis allowed her to come.

Cousin Stickles was there, her bonnet dripping all over her face, crying as woefully as if Cissy had been her nearest and dearest.

Funerals  [Funerals - похороны] always brought Cousin Stickles’ “own sad bereavement” back.  

And Uncle Wellington was a pall-bearer.  

This was not—could not be—the girl who had sat all night in the woods with Barney Snaith—who had gone tearing bareheaded through Deerwood and Port Lawrence.

This was the Valancy they knew.

Really, surprisingly capable and efficient.

Perhaps she had always been kept down a bit too much—Amelia really was rather strict—hadn’t had a chance to show what was in her.

So thought the Stirlings.

And Edward Beck, from the Port road, a widower  [widower - вдовец] with a large family who was beginning to take notice, took notice of Valancy and thought she might make a mighty  [mighty - могучий] fine second wife.

No beauty—but a fifty-year-old widower, [widower - вдовец]  Mr. Beck told himself very reasonably, [reasonably - разумно]  couldn’t expect everything.

Altogether, [Altogether - Вместе]  it seemed that Valancy’s matrimonial chances were never so bright as they were at Cecilia Gay’s funeral. [funeral - похоронный]   

What the Stirlings and Edward Beck would have thought had they known the back of Valancy’s mind must be left to the imagination.

Valancy was hating the funeral— [funeral - похоронный] hating the people who came to stare with curiosity  [curiosity - любопытство] at Cecilia’s marble-white face—hating the smugness—hating the dragging, melancholy singing—hating Mr. Bradly’s cautious  [cautious - осторожный] platitudes.

If she could have had her absurd  [absurd - абсурдный] way, there would have been no funeral  [funeral - похоронный] at all.

She would have covered Cissy over with flowers, shut her away from prying eyes, and buried her beside her nameless  [nameless - безымянный] little baby in the grassy burying-ground under the pines  [pines - сосны] of the “up back” church, with a bit of kindly prayer from the old Free Methodist minister. [minister - министр] 

She remembered Cissy saying once, “I wish I could be buried deep in the heart of the woods where nobody would ever come to say, ‘Cissy Gay is buried here,’ and tell over my miserable  [miserable - жалкий] story.”  

But this!

However, it would soon be over.

Valancy knew, if the Stirlings and Edward Beck didn’t, exactly what she intended to do then.

“My child,” she said tremulously, “you’ll come home _now_?”  

“Home,” said Valancy absently.

She was getting on an apron and calculating how much tea she must put to steep for supper.

There would be several guests from “up back”—distant relatives of the Gays’ who had not remembered them for years.

And she was so tired she wished she could borrow a pair of legs from the cat.  

“Yes, home,” said Mrs. Frederick, with a touch of asperity.

“I suppose you won’t dream of staying here _

now_—alone

with Roaring Abel.”  

“Oh, no, I’m not going to stay _here_,” said Valancy.

“Of course, I’ll have to stay for a day or two, to put the house in order generally.

But that will be all.

Excuse me, Mother, won’t you?

I’ve a frightful lot to do—all those “up back” people will be here to supper.”  

Mrs. Frederick retreated  [retreated - отступивший] in considerable  [considerable - значительный] relief, [relief - облегчение]  and the Stirlings went home with lighter hearts.  

“We will just treat  [treat - угощение] her as if nothing had happened when she comes back,” decreed Uncle Benjamin.

“That will be the best plan.

Just as if nothing had happened.”     

CHAPTER XXV   

On the evening of the day after the funeral  [funeral - похороны] Roaring Abel went off for a spree.

He had been sober  [sober - трезвый] for four whole days and could endure  [endure - терпение] it no longer.

Before he went, Valancy told him she would be going away the next day.

Roaring  [Roaring - Ревущий] Abel was sorry, and said so.

A distant  [distant - далекий] cousin from “up back” was coming to keep house for him—quite willing  [willing - желающий] to do so now since there was no sick girl to wait on—but Abel was not under any delusions concerning  [concerning - относительно] her.  

“She won’t be like you, my girl.

Well, I’m obliged  [obliged - обязанный] to you.

You helped me out of a bad hole and I won’t forget it.

And I won’t forget what you did for Cissy.

I’m your friend, and if you ever want any of the Stirlings spanked and sot in a corner send for me.

I’m going to wet  [wet - мокрый] my whistle.

Lord, but I’m dry!

Don’t reckon  [reckon - считающийся] I’ll be back afore tomorrow night, so if you’re going home tomorrow, good-bye now.”  

“I _may_ go home tomorrow,” said Valancy, “but I’m not going back to Deerwood.”  

“Not going——”  “You’ll find the key on the woodshed nail,” interrupted Valancy, politely and unmistakably. [unmistakably - безошибочно] 

Don’t forget to feed her till your cousin comes.

The pantry is full and I made bread and pies today.

Good-bye, Mr. Gay.

You have been very kind to me and I appreciate it.”  

“We’ve had a d——d decent  [decent - достойный] time of it together, and that’s a fact,” said Roaring  [Roaring - Ревущий] Abel.

“You’re the best small sport in the world, and your little finger is worth the whole Stirling clan  [clan - клан] tied together.

Good-bye and good-luck.”  

Valancy went out to the garden.

Her legs trembled a little, but otherwise she felt and looked composed. [composed - составленный] 

She held something tightly in her hand.

The garden was lying in the magic of the warm, odorous July twilight.

A few stars were out and the robins were calling through the velvety silences of the barrens.

Valancy stood by the gate expectantly.

Would he come?

If he did not——  He was coming.

Valancy heard Lady Jane Grey far back in the woods.

Her breath came a little more quickly.

Nearer—and nearer—she could see Lady Jane now—bumping down the lane— [lane - переулок] nearer—nearer—he was there—he had sprung from the car and was leaning  [leaning - наклонный] over the gate, looking at her.  

“Going home, Miss Stirling?”  “I don’t know—yet,” said Valancy slowly.

Her mind was made up, with no shadow of turning, but the moment was very tremendous. [tremendous - огромный]   

“I thought I’d run down and ask if there was anything I could do for you,” said Barney.  

Valancy took it with a canter.  

“Yes, there is something you can do for me,” she said, evenly and distinctly.

“Will you marry me?”  

For a moment Barney was silent.

There was no particular  [particular - особый] expression on his face.

Then he gave an odd  [odd - нечетный] laugh.  

“Come, now!
