I knew luck was just waiting around the corner for me.

All the signs have been pointing that way today.”  

“Wait.”

Valancy lifted her hand.

“I’m in earnest—but I want to get my breath after that question.

Of course, with my bringing up, I realise perfectly well that this is one of the things ‘a lady should not do.’”  “But why—why?”  

“For two reasons.”

Valancy was still a little breathless, but she looked Barney straight in the eyes, while all the dead Stirlings revolved  [revolved - вращающийся] rapidly in their graves and the living ones did nothing because they did not know that Valancy was at that moment proposing lawful marriage to the notorious  [notorious - пресловутый] Barney Snaith.

“The first reason is, I—I”—Valancy tried to say “I love you” but could not.

She had to take refuge  [refuge - убежище] in a pretended  [pretended - притворный] flippancy.

“I’m crazy about you.

The second is—this.”  

She handed him Dr. Trent’s letter.  

Barney opened it with the air of a man thankful to find some safe, sane  [sane - нормальный] thing to do.

As he read it his face changed.

He understood—more perhaps than Valancy wanted him to.  

“Are you sure nothing can be done for you?”  

Valancy did not misunderstand  [misunderstand - непонимание] the question.  

“Yes.

“You don’t know anything about me.

I may be a—murderer.”  

“No, I don’t.

You _may_ be something dreadful. [dreadful - ужасно] 

Everything they say of you may be true.

But it doesn’t matter to me.”  

“You care that much for me, Valancy?” said Barney incredulously, looking away from the star and into her eyes—her strange, mysterious eyes.  

“I care—that much,” said Valancy in a low voice.

She was trembling.

He had called her by her name for the first time.

It was sweeter than another man’s caress could have been just to hear him say her name like that.  

“If we are going to get married,” said Barney, speaking suddenly in a casual, [casual - небрежно]  matter-of-fact voice, “some things must be understood.”  

“Everything must be understood,” said Valancy.  

“I have things I want to hide,” said Barney coolly.

“You are not to ask me about them.”  

“I won’t,” said Valancy.  

“You must never ask to see my mail.”  

“Never.”  “And we are never to pretend anything to each other.”  

“We won’t,” said Valancy.

“You won’t even have to pretend you like me.

If you marry me I know you’re only doing it out of pity. [pity - жалость] ”  

“And we’ll never tell a lie to each other about anything—a big lie or a petty  [petty - мелко] lie.”  

“Especially a petty  [petty - мелко] lie,” agreed Valancy.  

“And you’ll have to live back on my island.

I won’t live anywhere else.”  

“That’s partly why I want to marry you,” said Valancy.  

Barney peered at her.  

“I believe you mean it.

Well—let’s get married, then.”  

“Thank you,” said Valancy, with a sudden return of primness.

She would have been much less embarrassed if he had refused her.  

“I suppose I haven’t any right to make conditions.

But I’m going to make one.

You are never to refer to my heart or my liability to sudden death.

You are never to urge  [urge - побуждение] me to be careful.

You are to forget—absolutely forget—that I’m not perfectly healthy.

I have written a letter to my mother—here it is—you are to keep it.

I have explained everything in it.

If I drop dead suddenly—as I likely will do——”  “It will exonerate me in the eyes of your kindred from the suspicion  [suspicion - подозрение] of having poisoned  [poisoned - отравленно] you,” said Barney with a grin. [grin - ухмылка]   

“Exactly.”

Valancy laughed gaily.

“Dear me, I’m glad this is over.

It has been—a bit of an ordeal.

You see, I’m not in the habit of going about asking men to marry me.

It is so nice of you not to refuse me—or offer to be a brother!”  

“I’ll go to the Port tomorrow and get a license.

We can be married tomorrow evening.

Dr. Stalling, I suppose?”  “Heavens, no.”

Valancy shuddered.

“Besides, he wouldn’t do it.

He’d shake his forefinger at me and I’d jilt you at the altar. [altar - алтарь] 

No, I want my old Mr. Towers to marry me.”  

“Will you marry me as I stand?” demanded Barney.

A passing car, full of tourists, honked  [honked - сигналил] loudly—it seemed derisively.

Valancy looked at him.

Blue homespun shirt, nondescript  [nondescript - невзрачно] hat, muddy  [muddy - мутно] overalls.

Unshaved!  

“Yes,” she said.  

Barney put his hands over the gate and took her little, cold ones gently  [gently - нежно] in his.  

“Valancy,” he said, trying to speak lightly, “of course I’m not in love with you—never thought of such a thing as being in love.

But, do you know, I’ve always thought you were a bit of a dear. [dear - дорогой] ”     

CHAPTER XXVI   

The next day passed for Valancy like a dream.

She could not make herself or anything she did seem real.

She saw nothing of Barney, though she expected he must go rattling past on his way to the Port for a license.  

Perhaps he had changed his mind.  

Valancy was waiting at the gate for her bridegroom.

She wore her green dress and her green hat because she had nothing else to wear.

She did not look or feel at all bride-like—she really looked like a wild elf strayed  [strayed - заблудший] out of the greenwood.

But that did not matter.

Nothing at all mattered except that Barney was coming for her.  

“Ready?” said Barney, stopping Lady Jane with some new, horrible noises.  

“Yes.” Valancy stepped in and sat down.

Barney was in his blue shirt and overalls.

But they were clean overalls.

He was smoking a villainous-looking pipe and he was bareheaded.

But he had a pair of oddly  [oddly - странно] smart boots on under his shabby  [shabby - потрепанный] overalls.

And he was shaved.

They clattered into Deerwood and through Deerwood and hit the long, wooded  [wooded - лесистый] road to the Port.  

“Haven’t changed your mind?” said Barney.  

“No.

Have you?”  

“No.”  

That was their whole conversation on the fifteen miles.

Everything was more dream-like than ever.

Valancy didn’t know whether she felt happy.

Or terrified. [terrified - испуганный] 

Or just plain fool. [fool - дурак]   

Then the lights of Port Lawrence were about them.

Valancy felt as if she were surrounded by the gleaming, hungry eyes of hundreds of great, stealthy panthers.

Barney briefly  [briefly - кратко]  [briefly - кратко] asked where Mr. Towers lived, and Valancy as briefly told him.

They stopped before the shabby  [shabby - потертый] little house in an unfashionable  [unfashionable - немодный] street.

They went in to the small, shabby  [shabby - потертый] parlour. [parlour - гостиная] 

Barney produced his license.

So he _had_ got it.

Also a ring.

This thing was real.

She, Valancy Stirling, was actually on the point of being married.  

They were standing up together before Mr. Towers.

Valancy heard Mr. Towers and Barney saying things.

She heard some other person saying things.

She herself was thinking of the way she had once planned to be married—away back in her early teens when such a thing had not seemed impossible.

White silk and tulle veil and orange-blossoms; [blossoms - цветы]  no bridesmaid.

Valancy lifted her eyes and saw herself and Barney in the little, slanting, distorting  [distorting - искажающий] mirror over the mantelpiece.

She in her odd, [odd - странный]  unbridal green hat and dress; Barney in shirt and overalls.

But it was Barney.

That was all that mattered.

No veil—no flowers—no guests—no presents—no wedding-cake—but just Barney.

For all the rest of her life there would be Barney.  

“Mrs. Snaith, I hope you will be very happy,” Mr. Towers was saying.  

He had not seemed surprised at their appearance—not even at Barney’s overalls.

He had seen plenty of queer  [queer - странный] weddings “up back.”

He did not know Valancy was one of the Deerwood Stirlings—he did not even know there _were_

Deerwood Stirlings.

He did not know Barney Snaith was a fugitive from justice.

Really, he was an incredibly ignorant  [ignorant - невежественный] old man.

Therefore he married them and gave them his blessing  [blessing - благословение] very gently  [gently - нежно] and solemnly and prayed for them that night after they had gone away.

His conscience  [conscience - совесть] did not trouble him at all.  

“What a nice way to get married!”

Barney was saying as he put Lady Jane in gear. [gear - снаряжение] 

“No fuss and flub-dub.

I never supposed it was half so easy.”  

“For heaven’s sake, [sake - ради] ” said Valancy suddenly, “let’s forget we _are_ married and talk as if we weren’t.

I can’t stand another drive like the one we had coming in.”  

Barney howled and threw Lady Jane into high with an infernal noise.  

“And I thought I was making it easy for you,” he said.

“You didn’t seem to want to talk.”  

“I didn’t.

But I wanted you to talk.

I don’t want you to make love to me, but I want you to act like an ordinary human being.

Tell me about this island of yours.

What sort of a place is it?”  

“The jolliest place in the world.

You’re going to love it.

The first time I saw it I loved it.

Old Tom MacMurray owned it then.

He built the little shack  [shack - лачуга] on it, lived there in winter and rented it to Toronto people in summer.

I bought it from him—became by that one simple transaction  [transaction - транзакция] a landed proprietor  [proprietor - владелец] owning a house and an island.

There is something so satisfying in owning a whole island.

And isn’t an uninhabited island a charming  [charming - очаровательный] idea?

I’d wanted to own one ever since I’d read _Robinson Crusoe_.

It seemed too good to be true.

And beauty!

Most of the scenery belongs to the government, but they don’t tax you for looking at it, and the moon belongs to everybody.

You won’t find my shack  [shack - хижина] very tidy.

I suppose you’ll want to make it tidy.”  

“Yes,” said Valancy honestly.

“I _have_ to be tidy.

I don’t really _want_ to be.

But untidiness hurts me.

Yes, I’ll have to tidy up your shack. [shack - лачуга] ”  

“No, you’ll only sweep  [sweep - подметать] up after me with the air of a martyr,” said Barney.

“Well, anyway, you can’t tidy the lean-to.

You can’t even enter it.

The door will be locked and I shall keep the key.”  

“Bluebeard’s chamber, [chamber - камера] ” said Valancy.

“I shan’t even think of it.

I don’t care how many wives you have hanging up in it.

So long as they’re really dead.”  “Dead as door-nails.

You can do as you like in the rest of the house.

There’s not much of it—just one big living- [living - жилой] room and one small bedroom.

Well built, though.

Old Tom loved his job.

The beams  [beams - балки] of our house are cedar and the rafters  [rafters - стропила] fir.

Our living- [living - жилой] room windows face west and east.

It’s wonderful to have a room where you can see both sunrise and sunset.

I have two cats there.

Banjo and Good Luck.

Adorable animals.

Banjo is a big, enchanting, [enchanting - феерический]  grey devil- [devil - черт] cat.

Striped, of course.

I don’t care a hang  [hang - вешать] for any cat that hasn’t stripes.

I never knew a cat who could swear as genteelly and effectively  [effectively - эффектно] as Banjo.

His only fault is that he snores  [snores - храпит] horribly when he is asleep.

Luck is a dainty little cat.

Always looking wistfully at you, as if he wanted to tell you something.

Maybe he will pull it off sometime.

Once in a thousand years, you know, one cat is allowed to speak.

My cats are philosophers— [philosophers - философы] neither of them ever cries over spilt milk.  

“Two old crows live in a pine- [pine - сосна] tree on the point and are reasonably  [reasonably - разумно] neighbourly. [neighbourly - по-соседски] 

Call ’em

Nip and Tuck.

Name, Leander.

I brought him up from a baby and he lives over on the mainland  [mainland - материк] and chuckles to himself o’ nights.

And bats— [bats - летучие мыши]  [bats - летучие мыши] it’s a great place for bats at night.

Scared of bats? [bats - летучие мыши] ”  

“No; I like them.”  

“So do I. Nice, queer, [queer - странный]  uncanny, mysterious creatures.

Coming from nowhere—going nowhere.

Swoop!

Banjo likes ’em, too.

Eats ’em.

I have a canoe  [canoe - каноэ] and a disappearing propeller boat.

Went to the Port in it today to get my license.

Quieter than Lady Jane.”  

“I thought you hadn’t gone at all—that you _had_ changed your mind,” admitted Valancy.  

Barney laughed—the laugh Valancy did not like—the little, bitter, cynical  [cynical - циничный] laugh.  

“I never change my mind,” he said shortly. [shortly - коротко]   

They went back through Deerwood.

Up the Muskoka road.

Past Roaring  [Roaring - ревущий] Abel’s.

Over the rocky, [rocky - каменистый]  daisied lane. [lane - переулок] 

The dark pine  [pine - сосна] woods swallowed  [swallowed - проглотил] them up.

Through the pine woods, where the air was sweet with the incense  [incense - ладан] of the unseen, fragile  [fragile - хрупкий] bells of the linnæas that carpeted the banks of the trail.

Through it the two enormous pine- [pine - сосна] trees that clasped hands over Barney’s shack  [shack - хижина] loomed  [loomed - вырисовывалась] out like dark turrets.

Behind them was a sky still rose-hued in the afterlight, and a pale young moon.  

Valancy shivered like a tree the wind stirs  [stirs - шевелится] suddenly.

Something seemed to sweep  [sweep - подметать] over her soul.  

“My Blue Castle!”

she said.

“Oh, my Blue Castle!”  

They got into the canoe  [canoe - каноэ] and paddled  [paddled - грести] out to it.

They left behind the realm  [realm - царство]  [realm - царство] of everyday and things known and landed on a realm of mystery and enchantment where anything might happen—anything might be true.

Barney lifted Valancy out of the canoe  [canoe - каноэ] and swung  [swung - качались] her to a lichen-covered rock under a young pine- [pine - сосна] tree.

His arms were about her and suddenly his lips were on hers.

Valancy found herself shivering with the rapture of her first kiss.  

“Welcome home, dear,” Barney was saying.     

CHAPTER XXVII   Cousin Georgiana came down the lane  [lane - переулок] leading up to her little house.

She lived half a mile out of Deerwood and she wanted to go in to Amelia’s and find out if Doss had come home yet.

Cousin Georgiana was anxious to see Doss.

She had something very important to tell her.

Something, she was sure, Doss would be delighted to hear.

Poor Doss!

She _had_ had rather a dull  [dull - тупой] life of it.

Cousin Georgiana owned to herself that _she_ would not like to live under Amelia’s thumb. [thumb - большой палец] 

But that would be all changed now.

Talk about luck.

Cousin Georgiana would have a chance to impart  [impart - передавать] her wonderful secret right away, with nobody else about to interrupt.

It was, you might say, a Providence.  

Valancy, who had been living for four days on her enchanted island, had decided that she might as well go in to Deerwood and tell her relatives that she was married.

Otherwise, finding that she had disappeared from Roaring  [Roaring - Ревущий] Abel’s, they might get out a search warrant for her.

Barney had offered to drive her in, but she had preferred to go alone.

She smiled very radiantly at Cousin Georgiana, who, she remembered, as of some one known a long time ago, had really been not a bad little creature.

Valancy was so happy that she could have smiled at anybody—even Uncle James.

She was not averse to Cousin Georgiana’s company.

Already, since the houses along the road were becoming numerous, she was conscious that curious  [curious - любопытный] eyes were looking at her from every window.  

“I suppose you’re going home, dear Doss?” said Cousin Georgiana as she shook hands—furtively eyeing Valancy’s dress and wondering if she had _any_ petticoat on at all.  

“Sooner or later,” said Valancy cryptically.  

“Then I’ll go along with you.

I’ve been wanting to see you very especially, Doss dear. [dear - дорогой] 

I’ve something quite _wonderful_ to tell you.”  

“Yes?” said Valancy absently.

What on earth was Cousin Georgiana looking so mysterious and important about?

But did it matter?

No.

Nothing mattered but Barney and the Blue Castle up back in Mistawis.  

“Who do you suppose called to see me the other day?” asked Cousin Georgiana archly.  

Valancy couldn’t guess.  

“Edward Beck.”

Cousin Georgiana lowered  [lowered - пониженный] her voice almost to a whisper.

“_Edward Beck_.”  

Why the italics?

And _was_ Cousin Georgiana blushing? [blushing - краснеющий]   

“Who on earth is Edward Beck?” asked Valancy indifferently.  

Cousin Georgiana stared.  

“Surely you remember Edward Beck,” she said reproachfully.

“He lives in that lovely house on the Port Lawrence road and he comes to our church—regularly.

You _must_ remember him.”  

“Oh, I think I do now,” said Valancy, with an effort of memory.

“He’s that old man with a wen on his forehead and dozens of children, who always sits in the pew by the door, isn’t he?”  

“Not dozens of children, dear—oh, no, not dozens.

Not even _one_ dozen.

Only nine.

At least only nine that count.

The rest are dead.

He isn’t old—he’s only about forty-eight—the prime of life, Doss—and what does it matter about a wen?”  

“Nothing, of course,” agreed Valancy quite sincerely.

It certainly did not matter to her whether Edward Beck had a wen or a dozen wens or no wen at all.

But Valancy was getting vaguely suspicious. [suspicious - подозрительный] 

There was certainly an air of suppressed  [suppressed - подавленный] triumph  [triumph - торжественный] about Cousin Georgiana.

Could it be possible that Cousin Georgiana was thinking of marrying again?

Marrying Edward Beck?

Absurd. [Absurd - абсурдный] 

Cousin Georgiana was sixty-five if she were a day and her little anxious face was as closely covered with fine wrinkles as if she had been a hundred.

But still——  “My dear, [dear - дорогой] ” said Cousin Georgiana, “Edward Beck wants to marry _you_.”  

Valancy stared at Cousin Georgiana for a moment.

Then she wanted to go off into a peal of laughter.

But she only said:  “Me?”  

“Yes, you.

He fell in love with you at the funeral. [funeral - похоронный] 

And he came to consult  [consult - посоветоваться] me about it.

I was such a friend of his first wife, you know.

He is very much in earnest, Dossie.

And it’s a wonderful chance for you.

He’s very well off—and you know—you—you——”  “Am not so young as I once was,” agreed Valancy.

“‘To her that hath shall be given.’

Do you really think I would make a good stepmother, [stepmother - мачеха]  Cousin Georgiana?”  

“I’m sure you would.

You were always so fond  [fond - любящий] of children.”  

“The two oldest are grown up and the third almost.

That leaves only six that really count.

And most of them are boys.

So much easier to bring up than girls.

There’s an excellent book—‘Health Care of the Growing Child’—Gladys has a copy, I think.

It would be such a help to you.

And there are books about morals.

You’d manage nicely. [nicely - красиво] 

Of course I told Mr. Beck that I thought you would—would——”  “Jump at him,” supplied  [supplied - поставляется] Valancy.  

“Oh, no, no, dear.

I wouldn’t use such an indelicate expression.

I told him I thought you would consider his proposal favourably.

And you will, won’t you, dearie?”  “There’s only one obstacle, [obstacle - препятствие] ” said Valancy dreamily. [dreamily - мечтательно] 

“You see, I’m married already.”  

“Married!”

Cousin Georgiana stopped stock- [stock - запас] still and stared at Valancy.

“Married!”  

“Yes.

I was married to Barney Snaith last Tuesday evening in Port Lawrence.”  

There was a convenient gate-post hard by.

Cousin Georgiana took firm  [firm - твердый] hold of it.  

“Doss, dear— [dear - дорогой] I’m an old woman—are you trying to make fun of me?”  

“Not at all.

I’m only telling you the truth.

For heaven’s sake, [sake - ради]  Cousin Georgiana,”—Valancy was alarmed by certain symptoms—“don’t go crying here on the public road!”  

Cousin Georgiana choked back the tears and gave a little moan of despair instead.  

“Oh, Doss, _what_ have you done?

What _have_

you done?”  

“I’ve just been telling you.

I’ve got married,” said Valancy, calmly  [calmly - спокойно] and patiently. [patiently - терпеливо]   

“To that—that—aw—that—_Barney Snaith_.

Why, they say he’s had a dozen wives already.”  

“I’m the only one round at present,” said Valancy.  

“What will your poor mother say?” moaned Cousin Georgiana.  

“Come along with me and hear, if you want to know,” said Valancy.

“I’m on my way to tell her now.”  

Cousin Georgiana let go the gate-post cautiously and found that she could stand alone.

She meekly trotted on beside Valancy—who suddenly seemed quite a different person in her eyes.

Cousin Georgiana had a tremendous  [tremendous - огромный] respect for a married woman.

But it was terrible to think of what the poor girl had done.

So rash. [rash - опрометчивый] 

So reckless.

Of course Valancy must be stark  [stark - резкий] mad.

She had never seen that look in Valancy’s eyes before.

But what _would_

Amelia say?

And Ben?  “To marry a man you know nothing about,” thought Cousin Georgiana aloud.  

“I know more about him than I know of Edward Beck,” said Valancy.  

“Edward Beck _goes to church_,” said Cousin Georgiana.

“Does Bar—does your husband?”  “He has promised that he will go with me on fine Sundays,” said Valancy.  

When they turned in at the Stirling gate Valancy gave an exclamation  [exclamation - восклицание] of surprise.  

“Look at my rosebush!

Why, it’s blooming! [blooming - цветение] ”  

It was.

Covered with blossoms. [blossoms - цветы] 

Great, crimson, velvety blossoms. [blossoms - цветы] 

Fragrant.

Glowing.

Wonderful.  

“My cutting it to pieces must have done it good,” said Valancy, laughing.

She gathered a handful  [handful - горсть] of the blossoms— [blossoms - цветы] they would look well on the supper-table of the verandah  [verandah - веранда] at Mistawis—and went, still laughing, up the walk, conscious that Olive was standing on the steps, Olive, goddess-like in loveliness, [loveliness - красота]  looking down with a slight  [slight - легкий] frown  [frown - хмурый взгляд] on her forehead.

Olive, beautiful, insolent.

Her full form voluptuous in its swathings of rose silk and lace. [lace - кружево] 

Her golden- [golden - золотой] brown hair curling richly under her big, white-frilled hat.

Her colour ripe and melting. [melting - тает]   

“Beautiful,” thought Valancy coolly, “but”—as if she suddenly saw her cousin through new eyes—“without

the slightest  [slightest - мельчайшее] touch of distinction. [distinction - отличие] ”  

She was looking triumphant—graceless!

That outlandish dress—that queer hat—those hands full of blood-red roses. [roses - розы] 

Yet there was something about both dress and hat, as Olive instantly  [instantly - мгновенно] felt, that was entirely lacking  [lacking - отсутствует] in her own attire.

This deepened the frown. [frown - хмурый] 

She put out a condescending hand.  

“So you’re back, Doss?

Very warm day, isn’t it?

Did you walk in?”  

“Yes.

Coming in?”  “Oh, no.

I’ve just been in.

I’ve come often to comfort poor Aunty.

She’s been so lonesome.

I’m going to Mrs. Bartlett’s tea.

I have to help pour.

She’s giving it for her cousin from Toronto.

Such a charming  [charming - очаровательный] girl.

You’d have loved meeting her, Doss.

I think Mrs. Bartlett did send you a card.

Perhaps you’ll drop in later on.”  

“No, I don’t think so,” said Valancy indifferently.

“I’ll have to be home to get Barney’s supper.

We’re going for a moonlit  [moonlit - залитый лунным светом] canoe  [canoe - каноэ] ride around Mistawis tonight.”  

“Barney? Supper?” gasped Olive.

“What _do_

you mean, Valancy Stirling?”  

“Valancy Snaith, by the grace  [grace - грация] of God.”  

Valancy flaunted her wedding-ring in Olive’s stricken face.

Then she nimbly stepped past her and into the house.

Cousin Georgiana followed.

She would not miss a moment of the great scene, even though Olive did look as if she were going to faint. [faint - слабый]   

Olive did not faint.

She went stupidly down the street to Mrs. Bartlett’s.

_

What_ did Doss mean?

She couldn’t have—that ring—oh, what fresh scandal  [scandal - скандал] was that wretched girl bringing on her defenceless  [defenceless - беззащитный] family now?

She should have been—shut up—long ago.  

Valancy opened the sitting- [sitting - сидящий] room door and stepped unexpectedly right into a grim  [grim - мрачный] assemblage of Stirlings.

They had not come together of malice prepense.

Aunt Wellington and Cousin Gladys and Aunt Mildred and Cousin Sarah had just called in on their way home from a meeting of the missionary society.

Uncle James had dropped in to give Amelia some information regarding a doubtful  [doubtful - сомнительный] investment. [investment - инвестиции] 

In all of their minds, unexpressed, was the idea of finding out if Valancy had yet come home, and, if not, what steps must be taken in the matter.  

Well, here was Valancy at last, a poised, confident thing, not humble  [humble - скромный] and deprecating as she should have been.

And so oddly, [oddly - странно]  improperly  [improperly - неправильно] young-looking.

She stood in the doorway  [doorway - дверной] and looked at them, Cousin Georgiana timorous, expectant, [expectant - выжидающий]  behind her.

Valancy was so happy she didn’t hate her people any more.

She could even see a number of good qualities in them that she had never seen before.

And she was sorry for them.

Her pity  [pity - жалость] made her quite gentle.  

“Well, Mother,” she said pleasantly.  

“So you’ve come home at last!” said Mrs. Frederick, getting out a handkerchief.

She dared  [dared - осмелился] not be outraged, but she did not mean to be cheated  [cheated - обманул] of her tears.  

“Well, not exactly,” said Valancy.

She threw her bomb.

“I thought I ought to drop in and tell you I was married.

Last Tuesday night.

To Barney Snaith.”  

Uncle Benjamin bounced  [bounced - отскочил] up and sat down again.  

“God bless  [bless - благослови] my soul!”

he said dully.

The rest seemed turned to stone.

Except Cousin Gladys, who turned faint.

Aunt Mildred and Uncle Wellington had to help her out to the kitchen.  

“She would have to keep up the Victorian  [Victorian - викторианскую] traditions,” said Valancy, with a grin. [grin - ухмылку] 

She sat down, uninvited, [uninvited - незваный]  on a chair.

Cousin Stickles had begun to sob. [sob - всхлип]   

“Is there _one_ day in your life that you haven’t cried?” asked Valancy curiously.  

“Valancy,” said Uncle James, being the first to recover the power of utterance, “did you mean what you said just now?”  “I did.”  

I do not want ever to see your face again.”  

“What have you left to say when I commit murder?” asked Valancy.  

Uncle Benjamin again appealed  [appealed - апеллировал] to God to bless  [bless - благословлял] his soul.  

“That drunken  [drunken - пьяный] outlaw—that——”  A dangerous spark  [spark - искра] appeared in Valancy’s eyes.

They might say what they liked to and of her

but they should not abuse  [abuse - ругань] Barney.  

“Say ‘damn’ and you’ll feel better,” she suggested.  

“I can express my feelings without blasphemy.

And I tell you have covered yourself with eternal  [eternal - вечный] disgrace  [disgrace - позор] and infamy by marrying that drunkard——”  “_You_ would be more endurable if you got drunk occasionally.

Barney is _not_ a drunkard.”  

“He was seen drunk in Port Lawrence—pickled to the gills,” said Uncle Benjamin.  

“If that is true—and I don’t believe it—he had a good reason for it.

Now I suggest that you all stop looking tragic  [tragic - трагический] and accept the situation.

I’m married—you can’t undo that.

And I’m perfectly happy.”  

“I suppose we ought to be thankful he has really married her,” said Cousin Sarah, by way of trying to look on the bright side.  

“If he really has,” said Uncle James, who had just washed his hands of Valancy.

“Who married you?”  “Mr. Towers, of Port Lawrence.”  

“By a Free Methodist!” groaned Mrs. Frederick—as if to have been married by an imprisoned  [imprisoned - заключенный] Methodist would have been a shade less disgraceful.

It was the first thing she had said.

Mrs. Frederick didn’t know _what_ to say.

The whole thing was too horrible—too nightmarish. [nightmarish - кошмарный] 

She was sure she must wake up soon.

After all their bright hopes at the funeral! [funeral - похороны]   

“It makes me think of those what-d’ye-call-’ems,” said Uncle Benjamin helplessly.

“Those yarns—you know—of fairies taking babies out of their cradles.”  

“Valancy could hardly be a changeling at twenty-nine,” said Aunt Wellington satirically.  

“She was the oddest-looking baby I ever saw, anyway,” averred Uncle Benjamin.

“I said so at the time—you remember, Amelia?

I said I had never seen such eyes in a human head.”  

“I’m glad _I_ never had any children,” said Cousin Sarah.

“If they don’t break your heart in one way they do it in another.”  

“Isn’t it better to have your heart broken than to have it wither up?” queried  [queried - допрашивали] Valancy.

“Before it could be broken it must have felt something splendid.

_That_ would be worth the pain.”  

“Dippy—clean dippy,” muttered  [muttered - бормотал] Uncle Benjamin, with a vague, [vague - расплывчато]  unsatisfactory  [unsatisfactory - неудовлетворительно] feeling that somebody had said something like that before.  

“Valancy,” said Mrs. Frederick solemnly, “do you ever pray to be forgiven for disobeying your mother?”  

“But I don’t pray about that at all.

I just thank God every day for my happiness.”  

“I would rather,” said Mrs. Frederick, beginning to cry rather belatedly, “see you dead before me than listen to what you have told me today.”  

Valancy looked at her mother and aunts, and wondered if they could ever have known anything of the real meaning of love.

She felt sorrier for them than ever.

They were so very pitiable.

And they never suspected  [suspected - подозреваемый] it.  

“Barney Snaith is a scoundrel to have deluded you into marrying him,” said Uncle James violently.  

“Oh, _I_ did the deluding.

I asked _him_ to marry me,” said Valancy, with a wicked  [wicked - злой] smile.  

“Have you _no_ pride? [pride - гордость] ” demanded Aunt Wellington.  

“Lots of it.

I am proud that I have achieved a husband by my own unaided  [unaided - без посторонней помощи] efforts.

Cousin Georgiana here wanted to help me to Edward Beck.”  

“Edward Beck is worth twenty thousand dollars and has the finest house between here and Port Lawrence,” said Uncle Benjamin.  

“That sounds very fine,” said Valancy scornfully, [scornfully - пренебрежительно]  “but it isn’t worth _

that_“—she snapped  [snapped - надломленный] her fingers—“compared to feeling Barney’s arms around me and his cheek  [cheek - щека] against mine.”  

“_Oh_, Doss!” said Cousin Stickles.

Cousin Sarah said, “Oh, _Doss_!”

Aunt Wellington said, “Valancy, you need not be indecent.”  

“Why, it surely isn’t indecent to like to have your husband put his arm around you?

I should think it would be indecent if you didn’t.”  

“Why expect decency from her?” inquired Uncle James sarcastically. [sarcastically - саркастически] 

“She has cut herself off from decency forevermore.

She has made her bed.

Let her lie on it.”  

“Thanks,” said Valancy very gratefully.

“How you would have enjoyed being Torquemada!

Now, I must really be getting back.

Mother, may I have those three woollen  [woollen - шерстяной] cushions I worked last winter?”  

“Take them—take everything!” said Mrs. Frederick.  

“Oh, I don’t want everything—or much.

I don’t want my Blue Castle cluttered. [cluttered - загроможденный] 

Just the cushions.

I’ll call for them some day when we motor in.”  

Valancy rose and went to the door.

There she turned.

She was sorrier than ever for them all.

_

They_ had no Blue Castle in the purple solitudes of Mistawis.  

“The trouble with you people is that you don’t laugh enough,” she said.  

“Doss, dear, [dear - дорогой] ” said Cousin Georgiana mournfully, “some day you will discover that blood is thicker than water.”  

“Of course it is.

But who wants water to be thick?” parried Valancy.

“We want water to be thin—sparkling— [sparkling - искрящийся] crystal- [crystal - хрустальный] clear.”  

Cousin Stickles groaned.  

Valancy would not ask any of them to come and see her—she was afraid they _would_ come out of curiosity. [curiosity - любопытство] 

But she said:  “Do you mind if I drop in and see you once in a while, Mother?”  

“I dare  [dare - дерзко] say the marriage isn’t legal,” said Uncle James comfortingly.

“He has probably been married half a dozen times before.

But _I_ am through with her.

I have done all I could, Amelia.

I think you will admit that.

Henceforth”—Uncle

James was terribly  [terribly - ужасно] solemn about it—“Valancy is to me as one dead.”  

“Mrs. Barney Snaith,” said Cousin Georgiana, as if trying it out to see how it would sound.  

“He has a score of aliases, no doubt,” said Uncle Benjamin.

“For my part, I believe the man is half Indian.

I haven’t a doubt they’re living in a wigwam.”  

“If he has married her under the name of Snaith and it isn’t his real name wouldn’t that make the marriage null and void?” asked Cousin Stickles hopefully. [hopefully - надеюсь]   

Uncle James shook his head.  

“No, it is the man who marries, not the name.”  

“You know,” said Cousin Gladys, who had recovered and returned but was still shaky, [shaky - шатко]  “I had a distinct  [distinct - отчётливо] premonition of this at Herbert’s silver dinner.

I remarked it at the time.

When she was defending Snaith.

You remember, of course.

It came over me like a revelation. [revelation - откровение] 

I spoke to David when I went home about it.”  

“What—_what_,” demanded Aunt Wellington of the universe, “has come over Valancy? _Valancy_!”  

The universe did not answer but Uncle James did.  

“Isn’t there something coming up of late about secondary  [secondary - вторично] personalities cropping out?

I don’t hold with many of those new-fangled notions, but there may be something in this one.

It would account for her incomprehensible conduct.”  

“Valancy is so fond  [fond - люблю] of mushrooms,” sighed  [sighed - вздохнул] Cousin Georgiana.

“I’m afraid she’ll get poisoned  [poisoned - отравил] eating toadstools by mistake living up back in the woods.”  

“There are worse things than death,” said Uncle James, believing that it was the first time in the world that such a statement had been made.  

“Nothing can ever be the same again!”

sobbed  [sobbed - всхлипнул] Cousin Stickles.  

Valancy, hurrying along the dusty road, back to cool Mistawis and her purple island, had forgotten all about them—just as she had forgotten that she might drop dead at any moment if she hurried.     

CHAPTER XXVIII   Summer passed by.

The Stirling clan— [clan - клан] with the insignificant exception  [exception - исключение] of Cousin Georgiana—had tacitly agreed to follow Uncle James’ example and look upon Valancy as one dead.

To be sure, Valancy had an unquiet, ghostly habit of recurring resurrections when she and Barney clattered through Deerwood and out to the Port in that unspeakable car.

Valancy, bareheaded, with stars in her eyes.

Barney, bareheaded, smoking his pipe.

But shaved.

Always shaved now, if any of them had noticed it.

They even had the audacity to go in to Uncle Benjamin’s store to buy groceries. [groceries - бакалея] 

Twice Uncle Benjamin ignored them.

Was not Valancy one of the dead?

While Snaith had never existed.

“I have made her happy,” he said coolly, “and she was miserable  [miserable - несчастный] with her friends.

So that’s that.”  

Uncle Benjamin stared.

It had never occurred to him that women had to be, or ought to be, “made happy.”  

“You—you pup!”

he said.  

“Why be so unoriginal?” queried  [queried - вопрошанный] Barney amiably.

“Anybody could call me a pup.

Why not think of something worthy  [worthy - достойный] of the Stirlings?

Besides, I’m not a pup.

I’m really quite a middle-aged dog.

Thirty-five, if you’re interested in knowing.”  

Uncle Benjamin remembered just in time that Valancy was dead.

He turned his back on Barney.  

Valancy _was_ happy—gloriously and entirely so.

She seemed to be living in a wonderful house of life and every day opened a new, mysterious room.

It was in a world which had nothing in common with the one she had left behind—a world where time was not—which was young with immortal  [immortal - бессмертный] youth—where there was neither past nor future but only the present.

She surrendered  [surrendered - преданный] herself utterly  [utterly - совершенно] to the charm  [charm - прелесть] of it.  

The absolute freedom of it all was unbelievable.

They could do exactly as they liked.

No Mrs. Grundy.

No traditions.

No relatives.

Or in-laws.

“Peace, perfect peace, with loved ones far away,” as Barney quoted  [quoted - цитируемый] shamelessly.  

Valancy had gone home once and got her cushions.

And Cousin Georgiana had given her one of her famous candlewick spreads of most elaborate  [elaborate - разработанный] design.

“For your spare- [spare - запасной] room bed, dear,” she said.  

“But I haven’t got any spare- [spare - запасной] room,” said Valancy.  

Cousin Georgiana looked horrified. [horrified - в ужасе] 

A house without a spare- [spare - запасной] room was monstrous to her.  

“But it’s a lovely spread,” said Valancy, with a kiss, “and I’m so glad to have it.

I’ll put it on my own bed.

Barney’s old patch- [patch - патч] work quilt is getting ragged.”  

“I don’t see how you can be contented to live up back,” sighed  [sighed - вздохнул] Cousin Georgiana.

“It’s so out of the world.”  

“Contented!”

Valancy laughed.

What was the use of trying to explain to Cousin Georgiana.

“It is,” she agreed, “most gloriously and entirely out of the world.”  

“And you are really happy, dear?” asked Cousin Georgiana wistfully.  

“When it’s going to last long,” agreed Valancy.  

Cousin Georgiana did not understand this at all.

But it worried her and she lay awake at nights wondering what Valancy meant by it.  

Valancy loved her Blue Castle and was completely satisfied with it.

The big living- [living - живые] room had three windows, all commanding exquisite  [exquisite - изысканные]  [exquisite - изысканные] views of exquisite Mistawis.

The one in the end of the room was an oriel window—which Tom MacMurray, Barney explained, had got out of some little, old “up back” church that had been sold.

It faced the west and when the sunsets flooded it Valancy’s whole being knelt  [knelt - преклонили колени] in prayer as if in some great cathedral. [cathedral - собор] 

The new moons always looked down through it, the lower pine  [pine - сосна] boughs swayed about the top of it, and all through the nights the soft, dim  [dim - тусклый] silver of the lake dreamed through it.  

There was a stone fireplace  [fireplace - камин] on the other side.

No desecrating gas imitation  [imitation - имитация] but a real fireplace  [fireplace - камин] where you could burn real logs.

With a big grizzly bearskin on the floor before it, and beside it a hideous, red-plush sofa of Tom MacMurray’s régime.

But its ugliness was hidden by silver-grey timber  [timber - дерево] wolf  [wolf - волк] skins, and Valancy’s cushions made it gay  [gay - веселый] and comfortable.

In a corner a nice, tall, lazy old clock ticked—the right kind of a clock.

One that did not hurry the hours away but ticked them off deliberately. [deliberately - нарочито] 

It was the jolliest looking old clock.

A fat, corpulent clock with a great, round, man’s face painted on it, the hands stretching out of its nose and the hours encircling it like a halo.  

Some comfortable old chairs that asked to be sat upon.

A squat little chair with a cushion was prescriptively Banjo’s.

If anybody else dared  [dared - осмелился] sit on it Banjo glared him out of it with his topaz-hued, black-ringed eyes.

Banjo had an adorable habit of hanging over the back of it, trying to catch his own tail. [tail - хвост] 

Losing his temper because he couldn’t catch it.

Giving it a fierce  [fierce - жестокий] bite for spite  [spite - злоба] when he _did_ catch it.

Yowling malignantly with pain.

Barney and Valancy laughed at him until they ached. [ached - болел] 

But it was Good Luck they loved.

They were both agreed that Good Luck was so lovable that he practically  [practically - практически] amounted to an obsession. [obsession - одержимость]   

One side of the wall was lined  [lined - подкладка] with rough, homemade book-shelves filled with books, and between the two side windows hung an old mirror in a faded gilt frame, with fat cupids gamboling in the panel  [panel - панель] over the glass.

A mirror, Valancy thought, that must be like the fabled mirror into which Venus had once looked and which thereafter reflected as beautiful every woman who looked into it.

Valancy thought she was almost pretty in that mirror.

But that may have been because she had shingled her hair.  

This was before the day of bobs and was regarded as a wild, unheard-of proceeding—unless you had typhoid. [typhoid - тиф] 

When Mrs. Frederick heard of it she almost decided to erase  [erase - стирать] Valancy’s name from the family Bible.

Barney cut the hair, square off at the back of Valancy’s neck, bringing it down in a short black fringe over her forehead.

It gave a meaning and a purpose to her little, three-cornered face that it never had possessed before.

Even her nose ceased  [ceased - прекратил] to irritate her.

Her eyes were bright, and her sallow skin had cleared to the hue of creamy  [creamy - кремовый] ivory.

The old family joke had come true—she was really fat at last—anyway, no longer skinny.

Valancy might never be beautiful, but she was of the type that looks its best in the woods—elfin—mocking—alluring.  

Her heart bothered her very little.

When an attack threatened  [threatened - угрожало] she was generally able to head it off with Dr. Trent’s prescription. [prescription - рецепт] 

But the rest of the time she would not—did not—let herself remember it at all.     

CHAPTER XXIX   Valancy toiled  [toiled - трудится] not, neither did she spin. [spin - крутиться] 

There was really very little work to do.

She cooked their meals on a coal-oil stove, performing all her little domestic  [domestic - домашнее] rites  [rites - обряды] carefully and exultingly, and they ate out on the verandah  [verandah - веранда] that almost overhung the lake.

Before them lay Mistawis, like a scene out of some fairy tale of old time.

And Barney smiling his twisted, [twisted - извилистый]  enigmatical smile at her across the table.  

“What a view old Tom picked out when he built this shack! [shack - лачуга] ”

Barney would say exultantly.  

Supper was the meal Valancy liked best.

The faint laughter of winds was always about them and the colours of Mistawis, imperial  [imperial - императорский] and spiritual, under the changing clouds were something that cannot be expressed in mere  [mere - просто] words.

Shadows, too.

Clustering in the pines  [pines - сосны] until a wind shook them out and pursued them over Mistawis.

They lay all day along the shores, [shores - берега]  threaded by ferns and wild blossoms. [blossoms - цветы] 

They stole around the headlands in the glow  [glow - сияние] of the sunset, until twilight wove them all into one great web of dusk. [dusk - сумерки]   

The cats, with their wise, innocent little faces, would sit on the verandah  [verandah - веранда] railing and eat the tidbits Barney flung them.

And how good everything tasted!

Barney paid her no end of compliments on her cooking.  

“After all,” he admitted, “there’s something to be said for square meals.

I’ve mostly got along by boiling two or three dozen eggs hard at once and eating a few when I got hungry, with a slice of bacon once in a while and a jorum of tea.”  

Valancy poured tea out of Barney’s little battered  [battered - потрепанный] old pewter teapot of incredible age.

She had not even a set of dishes—only Barney’s mismatched  [mismatched - несоответствующий] chipped bits—and a dear, big, pobby old jug of robin’s-egg blue.  

After the meal was over they would sit there and talk for hours—or sit and say nothing, in all the languages of the world, Barney pulling away at his pipe, Valancy dreaming idly  [idly - праздно] and deliciously, gazing at the far-off hills beyond Mistawis where the spires of firs came out against the sunset.

The moonlight  [moonlight - лунный свет] would begin to silver the Mistawis dusk. [dusk - сумерки] 

Bats  [Bats - Летучие мыши] would begin to swoop darkly  [darkly - темно] against the pale, western  [western - западный] gold.

The little waterfall that came down on the high bank not far away would, by some whim  [whim - прихоть] of the wildwood gods, [gods - боги]  begin to look like a wonderful white woman beckoning through the spicy, fragrant evergreens.

And Leander would begin to chuckle diabolically on the mainland  [mainland - материк] shore. [shore - берег] 

How sweet it was to sit there and do nothing in the beautiful silence, with Barney at the other side of the table, smoking!  

There were plenty of other islands in sight, though none were near enough to be troublesome  [troublesome - проблемный] as neighbours.

There was one little group of islets far off to the west which they called the Fortunate Isles.

At sunrise they looked like a cluster  [cluster - скопление]  [cluster - скопление] of emeralds, at sunset like a cluster of amethysts.

Music would drift  [drift - дрифт] to them alluringly from boats here and there, or from the verandahs on the big house of the millionaire  [millionaire - миллионер] on the biggest island.  

“Would you like a house like that, Moonlight?”

Barney asked once, waving his hand at it.

He had taken to calling her Moonlight, and Valancy loved it.  

“No,” said Valancy, who had once dreamed of a mountain castle ten times the size of the rich man’s “cottage” [cottage - коттедж]  and now pitied the poor inhabitants  [inhabitants - жители] of palaces.

“No.

It’s too elegant. [elegant - элегантный] 

I would have to carry it with me everywhere I went.

On my back like a snail. [snail - улитка] 

It would own me—possess me, body and soul.

I like a house I can love and cuddle and boss.

Just like ours here.

I don’t envy  [envy - зависть] Hamilton Gossard ‘the finest summer residence  [residence - резиденция] in Canada.’

It is magnificent, [magnificent - великолепный]  but it isn’t my Blue Castle.”  

Away down at the far  [far - далеко] end of the lake they got every night a glimpse  [glimpse - заглянуть] of a big, continental train rushing through a clearing.

Valancy liked to watch its lighted windows flash  [flash - вспышка] by and wonder who was on it and what hopes and fears it carried.

She also amused  [amused - развлекался] herself by picturing  [picturing - рисовал] Barney and herself going to the dances and dinners in the houses on the islands, but she did not want to go in reality.

From the smells that filtered through at times she concluded he must be conducting  [conducting - проводящий] chemical experiments—or counterfeiting money.

Valancy supposed there must be smelly processes in making counterfeit money.

But she did not trouble herself about it.

She had no desire to peer  [peer - равный] into the locked chambers  [chambers - камеры] of Barney’s house of life.

His past and his future concerned  [concerned - обеспокоенный] her not.

Only this rapturous present.

Nothing else mattered.  

Once he went away and stayed away two days and nights.

He had asked Valancy if she would be afraid to stay alone and she had said she would not.

He never told her where he had been.

She was not afraid to be alone, but she was horribly lonely.

The sweetest sound she had ever heard was Lady Jane’s clatter through the woods when Barney returned.

And then his signal whistle from the shore. [shore - берег] 

She ran down to the landing  [landing - приземляющийся] rock to greet him—to nestle herself into his eager  [eager - нетерпеливый]  [eager - нетерпеливый] arms—they _did_ seem eager.  

“Have you missed me, Moonlight?”

Barney was whispering. [whispering - шепчущий]   

“It seems a hundred years since you went away,” said Valancy.  

“I won’t leave you again.”  

“You must,” protested  [protested - протестующий] Valancy, “if you want to.

I’d be miserable  [miserable - жалкий] if I thought you wanted to go and didn’t, because of me.

I want you to feel perfectly free.”  

Barney laughed—a little cynically. [cynically - цинично]   

“There is no such thing as freedom on earth,” he said.

“Only different kinds of bondages.

And comparative  [comparative - сравнительный] bondages.

_

You_ think you are free now because you’ve escaped from a peculiarly unbearable  [unbearable - невыносимый] kind of bondage.

But are you?

You love me—_that’s_ a bondage.”  

When I came down through the woods and saw my home lights—mine—gleaming out under the old pines— [pines - сосны] something I’d never seen before—oh, girl, I was glad—glad!”  

But in spite  [spite - злоба] of Barney’s doctrine  [doctrine - учение] of bondage, Valancy thought they were splendidly free.

It was amazing to be able to sit up half the night and look at the moon if you wanted to.

To be late for meals if you wanted to—she who had always been rebuked so sharply  [sharply - резко] by her mother and so reproachfully by Cousin Stickles if she were one minute late.

Dawdle over meals as long as you wanted to.

Leave your crusts if you wanted to.

Not come home at all for meals if you wanted to.

Sit on a sun-warm rock and paddle  [paddle - весло] your bare  [bare - голый] feet in the hot sand if you wanted to.

Just sit and do nothing in the beautiful silence if you wanted to.

In short, do any fool  [fool - дурак] thing you wanted to whenever the notion took you.

If _that_ wasn’t freedom, what was?     

CHAPTER XXX   

They didn’t spend all their days on the island.

They spent more than half of them wandering  [wandering - бродячий] at will through the enchanted Muskoka country.

Barney knew the woods as a book and he taught their lore and craft  [craft - ремесло] to Valancy.

He could always find trail and haunt  [haunt - преследовать] of the shy wood people.

Valancy learned the different fairy-likenesses of the mosses—the charm  [charm - очарование] and exquisiteness of woodland blossoms. [blossoms - цветы] 

She learned to know every bird at sight and mimic  [mimic - мимикрировать] its call—though never so perfectly as Barney.

She made friends with every kind of tree.

She learned to paddle  [paddle - весло] a canoe  [canoe - каноэ] as well as Barney himself.

She liked to be out in the rain and she never caught cold.  

Sometimes they took a lunch with them and went berrying—strawberries and blueberries.

And Valancy learned the real flavour  [flavour - аромат] of the strawberry in its highest perfection.

There was a certain sunlit dell on the banks of Mistawis along which white birches grew on one side and on the other still, changeless ranks of young spruces.

There were long grasses at the roots of the birches, combed  [combed - гребенчатый] down by the winds and wet with morning dew late into the afternoons.

Here they found berries  [berries - ягоды] that might have graced the banquets of Lucullus, great ambrosial sweetnesses hanging like rubies to long, rosy  [rosy - розовый] stalks.

They lifted them by the stalk and ate them from it, uncrushed and virgin, tasting each berry  [berry - ягодный] by itself with all its wild fragrance  [fragrance - аромат] ensphered therein.

When Valancy carried any of these berries  [berries - ягоды]  [berries - ягоды] home that elusive  [elusive - неуловимый] essence  [essence - эссенция] escaped and they became nothing more than the common berries of the market-place—very kitchenly good indeed, but not as they would have been, eaten in their birch dell until her fingers were stained  [stained - витражный] as pink as Aurora’s eyelids. [eyelids - веки]   

Or they went after water-lilies.

Barney knew where to find them in the creeks  [creeks - ручьи] and bays  [bays - бухты] of Mistawis.

Sometimes they went trouting on little nameless  [nameless - безымянный] rivers or hidden brooks on whose banks Naiads might have sunned their white, wet limbs. [limbs - конечности] 

Then all they took with them were some raw potatoes and salt.

They roasted the potatoes over a fire and Barney showed Valancy how to cook the trout by wrapping them in leaves, coating them with mud and baking them in a bed of hot coals.

Never were such delicious meals.

Valancy had such an appetite  [appetite - аппетит] it was no wonder she put flesh  [flesh - плоть] on her bones.  

Or they just prowled  [prowled - крался] and explored through woods that always seemed to be expecting something wonderful to happen.

At least, that was the way Valancy felt about them.

Down the next hollow— [hollow - полый] over the next hill—you would find it.  

“We don’t know where we’re going, but isn’t it fun to go?”

Barney used to say.  

Once or twice night overtook  [overtook - нагнал] them, too far from their Blue Castle to get back.

But Barney made a fragrant bed of bracken and fir boughs and they slept on it dreamlessly, under a ceiling  [ceiling - потолок] of old spruces with moss hanging from them, while beyond them moonlight  [moonlight - лунный свет] and the murmur  [murmur - бормотание] of pines  [pines - сосны] blended together so that one could hardly tell which was light and which was sound. [sound - звук]   

There were rainy days, of course, when Muskoka was a wet green land.

Days when showers drifted  [drifted - дрейфовал] across Mistawis like pale ghosts of rain and they never thought of staying in because of it.

Days when it rained in right good  [good - хороший] earnest

and they had to stay in.

One felt really too happy for Sunday.

Valancy had never really liked Sundays before.  

And always, Sundays and weekdays, she was with Barney.

Nothing else really mattered.

And what a companion  [companion - компаньон] he was!

How understanding! [understanding - понимание] 

How jolly!

How—how Barney—like!

That summed it all up.  

Valancy had taken some of her two hundred dollars out of the bank and spent it in pretty clothes.

She had a little smoke-blue chiffon which she always put on when they spent the evening at home—smoke-blue with touches of silver about it.

It was after she began wearing it that Barney began calling her Moonlight.  

“Moonlight and blue twilight—that is what you look like in that dress.

I like it.

It belongs to you.

You aren’t exactly pretty, but you have some adorable beauty-spots.

Your eyes.

And that little kissable dent just between your collar bones.

You have the wrist  [wrist - запястье] and ankle of an aristocrat. [aristocrat - аристократ] 

That little head of yours is beautifully shaped.

And when you look backward  [backward - отсталый] over your shoulder you’re maddening—especially in twilight or moonlight. [moonlight - лунный свет] 

An elf maiden.

A wood sprite.

You belong to the woods, Moonlight—you should never be out of them.

In spite  [spite - злоба] of your ancestry, there is something wild and remote and untamed about you.

And you have such a nice, sweet, throaty, summery voice.

Such a nice voice for love-making. [making - делающий] ”  

“Shure an’ ye’ve kissed the Blarney Stone,” scoffed  [scoffed - осмеянный] Valancy.

But she tasted these compliments for weeks.  

She got a pale green bathing-suit, too—a garment which would have given her clan  [clan - клан] their deaths if they had ever seen her in it.

Barney taught her how to swim.

Sometimes she put her bathing-dress on when she got up and didn’t take it off until she went to bed—running down to the water for a plunge  [plunge - окунание] whenever she felt like it and sprawling  [sprawling - расползание] on the sun-warm rocks to dry.  

She had forgotten all the old humiliating  [humiliating - унижение] things that used to come up against her in the night—the injustices  [injustices - несправедливости] and the disappointments. [disappointments - разочарования] 

It was as if they had all happened to some other person—not to her, Valancy Snaith, who had always been happy.  

“I understand now what it means to be born again,” she told Barney.  

She found it hard to believe that she had ever been lonely and unhappy and afraid.  

“When death comes, I shall have lived,” thought Valancy.

“I shall have had my hour.”  

And her dust-pile!  

One day Valancy had heaped  [heaped - нагроможденный] up the sand in the little island cove in a tremendous  [tremendous - огромный] cone  [cone - конус] and stuck a gay  [gay - веселый] little Union Jack on top of it.  

“What are you celebrating?”

Barney wanted to know.  

“I’m just exorcising an old demon, [demon - демон] ” Valancy told him.     

CHAPTER XXXI   Autumn came.

Late September with cool nights.

They had to forsake the verandah; [verandah - веранда]  but they kindled a fire in the big fireplace  [fireplace - камин] and sat before it with jest and laughter.

They left the doors open, and Banjo and Good Luck came and went at pleasure.

Sometimes they sat gravely  [gravely - гравийный] on the bearskin rug  [rug - коврик] between Barney and Valancy; sometimes they slunk off into the mystery of the chill  [chill - холод] night outside.

The stars smouldered in the horizon  [horizon - горизонт] mists  [mists - туманы] through the old oriel.

The haunting, [haunting - навязчивый]  persistent  [persistent - настойчивый] croon of the pine- [pine - сосна] trees filled the air.

They needed no light but the firelight that sometimes leaped up and revealed them—sometimes shrouded them in shadow.

When the night wind rose higher Barney would shut the door and light a lamp and read to her—poetry and essays and gorgeous, [gorgeous - великолепный]  dim  [dim - тусклый] chronicles of ancient wars.

Barney never would read novels: he vowed  [vowed - поклявшийся] they bored him.

But sometimes she read them herself, curled up on the wolf  [wolf - волк] skins, laughing aloud in peace.

For Barney was not one of those aggravating people who can never hear you smiling audibly over something you’ve read without inquiring placidly, [placidly - безмятежно]  “What is the joke?”  October—with a gorgeous  [gorgeous - великолепный] pageant of color around Mistawis, into which Valancy plunged  [plunged - погруженный] her soul.

Never had she imagined anything so splendid.

A great, tinted peace.

Blue, wind-winnowed skies.

Sunlight sleeping in the glades of that fairyland.

Long dreamy  [dreamy - мечтательный] purple days paddling  [paddling - гребущий] idly  [idly - праздно] in their canoe  [canoe - каноэ] along shores  [shores - берега] and up the rivers of crimson and gold.

A sleepy, red hunter’s moon.

Enchanted tempests that stripped  [stripped - раздетый] the leaves from the trees and heaped  [heaped - наваленный] them along the shores. [shores - берега] 

Flying shadows of clouds.

With dear days when the austere woods were beautiful and gracious in a dignified serenity of folded hands and closed eyes—days full of a fine, pale sunshine that sifted through the late, leafless gold of the juniper-trees and glimmered among the grey beeches, lighting up evergreen banks of moss and washing the colonnades of the pines. [pines - сосны] 

Days with a high-sprung sky of flawless  [flawless - безупречный] turquoise.

Days when an exquisite  [exquisite - изысканный] melancholy seemed to hang over the landscape and dream about the lake.

But days, too, of the wild blackness  [blackness - чернота] of great autumn storms, followed by dank, wet, streaming  [streaming - поток] nights when there was witch-laughter in the pines  [pines - сосны] and fitful moans among the mainland  [mainland - материк] trees.

What cared they?

Old Tom had built his roof well, and his chimney  [chimney - дымоход] drew.  

“Warm fire—books—comfort—safety from storm—our cats on the rug. [rug - коврик] 

Moonlight,” said Barney, “would you be any happier now if you had a million dollars?”  

“No—nor half so happy.

I’d be bored by conventions  [conventions - конвенции] and obligations  [obligations - обязательства] then.”  

December.

Early snows and Orion.

The pale fires of the Milky Way.

It was really winter now—wonderful, cold, starry  [starry - звездный] winter.

How Valancy had always hated winter!

Dull, [Dull - Скучный]  brief, uneventful  [uneventful - без происшествий] days.

Long, cold, companionless nights.

Cousin Stickles whining over the price of coal.

Her mother, probing, questioning, [questioning - вопрошание]  ignoring.

Endless  [Endless - Бесконечный] colds and bronchitis—or the dread of it.

Redfern’s Liniment and Purple Pills.  

But now she loved winter.

Winter was beautiful “up back”—almost intolerably beautiful.

Days of clear brilliance. [brilliance - блеск] 

Evenings that were like cups of glamour— [glamour - гламур] the purest vintage of winter’s wine.

Nights with their fire of stars.

Cold, exquisite  [exquisite - изысканность] winter sunrises.

Lovely ferns of ice all over the windows of the Blue Castle.

Moonlight  [Moonlight - Лунный свет] on birches in a silver thaw.

Ragged shadows on windy evenings—torn, twisted, [twisted - скрученный]  fantastic shadows.

Great silences, austere and searching.

Jewelled, barbaric hills.

The sun suddenly breaking through grey clouds over long, white Mistawis.

Icy- [Icy - Ледяной] grey twilights, broken by snow-squalls, when their cosy living- [living - живой] room, with its goblins of firelight and inscrutable cats seemed cosier than ever.

Every hour brought a new revelation  [revelation - откровение] and wonder.  

Barne [barn - амбар] y ran Lady Jane into Roaring  [Roaring - Ревущий] Abel’s barn and taught Valancy how to snowshoe—Valancy, who ought to be laid up with bronchitis.

But Valancy had not even a cold.

Later on in the winter Barney had a terrible one and Valancy nursed him through it with a dread of pneumonia  [pneumonia - пневмония] in her heart.

But Valancy’s colds seemed to have gone where old moons go.

Which was luck—for she hadn’t even Redfern’s Liniment.

She had thoughtfully  [thoughtfully - задумчиво] bought a bottle at the Port and Barney had hurled it into frozen Mistawis with a scowl.  

“Bring no more of that devilish stuff here,” he had ordered briefly. [briefly - кратко] 

It was the first and last time he had spoken harshly to her.  

At times they seemed to be walking through a spellbound  [spellbound - завороженный] world of crystal and pearl, [pearl - жемчуг]  so white and radiant were clearings and lakes and sky.

The air was so crisp  [crisp - четкость] and clear that it was half intoxicating.  

Once they stood in a hesitation  [hesitation - колебание] of ecstasy at the entrance of a narrow path between ranks of birches.

Every twig and spray  [spray - брызги] was outlined in snow.

The undergrowth along its sides was a little fairy forest cut out of marble.

The shadows cast  [cast - бросок] by the pale sunshine were fine and spiritual.  

“Come away,” said Barney, turning.

“We must not commit the desecration of tramping through there.”  

One evening they came upon a snowdrift far back in an old clearing which was in the exact likeness of a beautiful woman’s profile. [profile - профиль] 

Seen too close by, the resemblance was lost, as in the fairy-tale of the Castle of St. John.

Seen from behind, it was a shapeless oddity. [oddity - странность] 

But at just the right distance and angle  [angle - угол] the outline  [outline - контур] was so perfect that when they came suddenly upon it, gleaming out against the dark background of spruce in the glow  [glow - сияние] of that winter sunset they both exclaimed  [exclaimed - воскликнул] in amazement. [amazement - изумление] 

“‘All the beauty that old Greece and Rome, sung painted, taught,’” quoted  [quoted - цитируемая] Barney.  

“And to think no human eyes save ours have seen or will see it,” breathed Valancy, who felt at times as if she were living in a book by John Foster.

As she looked around her she recalled some passages she had marked in the new Foster book Barney had brought her from the Port—with an adjuration not to expect _him_ to read or listen to it.  

“‘All the tintings of winter woods are extremely delicate  [delicate - нежная] and elusive, [elusive - неуловимая] ’” recalled Valancy.

“‘When the brief afternoon wanes and the sun just touches the tops of the hills, there seems to be all over the woods an abundance, [abundance - изобилие]  not of colour, but of the spirit of colour.

There is really nothing but pure white after all, but one has the impression of fairy-like blendings of rose  [rose - роза] and violet, opal and heliotrope on the slopes—in the dingles and along the curves of the forest-land.

You feel sure the tint is there, but when you look at it directly it is gone.

From the corner of your eye you are aware that it is lurking over yonder in a spot where there was nothing but pale purity  [purity - чистота] a moment ago.

Only just when the sun is setting is there a fleeting  [fleeting - мимолетное] moment of real colour.

Then the redness streams out over the snow and incarnadines the hills and rivers and smites the crest of the pines  [pines - сосны] with flame. [flame - пламя] 

Just a few minutes of transfiguration and revelation— [revelation - откровение] and it is gone.’”  

“I wonder if John Foster ever spent a winter in Mistawis,” said Valancy.  

“Not likely,” scoffed  [scoffed - насмешливое] Barney.

“People who write tosh like that generally write it in a warm house on some smug  [smug - самодовольное] city street.”  

“You are too hard on John Foster,” said Valancy severely. [severely - сурово] 

“No one could have written that little paragraph I read you last night without having seen it first—you know he couldn’t.”  

“I didn’t listen to it,” said Barney morosely.

“You know I told you I wouldn’t.”  

“Then you’ve got to listen to it now,” persisted  [persisted - настойчивое] Valancy.

She made him stand still on his snowshoes while she repeated it.  

“‘She is a rare artist, this old Mother Nature, who works “for the joy of working” and not in any spirit of vain show.

Today the fir woods are a symphony of greens and greys, so subtle  [subtle - тонкое] that you cannot tell where one shade begins to be the other.

She must have a dash  [dash - рывок] of colour.

See it.

A broken dead fir bough, of a beautiful red-brown, swinging among the beards of moss.’”  

“Good Lord, do you learn all that fellow’s books by heart?” was Barney’s disgusted  [disgusted - отвращение] reaction  [reaction - реакция] as he strode  [strode - строде] off.  

“John Foster’s books were all that saved my soul alive the past five years,” averred Valancy.

“Oh, Barney, look at that exquisite  [exquisite - изысканный] filigree of snow in the furrows of that old elm-tree trunk. [trunk - ствол] ”  

When they came out to the lake they changed from snowshoes to skates and skated home.

For a wonder Valancy had learned, when she was a little schoolgirl, [schoolgirl - школьница]  to skate on the pond  [pond - пруд] behind the Deerwood school.

She never had any skates of her own, but some of the other girls had lent her theirs and she seemed to have a natural knack of it.

Uncle Benjamin had once promised her a pair of skates for Christmas, but when Christmas came he had given her rubbers instead.

She had never skated since she grew up, but the old trick came back quickly, and glorious  [glorious - великолепный] were the hours she and Barney spent skimming  [skimming - скольжение] over the white lakes and past the dark islands where the summer cottages  [cottages - коттеджи] were closed and silent.

Tonight they flew down Mistawis before the wind, in an exhilaration  [exhilaration - волнение] that crimsoned Valancy’s cheeks  [cheeks - щеки] under her white tam.

And at the end was her dear little house, on the island of pines, [pines - сосны]  with a coating of snow on its roof, sparkling in the moonlight. [moonlight - лунный свет] 

“Looks exactly like a picture-book, doesn’t it?” said Barney.  

They had a lovely Christmas.

No rush.

No scramble. [scramble - схватка] 

No niggling attempts to make ends meet.

No wild effort to remember whether she hadn’t given the same kind of present to the same person two Christmases before—no mob  [mob - толпа] of last-minute shoppers—no dreary family “reunions” where she sat mute and unimportant—no attacks of “nerves. [nerves - нервы] ”

They decorated  [decorated - украшенный] the Blue Castle with pine  [pine - сосна] boughs, and Valancy made delightful little tinsel stars and hung them up amid  [amid - среди] the greenery.

She cooked a dinner to which Barney did full justice, while Good Luck and Banjo picked the bones.  

“A land that can produce a goose  [goose - гуся] like that is an admirable  [admirable - замечательный] land,” vowed  [vowed - поклялся] Barney.

“Canada forever!”

And they drank to the Union Jack a bottle of dandelion  [dandelion - одуванчик] wine that Cousin Georgiana had given Valancy along with the bedspread.  

“One never knows,” Cousin Georgiana had said solemnly, “when one may need a little stimulant. [stimulant - стимулятор] ”  

Barney had asked Valancy what she wanted for a Christmas present.  

“Something frivolous and unnecessary,” said Valancy, who had got a pair of goloshes last Christmas and two long-sleeved, woolen undervests the year before.

And so on back.  

To her delight, Barney gave her a necklace of pearl  [pearl - жемчуг] beads.

Valancy had wanted a string of milky pearl  [pearl - жемчуг] beads—like congealed moonshine—all her life.

And these were so pretty.

All that worried her was that they were really too good.

They must have cost a great deal—fifteen dollars, at least.

Could Barney afford that?

She didn’t know a thing about his finances. [finances - финансы] 

She had refused to let him buy any of her clothes—she had enough for that, she told him, as long as she would need clothes.

In a round, black jar on the chimney- [chimney - дымоход] piece Barney put money for their household expenses—always enough.

The jar was never empty, though Valancy never caught him replenishing it.

She would wear it and enjoy it.

It was the first pretty thing she had ever had.     

CHAPTER XXXII   New Year.

The old, shabby, [shabby - ветхий]  inglorious outlived  [outlived - пережил] calendar came down.

The new one went up.

January was a month of storms.

It snowed for three weeks on end.

The thermometer  [thermometer - термометр] went miles below zero and stayed there.

But, as Barney and Valancy pointed out to each other, there were no mosquitoes.

And the roar and crackle of their big fire drowned  [drowned - утонул] the howls of the north wind.

Good Luck and Banjo waxed fat  [fat - жирный] and developed resplendent  [resplendent - блестящий] coats of thick, silky fur.

Nip and Tuck had gone.  

“But they’ll come back in spring,” promised Barney.  

There was no monotony. [monotony - однообразие] 

Sometimes they had dramatic little private spats that never even thought of becoming quarrels. [quarrels - ссоры] 

Sometimes Roaring  [Roaring - ревущий] Abel dropped in—for an evening or a whole day—with his old tartan  [tartan - шотландка] cap and his long red beard coated with snow.

He generally brought his fiddle  [fiddle - скрипка] and played for them, to the delight of all except Banjo, who would go temporarily  [temporarily - временно] insane and retreat  [retreat - отступление] under Valancy’s bed.

Sometimes Abel and Barney talked while Valancy made candy for them; sometimes they sat and smoked in silence _à la_

Tennyson and Carlyle, until the Blue Castle reeked and Valancy fled  [fled - бежал] to the open.

Sometimes they played checkers fiercely  [fiercely - яростно] and silently the whole night through.

Sometimes they all ate the russet apples Abel had brought, while the jolly old clock ticked the delightful minutes away.  

Presumably  [Presumably - Предположительно] none of the Stirlings ever thought about her—except Cousin Georgiana, who used to lie awake worrying about poor Doss.

Did she have enough to eat?

Was that dreadful  [dreadful - ужасный] creature good to her?

Was she warm enough at nights?  

Valancy was quite warm at nights.

She used to wake up and revel  [revel - пик] silently in the cosiness of those winter nights on that little island in the frozen lake.

The nights of other winters had been so cold and long.

Valancy hated to wake up in them and think about the bleakness and emptiness  [emptiness - пустота]  [emptiness - пустота] of the day that had passed and the bleakness and emptiness of the day that would come.

Now, she almost counted that night lost on which she didn’t wake up and lie awake for half an hour just being happy, while Barney’s regular breathing went on beside her, and through the open door the smouldering brands in the fireplace  [fireplace - камин] winked at her in the gloom. [gloom - мрак] 

It was very nice to feel a little Lucky cat jump up on your bed in the darkness  [darkness - мрак] and snuggle down at your feet, purring; but Banjo would be sitting dourly by himself out in front of the fire like a brooding demon. [demon - демон] 

At such moments Banjo was anything but canny, but Valancy loved his uncanniness.  

The side of the bed had to be right against the window.

There was no other place for it in the tiny room.

Valancy, lying there, could look out of the window, through the big pine  [pine - сосна] boughs that actually touched it, away up Mistawis, white and lustrous as a pavement  [pavement - мостовая] of pearl, [pearl - жемчужина]  or dark and terrible in the storm.

Sometimes the pine  [pine - сосна] boughs tapped  [tapped - постучал] against the panes with friendly  [friendly - дружелюбно] signals.

Sometimes she heard the little hissing whisper of snow against them right at her side.

Valancy wasted many perfectly good sleeping hours in these delightful communings.

But she could sleep as long in the morning as she wanted to.

Nobody cared.

Barney cooked his own breakfast of bacon and eggs and then shut himself up in Bluebeard’s Chamber till supper time.

Then they had an evening of reading and talk.

They talked about everything in this world and a good many things in other worlds.

They laughed over their own jokes until the Blue Castles re-echoed.  

“You _do_ laugh beautifully,” Barney told her once.

“It makes me want to laugh just to hear you laugh.

There’s a trick about your laugh—as if there were so much more fun back of it that you wouldn’t let out.

Did you laugh like that before you came to Mistawis, Moonlight?”  

“I never laughed at all—really.

I used to giggle foolishly  [foolishly - глупо] when I felt I was expected to.

But now—the laugh just comes.”  

It struck Valancy more than once that Barney himself laughed a great deal oftener than he used to and that his laugh had changed.

It had become wholesome.

She rarely heard the little cynical  [cynical - цинично] note in it now.

Could a man laugh like that who had crimes on his conscience? [conscience - совесть] 

Yet Barney _must_ have done something.

Valancy had indifferently made up her mind as to what he had done.

She concluded he was a defaulting bank cashier. [cashier - кассир] 

She had found in one of Barney’s books an old clipping cut from a Montreal paper in which a vanished, [vanished - исчез]  defaulting cashier  [cashier - кассир] was described.

The description applied to Barney—as well as to half a dozen other men Valancy knew—and from some casual  [casual - случайно] remarks  [remarks - замечания] he had dropped from time to time she concluded he knew Montreal rather well.

Valancy had it all figured out in the back of her mind.

Barney had been in a bank.

He was tempted  [tempted - соблазн] to take some money to speculate— [speculate - спекулировать] meaning, of course, to put it back.

He had got in deeper  [deeper - глубже]  [deeper - глубже] and deeper, until he found there was nothing for it but flight.

It had happened so to scores of men.

He had, Valancy was absolutely certain, never meant to do wrong.

Of course, the name of the man in the clipping was Bernard Craig.

But Valancy had always thought Snaith was an alias.

Not that it mattered.  

Valancy had only one unhappy night that winter.

It came in late March when most of the snow had gone and Nip and Tuck had returned.

Barney had gone off in the afternoon for a long, woodland tramp, [tramp - бродяга]  saying he would be back by dark if all went well.

Soon after he had gone it had begun to snow.

The wind rose and presently  [presently - в настоящее время] Mistawis was in the grip  [grip - хватка] of one of the worst storms of the winter.

It tore up the lake and struck at the little house.

The trees on the island crouched in fear.

Valancy spent the night huddled on the rug  [rug - ковер] before the fire, her face buried in her hands, when she was not vainly peering from the oriel in a futile effort to see through the furious  [furious - яростный] smoke of wind and snow that had once been blue-dimpled Mistawis.

Where was Barney?

Lost on the merciless lakes?

Sinking exhausted in the drifts of the pathless woods?

Valancy died a hundred deaths that night and paid in full for all the happiness of her Blue Castle.

When morning came the storm broke and cleared; the sun shone gloriously over Mistawis; and at noon  [noon - полдень] Barney came home.

Valancy saw him from the oriel as he came around a wooded  [wooded - лесной] point, slender and black against the glistening white world.

She did not run to meet him.

Something happened to her knees and she dropped down on Banjo’s chair.

Luckily Banjo got out from under in time, his whiskers bristling with indignation.

Barney found her there, her head buried in her hands.  

“Barney, I thought you were dead,” she whispered. [whispered - шептал]   

Barney hooted.  

“After two years of the Klondike did you think a baby storm like this could get me?

I spent the night in that old lumber shanty over by Muskoka.

A bit cold but snug enough.

Little goose! [goose - гусь] 

Your eyes look like burnt holes in a blanket. [blanket - одеяло] 

Did you sit up here all night worrying over an old woodsman  [woodsman - лесник] like me?”  

“Yes,” said Valancy.

“I—couldn’t help it.

The storm seemed so wild.

Anybody might have been lost in it.

When—I saw you—come round the point—there—something happened to me.

I don’t know what.

It was as if I had died and come back to life.

I can’t describe it any other way.”     

CHAPTER XXXIII   Spring.

Mistawis black and sullen for a week or two, then flaming in sapphire and turquoise, lilac and rose again, laughing through the oriel, caressing its amethyst islands, rippling under winds soft as silk.
