Frogs, little green wizards of swamp and pool, singing everywhere in the long twilights and long into the nights; islands fairy-like in a green haze; the evanescent beauty of wild young trees in early leaf; frost-like loveliness  [loveliness - прелесть] of the new foliage of juniper-trees; the woods putting on a fashion of spring flowers, dainty, spiritual things akin  [akin - родственный] to the soul of the wilderness; [wilderness - пустыня]  red mist  [mist - туман] on the maples; willows decked out with glossy silver pussies; all the forgotten violets of Mistawis blooming  [blooming - цветение] again; lure  [lure - приманка] of April moons.  

“Think how many thousands of springs have been here on Mistawis—and all of them beautiful,” said Valancy.

“Oh, Barney, look at that wild plum! [plum - слива] 

The fingers of wood pixies must have woven  [woven - сотканный] it, for nothing like it ever came from an earthly loom.

I vow  [vow - клятва] the tree is conscious of its loveliness. [loveliness - красота] 

It is bridling before our very eyes—as if its beauty were not the most ephemeral thing in the woods, as it is the rarest and most exceeding, for today it is and tomorrow it is not.

Every south wind purring through the boughs will winnow away a shower of slender petals.

But what matter?

Today it is queen of the wild places and it is always today in the woods.’”  

“I’m sure you feel much better since you’ve got that out of your system,” said Barney heartlessly.  

“Here’s a patch  [patch - патч] of dandelions, [dandelions - одуванчики] ” said Valancy, unsubdued.

“Dandelions  [Dandelions - Одуванчики] shouldn’t grow in the woods, though.

They haven’t any sense of the fitness of things at all.

They are too cheerful and self-satisfied.

They haven’t any of the mystery and reserve of the real wood-flowers.”  

“In short, they’ve no secrets,” said Barney.

“But wait a bit.

The woods will have their own way even with those obvious dandelions. [dandelions - одуванчики] 

In a little while all that obtrusive yellowness and complacency will be gone and we’ll find here misty, phantom-like globes  [globes - глобусы] hovering  [hovering - парящие] over those long grasses in full harmony  [harmony - гармония] with the traditions of the forest.”  

“That sounds John Fosterish,” teased  [teased - дразнили] Valancy.  

“What have I done that deserved a slam like that?” complained Barney.  

One of the earliest signs of spring was the renaissance of Lady Jane.

Barney put her on roads that no other car would look at, and they went through Deerwood in mud to the axles.

They passed several Stirlings, who groaned and reflected that now spring was come they would encounter  [encounter - встреча] that shameless  [shameless - бесстыдные] pair everywhere.

There sat her mother and Cousin Stickles drearily, grimly knitting.

Baffling and inhuman  [inhuman - нечеловеческий] as ever.

If they had looked the least bit lonesome Valancy would have gone in.

But they did not.

Valancy would not disturb  [disturb - беспокоить] them for worlds.     

CHAPTER XXXIV   Valancy had two wonderful moments that spring.  

One day, coming home through the woods, with her arms full of trailing  [trailing - плетущийся] arbutus and creeping spruce, she met a man who she knew must be Allan Tierney.

Allan Tierney, the celebrated  [celebrated - прославленный] painter of beautiful women.

He lived in New York in winter, but he owned an island cottage  [cottage - коттеджный] at the northern end of Mistawis to which he always came the minute the ice was out of the lake.

He was reputed  [reputed - известный] to be a lonely, eccentric  [eccentric - чудаковатый] man.

He never flattered  [flattered - польщенный] his sitters.

There was no need  [need - нужда] to, for he would not paint any one who required flattery. [flattery - лесть] 

To be painted by Allan Tierney was all the _cachet_ of beauty a woman could desire. [desire - желание] 

Valancy had heard so much about him that she couldn’t help turning her head back over her shoulder for another shy, curious  [curious - любопытный] look at him.

A shaft of pale spring sunlight fell through a great pine  [pine - сосна] athwart her bare  [bare - голый] black head and her slanted eyes.

She wore a pale green sweater and had bound  [bound - связанный] a fillet of linnæa vine about her hair.

“Who?” Valancy was surprised but indifferent. [indifferent - безразличный] 

She began filling a basket  [basket - корзина] with arbutus.  

“Allan Tierney.

He wants to paint you, Moonlight.”  

“Me!”

Valancy dropped her basket  [basket - корзина] and her arbutus.

“You’re laughing at me, Barney.”  

“I’m not.

That’s what Tierney came for.

To ask my permission to paint my wife—as the Spirit of Muskoka, or something like that.”  

“But—but—” stammered  [stammered - заикался] Valancy, “Allan Tierney never paints any but—any but——”  “Beautiful women,” finished Barney.

“Conceded. [Conceded - уступал] 

Q. E. D., Mistress Barney Snaith is a beautiful woman.”  

“Nonsense, [Nonsense - ерунда] ” said Valancy, stooping to retrieve  [retrieve - извлекал] her arbutus.

“You _know_

that’s nonsense, [nonsense - ерунда]  Barney.

I know I’m a heap  [heap - куча] better-looking than I was a year ago, but I’m not beautiful.”  

“Allan Tierney never makes a mistake,” said Barney.

“You forget, Moonlight, that there are different kinds of beauty.

Your imagination is obsessed  [obsessed - одержимый] by the very obvious type of your cousin Olive.

Oh, I’ve seen her—she’s a stunner—but you’d never catch Allan Tierney wanting to paint her.

In the horrible but expressive  [expressive - выразительный] slang  [slang - сленг] phrase, she keeps all her goods  [goods - добро] in the shop-window.

But in your subconscious mind you have a conviction  [conviction - убеждение] that nobody can be beautiful who doesn’t look like Olive.

Also, you remember your face as it was in the days when your soul was not allowed to shine through it.

Tierney said something about the curve of your cheek  [cheek - щека] as you looked back over your shoulder.

“Well, I don’t want to be painted,” said Valancy.

“I hope you told him that.”  

“I couldn’t tell him that.

I didn’t know what _you_ wanted.

But I told him _

I_ didn’t want my wife painted—hung up in a salon  [salon - салон] for the mob  [mob - толпа] to stare at.

Belonging to another man.

For of course I couldn’t buy the picture.

So even if you had wanted to be painted, Moonlight, your tyrannous husband would not have permitted it.

Tierney was a bit squiffy.

He isn’t used to being turned down like that.

His requests are almost like royalty’ [royalty - королевство] s.”  

“But we are outlaws,” laughed Valancy.

“We bow  [bow - лук] to no decrees—we acknowledge no sovereignty. [sovereignty - суверенитет] ”  

In her heart she thought unashamedly: [unashamedly - нагло]   “I wish Olive could know that Allan Tierney wanted to paint me.

_

Me_!

Little-old-maid-Valancy-Stirling-that-was.”  

Her second wonder-moment came one evening in May.

She realised that Barney actually liked her.

She had always hoped he did, but sometimes she had a little, disagreeable, [disagreeable - неприятный]  haunting  [haunting - преследующий] dread that he was just kind and nice and chummy out of pity; [pity - жалость]  knowing that she hadn’t long to live and determined she should have a good time as long as she did live; but away back in his mind rather looking forward to freedom again, with no intrusive woman creature in his island fastness and no chattering thing beside him in his woodland prowls.

She knew he could never love her.

She did not even want him to.

If he loved her he would be unhappy when she died—Valancy never flinched from the plain word.

No “passing away” for her.

And she did not want him to be the least unhappy.

But neither did she want him to be glad—or relieved. [relieved - облегченный] 

She wanted him to like her and miss her as a good chum.

But she had never been sure until this night that he did.  

They had walked over the hills in the sunset.

They had the delight of discovering a virgin spring in a ferny hollow  [hollow - полый] and had drunk together from it out of a birch-bark cup; they had come to an old tumble-down rail  [rail - рельс] fence and sat on it for a long time.

They didn’t talk much, but Valancy had a curious  [curious - любопытный] sense of _oneness_.

She knew that she couldn’t have felt that if he hadn’t liked her.  

“You nice little thing,” said Barney suddenly.

“Oh, you nice little thing!

Sometimes I feel you’re too nice to be real—that I’m just dreaming you.”  

“Why can’t I die now—this very minute—when I am so happy!” thought Valancy.  

Well, it couldn’t be so very long now.

Somehow, Valancy had always felt she would live out the year Dr. Trent had allotted.

She had not been careful—she had never tried to be.

But, somehow, she had always counted on living out her year.

She had not let herself think about it at all.

But now, sitting here beside Barney, with her hand in his, a sudden realisation came to her.

She had not had a heart attack for a long while—two months at least.

The last one she had had was two or three nights before Barney was out in the storm.

Since then she had not remembered she had a heart.

Well, no doubt, it betokened the nearness of the end.

Nature had given up the struggle.

There would be no more pain.  

“I’m afraid heaven will be very dull  [dull - скучный] after this past year,” thought Valancy.

“But perhaps one will not remember.

Would that be—nice?

No, no.

I don’t want to forget Barney.

I’d rather be miserable  [miserable - жалкий] in heaven remembering him than happy forgetting him.

And I’ll always remember through all eternity—that he really, _really_ liked me.”     

CHAPTER XXXV   

Thirty seconds can be very long sometimes.

In thirty seconds life changed wholly  [wholly - полностью] for Barney and Valancy Snaith.  

They had gone around the lake one June evening in their disappearing propeller, fished for an hour in a little creek, [creek - ручей]  left their boat there, and walked up through the woods to Port Lawrence two miles away.

Valancy prowled  [prowled - бродил] a bit in the shops and got herself a new pair of sensible  [sensible - разумный] shoes.

Her old pair had suddenly and completely given out, and this evening she had been compelled  [compelled - вынужденный] to put on the little fancy pair of patent- [patent - патент] leather with rather high, slender heels, which she had bought in a fit  [fit - подгонка] of folly  [folly - глупость] one day in the winter because of their beauty and because she wanted to make one foolish, extravagant purchase  [purchase - покупка] in her life.

She sometimes put them on of an evening in the Blue Castle, but this was the first time she had worn them outside.

She had not found it any too easy walking up through the woods in them, and Barney guyed her unmercifully about them.

But in spite  [spite - назло] of the inconvenience, Valancy secretly rather liked the look of her trim ankles and high instep above those pretty, foolish shoes and did not change them in the shop as she might have done.  

The sun was hanging low above the pines  [pines - сосны] when they left Port Lawrence.

To the north of it the woods closed around the town quite suddenly.

Valancy always had a sense of stepping from one world to another—from reality to fairyland—when she went out of Port Lawrence and in a twinkling found it shut off behind her by the armies of the pines. [pines - сосны]   

A mile and a half from Port Lawrence there was a small railroad station with a little station-house which at this hour of the day was deserted, [deserted - пустынный]  since no local train was due.

Not a soul was in sight when Barney and Valancy emerged from the woods.

Off to the left a sudden curve in the track hid it from view, but over the tree-tops beyond, the long plume of smoke betokened the approach of a through train.

The rails  [rails - рельсы] were vibrating  [vibrating - вибрирующий] to its thunder as Barney stepped across the switch.

She could never tell how it happened.

The ensuing  [ensuing - последующий] thirty seconds always seemed in her recollection  [recollection - воспоминание] like a chaotic  [chaotic - хаотический] nightmare  [nightmare - кошмар] in which she endured  [endured - пережитый] the agony  [agony - агония] of a thousand lifetimes. [lifetimes - жизни]   

The heel of her pretty, foolish shoe caught in a crevice of the switch.

She could not pull it loose.  

“Barney—Barney!”

she called in alarm.  

Barney turned—saw her predicament— [predicament - затруднительное положение] saw her ashen face—dashed  [dashed - разбитый] back.

He tried to pull her clear—he tried to wrench  [wrench - гаечный ключ] her foot from the prisoning hold.

In vain.

In a moment the train would sweep  [sweep - разметать] around the curve—would be on them.  

“Go—go—quick—you’ll be killed, Barney!” shrieked Valancy, trying to push him away.  

Barney dropped on his knees, ghost-white, frantically  [frantically - лихорадочно] tearing at her shoe-lace. [lace - кружево] 

The knot  [knot - узел] defied  [defied - бросил вызов] his trembling fingers.

Her mind was full of the hideous thought that Barney was going to be killed.

She had no thought for her own danger.  

“Barney—go—go—for God’s sake— [sake - саке] go!”  

“Never!” muttered  [muttered - бормотал] Barney between his set teeth.

He gave one mad wrench at the lace. [lace - кружево] 

As the train thundered around the curve he sprang up and caught Valancy—dragging her clear, leaving the shoe behind her.

The wind from the train as it swept  [swept - пронесло] by turned to icy  [icy - ледяной] cold the streaming perspiration  [perspiration - пот] on his face.  

“Thank God!”

he breathed.  

For a moment they stood stupidly staring at each other, two white, shaken, [shaken - потрясенный]  wild-eyed creatures.

Then they stumbled  [stumbled - спотыкался] over to the little seat at the end of the station-house and dropped on it.

Barney buried his face in his hands and said not a word.

Valancy sat, staring straight ahead of her with unseeing eyes at the great pine woods, the stumps of the clearing, the long, gleaming rails. [rails - рельсы] 

There was only one thought in her dazed mind—a thought that seemed to burn it as a shaving of fire might burn her body.  

Dr. Trent had told her over a year ago that she had a serious form of heart-disease—that any excitement might be fatal. [fatal - смертельный]   

If that were so, why was she not dead now?

This very minute?

She had just experienced as much and as terrible excitement as most people experience in a lifetime, [lifetime - на всю жизнь]  crowded into that endless  [endless - бесконечный] thirty seconds.

Yet she had not died of it.

She was not an iota the worse for it.

A little wobbly at the knees, as any one would have been; a quicker heart-beat, [beat - бит]  as any one would have; nothing more.  

Why!  _Was it possible Dr. Trent had made a mistake?_  

Valancy shivered as if a cold wind had suddenly chilled  [chilled - охлажденный] her to the soul.

She looked at Barney, hunched up beside her.

His silence was very eloquent. [eloquent - красноречивый] 

Had the same thought occurred to him?

Valancy turned sick before the horror of it.

It could not be.

It would be too cruel—too devilish.

Dr. Trent _couldn’t_ have made a mistake.

Impossible.

He was one of the best heart specialists in Ontario.

She was foolish—unnerved by the recent horror.

She remembered some of the hideous spasms of pain she had had.

There must be something serious the matter with her heart to account for them.  

But she had not had any for nearly three months.  

Why?  Presently  [Presently - В настоящее время] Barney bestirred himself.

He stood up, without looking at Valancy, and said casually: [casually - небрежно]   “I suppose we’d better be hiking back.

Sun’s getting low.

Are you good for the rest of the road?”  

“I think so,” said Valancy miserably. [miserably - жалко]   

Barney went across the clearing and picked up the parcel he had dropped—the parcel containing her new shoes.

He brought it to her and let her take out the shoes and put them on without any assistance, [assistance - помощь]  while he stood with his back to her and looked out over the pines. [pines - сосны]   

They walked in silence down the shadowy trail to the lake.

In silence Barney steered  [steered - управляемые] his boat into the sunset miracle  [miracle - чудо] that was Mistawis.

In silence they went around feathery headlands and across coral bays  [bays - бухты] and silver rivers where canoes  [canoes - каноэ] were slipping up and down in the afterglow.

In silence they went past cottages  [cottages - коттеджи] echoing with music and laughter.

In silence drew up at the landing- [landing - приземление] place below the Blue Castle.  

Valancy went up the rock steps and into the house.

Valancy would have given her year of happiness to have been able honestly to answer “Yes.”  

“No,” she said flatly.  

Barney went into Bluebeard’s Chamber and shut the door.

She heard him pacing  [pacing - темп] up and down—up and down.

He had never paced  [paced - шаг] like that before.  

And an hour ago—only an hour ago—she had been so happy!     

CHAPTER XXXVI   

Finally Valancy went to bed.

Before she went she re-read Dr. Trent’s letter.

It comforted her a little.

So positive.

So assured.

The writing so black and steady.

Not the writing of a man who didn’t know what he was writing about.

But she could not sleep.

She pretended to be asleep when Barney came in.

Barney pretended to go to sleep.

But Valancy knew perfectly well he wasn’t sleeping any more than she was.

She knew he was lying there, staring through the darkness. [darkness - тьма] 

Thinking of what?

Trying to face—what?  Valancy, who had spent so many happy wakeful hours of night lying by that window, now paid the price of them all in this one night of misery. [misery - страдание] 

A horrible, portentous fact was slowly looming  [looming - вырисовывающийся] out before her from the nebula of surmise and fear.

She could not shut her eyes to it—push it away—ignore it.  

There could be nothing seriously wrong with her heart, no matter what Dr. Trent had said.

If there had been, those thirty seconds would have killed her.

It was no use to recall Dr. Trent’s letter and reputation.

The greatest specialists made mistakes sometimes.

Dr. Trent had made one.  

Towards morning Valancy fell into a fitful dose  [dose - доза] with ridiculous  [ridiculous - нелепый] dreams.

One of them was of Barney taunting her with having tricked  [tricked - обманутый] him.

In her dream she lost her temper and struck him violently on the head with her rolling-pin. [pin - булавка] 

He proved to be made of glass and shivered into splinters all over the floor.

She woke with a cry of horror—a gasp  [gasp - удушье] of relief— [relief - облегчение] a short laugh over the absurdity of her dream—a miserable  [miserable - жалкое] sickening recollection  [recollection - воспоминание] of what had happened.  

Barney was gone.

Valancy knew, as people sometimes know things—inescapably, without being told—that he was not in the house or in Bluebeard’s Chamber either.

There was a curious  [curious - любопытный] silence in the living- [living - живой] room.

A silence with something uncanny about it.

The old clock had stopped.

So Barney had betaken himself to the wilds.

He would not return till night—perhaps not even then.

He must be angry with her.

That furious  [furious - яростный] silence of his must mean anger—cold, deep, [deep - глубокий]  justifiable resentment. [resentment - обида] 

Well, Valancy knew what she must do first.

She was not suffering very keenly  [keenly - остро] now.

Yet the curious  [curious - любопытный] numbness that pervaded her being was in a way worse than pain.

It was as if something in her had died.

She forced  [forced - вынужденный] herself to cook and eat a little breakfast.

Mechanically  [Mechanically - Механически] she put the Blue Castle in perfect order.

Then she put on her hat and coat, locked the door and hid the key in the hollow  [hollow - полый] of the old pine  [pine - сосна] and crossed to the mainland  [mainland - материк] in the motor boat.

She was going into Deerwood to see Dr. Trent.

She must _know_.     

CHAPTER XXXVII   Dr. Trent looked at her blankly  [blankly - тупо] and fumbled among his recollections. [recollections - воспоминания]   

“Er—Miss—Miss—”  “Mrs. Snaith,” said Valancy quietly.

“I was Miss Valancy Stirling when I came to you last May—over a year ago.

I wanted to consult  [consult - советоваться] you about my heart.”  

Dr. Trent’s face cleared.  

“Oh, of course.

I remember now.

I’m really not to blame for not knowing you.

You’ve changed—splendidly.

And married.

Well, well, it has agreed with you.

You don’t look much like an invalid  [invalid - неверный] now, hey?

I remember that day.

I was badly upset.

Hearing about poor Ned bowled me over.

But Ned’s as good as new and you, too, evidently. [evidently - очевидно] 

I told you so, you know—told you there was nothing to worry over.”  

Valancy looked at him.  

“I couldn’t have told you that!”  

Valancy took his letter from her bag and handed it to him.  

“Miss Valancy Stirling,” he read.

“Yes—yes.

Of course I wrote you—on the train—that night.

But I _told_

you there was nothing serious——”  “Read your letter,” insisted Valancy.  

Dr. Trent took it out—unfolded  [unfolded - развернулся] it—glanced over it.

A dismayed look came into his face.

He jumped to his feet and strode  [strode - шагал] agitatedly about the room.  

“Good heavens!

This is the letter I meant for old Miss Jane Sterling.

From Port Lawrence.

She was here that day, too.

I sent you the wrong letter.

What unpardonable carelessness!

But I was beside myself that night.

My God, and you believed that—you believed—but you didn’t—you went to another doctor——”  Valancy stood up, turned round, looked foolishly  [foolishly - глупо] about her and sat down again.  

“I believed it,” she said faintly. [faintly - слабо] 

“I didn’t go to any other doctor.

I—I—it would take too long to explain.

But I believed I was going to die soon.”  

Dr. Trent halted before her.  

“I can never forgive myself.

What a year you must have had!

But you don’t look—I can’t understand!”  

“Never mind,” said Valancy dully.

“And so there’s nothing the matter with my heart?”  

“Well, nothing serious.

You had what is called pseudo-angina.

It’s never fatal— [fatal - фатально] passes away completely with proper treatment.

Or sometimes with a shock of joy.

Have you been troubled much with it?”  

“Not at all since March,” answered Valancy.

She remembered the marvellous feeling of re-creation  [creation - творение] she had had when she saw Barney coming home safe after the storm.

Had that “shock of joy” cured  [cured - вылечил] her?  

“Then likely you’re all right.

I told you what to do in the letter you should have got.

_

And_ of course I supposed you’d go to another doctor.

Child, why didn’t you?”  “I didn’t want anybody to know.”  

“Idiot, [Idiot - Идиот] ” said Dr. Trent bluntly. [bluntly - прямо] 

“I can’t understand such folly. [folly - глупость] 

And poor old Miss Sterling.

She must have got your letter—telling her there was nothing serious the matter.

Well, well, it couldn’t have made any difference.

Her case was hopeless.

Nothing that she could have done or left undone could have made any difference.

I was surprised she lived as long as she did—two months.

She was here that day—not long before you.

I hated to tell her the truth.

You think I’m a blunt old curmudgeon—and my letters _are_ blunt enough.

I can’t soften  [soften - смягчил] things.

But I’m a snivelling coward  [coward - трус] when it comes to telling a woman face to face that she’s got to die soon.

I told her I’d look up some features of the case I wasn’t quite sure of and let her know next day.

But you got her letter—look here, ‘Dear Miss S-t-_e_-r-l-i-n-g.’”  “Yes.

I noticed that.

But I thought it a mistake.

I didn’t know there were any Sterlings in Port Lawrence.”  

“She was the only one.

A lonely old soul.

Lived by herself with only a little home girl.

She died two months after she was here—died in her sleep.

My mistake couldn’t have made any difference to her.

But you!

I can’t forgive myself for inflicting  [inflicting - причинил] a year’s misery  [misery - страдание] on you.

It’s time I retired, all right, when I do things like that—even if my son was supposed to be fatally injured.

Can you ever forgive me?”  

A year of misery! [misery - страдание] 

Valancy smiled a tortured smile as she thought of all the happiness Dr. Trent’s mistake had bought her.

But she was paying for it now—

oh, she was paying.

If to feel was to live she was living with a vengeance.  

She let Dr. Trent examine her and answered all his questions.

Anybody would have thought, from her hopeless eyes and woebegone face, that he had given her a sentence of death instead of life.

Snaith? Snaith?

Who the devil  [devil - черт] had she married?

He had never heard of Snaiths in Deerwood.

And she had been such a sallow, faded, [faded - поблек]  little old maid.

Gad, but marriage _had_ made a difference in her, anyhow, whoever Snaith was.

Snaith?

Dr. Trent remembered.

That rapscallion “up back!”

Had Valancy Stirling married _him_?

And her clan  [clan - клан] had let her!

Well, probably that solved the mystery.

She had married in haste  [haste - поспешность] and repented at leisure, and that was why she wasn’t overjoyed at learning she was a good insurance prospect, [prospect - перспектива]  after all.

Married!

To God knew whom!

Or what!

Jail-bird?

Defaulter?

Fugitive from justice?

It must be pretty bad if she had looked to death as a release, poor girl.

But why were women such fools? [fools - дураков] 

Dr. Trent dismissed  [dismissed - уволили] Valancy from his mind, though to the day of his death he was ashamed of putting those letters into the wrong envelopes. [envelopes - конверты]      

CHAPTER XXXVIII   Valancy walked quickly through the back streets and through Lover’s Lane.

She did not want to meet any one she knew.

She didn’t want to meet even people she didn’t know.

She hated to be seen.

Her mind was so confused, so torn, so messy.

She felt that her appearance must be the same.

She drew a sobbing  [sobbing - рыдания] breath of relief  [relief - облегчение] as she left the village behind and found herself on the “up back” road.

There was little fear of meeting any one she knew here.

The cars that fled  [fled - бежали] by her with raucous shrieks were filled with strangers.

On [O - О] e of them was packed with young people who whirled past her singing uproariously:  “My wife has the fever, [fever - лихорадка]  [fever - лихорадка]  O then, My wife has the fever,

She had made a covenant with death and death had cheated  [cheated - обманутый] her.

Now life stood mocking her.

She had trapped Barney.

Trapped him into marrying her.

And divorce  [divorce - развод] was so hard to get in Ontario.

So expensive.

And Barney was poor.  

With life, fear had come back into her heart.

Sickening fear.

Fear of what Barney would think.

Would say.

Fear of the future that must be lived without him.

Fear of her insulted, [insulted - обиженный]  repudiated clan. [clan - родовой]   

She had had one draught from a divine  [divine - божественный] cup and now it was dashed  [dashed - накатанный] from her lips.

With no kind, friendly  [friendly - дружеский] death to rescue her.

She must go on living  [living - живой] and longing for it.

Everything was spoiled, [spoiled - испорченный]  smirched, defaced.

Even that year in the Blue Castle.

Even her unashamed love for Barney.

It had been beautiful because death waited.

Now it was only sordid because death was gone.

How could any one bear an unbearable  [unbearable - невыносимый] thing?  

She must go back and tell him.

Make him believe she had not meant to trick  [trick - хитрость] him—she _must_ make him believe that.

She must say good-bye to her Blue Castle and return to the brick  [brick - кирпич] house on Elm Street.

Back to everything she had thought left behind forever.

The old bondage—the old fears.

But that did not matter.

All that mattered now was that Barney must somehow be made to believe she had not consciously  [consciously - сознательно] tricked  [tricked - обманутый] him.  

A wonderful car.

A purple car.

Not a dark, royal purple but a blatant, screaming purple.

It shone like a mirror and its interior plainly  [plainly - прямо] indicated the car caste of Vere de Vere.

On the driver’s seat sat a haughty chauffeur in livery.

And in the tonneau sat a man who opened the door and bounced  [bounced - подпрыгнул] out nimbly as Valancy came down the path to the landing- [landing - приземлился] place.

He stood under the pines  [pines - сосны] waiting for her and Valancy took in every detail of him.  

A stout, short, pudgy man, with a broad, rubicund, good-humoured face—a clean-shaven face, though an unparalysed little imp  [imp - черенок] at the back of Valancy’s paralysed mind suggested the thought, “Such a face should have a fringe of white whisker around it.”

Old-fashioned, steel-rimmed spectacles  [spectacles - очки] on prominent  [prominent - выдающийся] blue eyes.

A pursey mouth; a little round, knobby nose.

Where—where—where, groped Valancy, had she seen that face before?

It seemed as familiar to her as her own.  

The stranger wore a green hat and a light fawn overcoat  [overcoat - шинель] over a suit of a loud check pattern.

His tie was a brilliant green of lighter shade; on the plump  [plump - пухлый] hand he outstretched to intercept Valancy an enormous diamond winked at her.

But he had a pleasant, fatherly smile, and in his hearty, unmodulated voice was a ring of something that attracted her.  

“Can you tell me, Miss, if that house yonder belongs to a Mr. Redfern?

And if so, how can I get to it?”  

Redfern!

A vision of bottles seemed to dance before Valancy’s eyes—long bottles of bitters—round bottles of hair tonic—square bottles of liniment—short, corpulent little bottles of purple pills— [pills - таблетки] and all of them bearing that very prosperous, beaming moon-face and steel-rimmed spectacles  [spectacles - очки] on the label. [label - этикетка]   

Dr. Redfern!  

“No,” said Valancy faintly. [faintly - слабо] 

“No—that house belongs to Mr. Snaith.”  

Dr. Redfern nodded. [nodded - кивал]   

“Yes, I understand Bernie’s been calling himself Snaith.

Well, it’s his middle name—was his poor mother’s.

Bernard Snaith Redfern—that’s him.

And now, Miss, you can tell me how to get over to that island?

Nobody seems to be home there.

I’ve done some waving and yelling. [yelling - орать] 

Raised nothing but a couple of crows.

Guess Bernie’s out for the day.”  

“He was away when I left this morning,” said Valancy.

“I suppose he hasn’t come home yet.”  

She spoke flatly and tonelessly.

This last shock had temporarily  [temporarily - временно] bereft her of whatever little power of reasoning  [reasoning - рассуждение] had been left her by Dr. Trent’s revelation. [revelation - откровение] 

In the back of her mind the aforesaid little imp  [imp - черенок] was jeeringly repeating a silly old proverb, “It never rains but it pours.”

But she was not trying to think.

What was the use?  

Dr. Redfern was gazing at her in perplexity.  

“When you left this morning?

Do you live—over there?”  

He waved his diamond at the Blue Castle.  

“Of course,” said Valancy stupidly.

“I’m his wife.”  

Dr. Redfern took out a yellow silk handkerchief, removed his hat and mopped his brow.

He was very bald, and Valancy’s imp  [imp - черенок] whispered, [whispered - шептал]  “Why be bald?

Why lose your manly beauty?

Try Redfern’s Hair Vigor.

It keeps you young.”  

“Excuse me,” said Dr. Redfern.

“This is a bit of a shock.”  

“Shocks seem to be in the air this morning.”

The imp  [imp - черенок] said this out loud before Valancy could prevent it.  

“I didn’t know Bernie was—married.

I didn’t think he _would_ have got married without telling his old dad.”  Were Dr. Redfern’s eyes misty?

Amid  [Amid - Среди] her own dull  [dull - унылых] ache of misery  [misery - страданий] and fear and dread, Valancy felt a pang of pity  [pity - жалости] for him.  

“Don’t blame him,” she said hurriedly.

“It—it wasn’t his fault.

It—was all my doing.”  

“You didn’t ask him to marry you, I suppose,” twinkled Dr. Redfern.

“He might have let me know.

I’d have got acquainted with my daughter-in-law before this if he had.

But I’m glad to meet you now, my dear—very glad.

You look like a sensible  [sensible - разумного] young woman.

I used to sorter fear  [fear - страха] Barney’d pick out some pretty bit of fluff just because she was good-looking.

They were all after him, of course.

Wanted his money?

Eh?

Didn’t like the pills  [pills - таблетки] and the bitters but liked the dollars.

Eh? Wanted to dip  [dip - окунают] their pretty little fingers in old Doc’s millions.

“Millions,” said Dr. Redfern complacently.

“And Bernie chucks them for—that.”

Again he shook the diamond contemptuously at the Blue Castle.

“Wouldn’t you think he’d have more sense?

And all on account of a white bit of a girl.

He must have got over _that_ feeling, anyhow, since he’s married.

You must persuade him to come back to civilisation.

All nonsense  [nonsense - бред] wasting his life like this.

Ain’t you going to take me over to your house, my dear? [dear - дорогой] 

I suppose you’ve some way of getting there.”  

“Of course,” said Valancy stupidly.

She led the way down to the little cove where the disappearing propeller boat was snuggled.  

“Does your—your man want to come, too?”  

“Who?

Henry.

Not he.

Look at him sitting there disapproving. [disapproving - неодобрительный] 

Disapproves of the whole expedition. [expedition - экспедиция] 

The trail up from the road nearly gave him a conniption.

Well, it _was_ a devilish road to put a car on.

Whose old bus is that up there?”  

“Barney’s.”  “Good Lord!

Does Bernie Redfern ride in a thing like that?

It looks like the great-great-grand-mother of all the Fords.”  

“It isn’t a Ford.

It’s a Grey Slosson,” said Valancy spiritedly.

For some occult reason, Dr. Redfern’s good-humoured ridicule of dear old Lady Jane stung her to life.

A life that was all pain but still _life_.

Better than the horrible half-dead-and-half-aliveness of the past few minutes—or years.

She waved Dr. Redfern curtly into the boat and took him over to the Blue Castle.

The key was still in the old pine— [pine - сосна] the house still silent and deserted.

Valancy took the doctor through the living- [living - живой] room to the western  [western - западный] verandah. [verandah - веранда] 

She must at least be out where there was air.

It was still sunny, but in the southwest a great thundercloud, with white crests and gorges  [gorges - ущелья] of purple shadow, was slowly rising over Mistawis.

The doctor dropped with a gasp  [gasp - вздох] on a rustic chair and mopped his brow again.  

“Warm, eh?

Lord, what a view!

Wonder if it would soften  [soften - смягчить] Henry if he could see it.”  “Have you had dinner?” asked Valancy.  

“Yes, my dear— [dear - дорогой] had it before we left Port Lawrence.

Didn’t know what sort of wild hermit’s hollow  [hollow - полый] we were coming to, you see.

Hadn’t any idea I was going to find a nice little daughter-in-law here all ready to toss  [toss - швырять] me up a meal.

Cats, eh?

Puss, puss!

See that.

Cats love me.

Bernie was always fond  [fond - любить] of cats!

It’s about the only thing he took from me.

He’s his poor mother’s boy.”  

She had remained standing by the steps, but Dr. Redfern waved her to the swing  [swing - качели] seat.  

“Sit down, dear.

Never stand when you can sit.

I want to get a good look at Barney’s wife.

Well, well, I like your face.

No beauty—you don’t mind my saying that—you’ve sense  [sense - чувство] enough to know it, I reckon. [reckon - рассчитываться] 

Sit down.”  

Valancy sat down.

To be obliged  [obliged - обязан] to sit still when mental agony  [agony - агония] urges  [urges - побуждения] us to stride  [stride - шаг] up and down is the refinement of torture. [torture - пытка] 

Every nerve  [nerve - нерв] in her being was crying out to be alone—to be hidden.

But she had to sit and listen to Dr. Redfern, who didn’t mind talking at all.  

“When do you think Bernie will be back?”  

“I don’t know—not before night probably.”  

“Where did he go?”  

“I don’t know that either.

Likely to the woods—up back.”  

“So he doesn’t tell you his comings and goings, either?

Bernie was always a secretive young devil. [devil - дьявол] 

Never understood him.

Just like his poor mother.

But I thought a lot of him.

It hurt me when he disappeared as he did.

Eleven years ago.

I haven’t seen my boy for eleven years.”  

“Eleven years.”

Valancy was surprised.

“It’s only six since he came here.”  

“Oh, he was in the Klondike before that—and all over the world.

He used to drop me a line now and then—never give any clue to where he was but just a line to say he was all right.

I s’pose he’s told you all about it.”  

“No.

I know nothing of his past life,” said Valancy with sudden eagerness.

She wanted to know—she must know now.

It hadn’t mattered before.

Now she must know all.

And she could never hear it from Barney.

She might never even see him again.

If she did, it would not be to talk of his past.  

“What happened?

Why did he leave his home?

Tell me.

Tell me.”  

“Well, it ain’t much of a story.

Just a young fool  [fool - дурак] gone mad because of a quarrel  [quarrel - ссора] with his girl.

Only Bernie was a stubborn fool. [fool - дурак] 

Always stubborn.

You never could make that boy do anything he didn’t want to do.

From the day he was born.

Yet he was always a quiet, gentle little chap, [chap - парень]  too.

Good as gold.

His poor mother died when he was only two years old.

I’d just begun to make money with my Hair Vigor.

I’d dreamed the formula  [formula - формула] for it, you see.

Some dream that.

Wanted me to buy a paper for him—or back him in publishing what he called a ‘real, worthwhile, [worthwhile - стоящее]  honest-to-goodness  [goodness - добро] Canadian Magazine.’

I s’pose I’d have done it—I always did what he wanted me to do.

Wasn’t he all I had to live for?

I wanted him to be happy.

And he never was happy.

Can you believe it?

Not that he said so.

But I’d always a feeling that he wasn’t happy.

Everything he wanted—all the money he could spend—his own bank account—travel—seeing the world—but he wasn’t happy.

Not till he fell in love with Ethel Traverse.

Then he was happy for a little while.”  

The cloud had reached the sun and a great, chill, [chill - холод]  purple shadow came swiftly  [swiftly - стремительно] over Mistawis.

It touched the Blue Castle—rolled  [rolled - катился] over it.

Valancy shivered.  

“Yes,” she said, with painful eagerness, though every word was cutting her to the heart.

“What—was—she—like?”  

“Prettiest girl in Montreal,” said Dr. Redfern.

“Oh, she was a looker, all right.

Eh?

Gold hair—shiny as silk—great, big, soft, black eyes—skin like milk and roses. [roses - розы] 

Don’t wonder Bernie fell for her.

And brains as well.

_

She_ wasn’t a bit of fluff.

B. A. from McGill.

A thoroughbred, too.

One of the best families.

But a bit lean in the purse.

Eh! Bernie was mad about her.

Happiest young fool  [fool - дурак] you ever saw.

Then—the bust-up.”  

“What happened?”

Valancy had taken off her hat and was absently thrusting a pin  [pin - булавка] in and out of it.

Good Luck was purring beside her.

Banjo was regarding Dr. Redfern with suspicion. [suspicion - подозрение] 

Nip and Tuck were lazily cawing in the pines. [pines - сосны] 

Mistawis was beckoning.

Everything was the same.

Nothing was the same.

It was a hundred years since yesterday.

Yesterday, at this time, she and Barney had been eating a belated dinner here with laughter.

Laughter?

Valancy felt that she had done with laughter forever.

And with tears, for that matter.

She had no further use for either of them.  

“Blest if I know, my dear. [dear - дорогой] 

Some fool  [fool - дурак] quarrel, [quarrel - ссора]  I suppose.

Bernie just lit out—disappeared.

He wrote me from the Yukon.

Said his engagement  [engagement - помолвка] was broken and he wasn’t coming back.

And not to try to hunt him up because he was never coming back.

I didn’t.

What was the use?

I knew Bernie.

I went on piling, up money because there wasn’t anything else to do.

But I was mighty lonely.

All I lived for was them little notes now and then from Bernie—Klondike—England—South Africa—China—everywhere.

I thought maybe he’d come back some day to his lonesome old dad.

Then six years ago even the letters stopped.

I didn’t hear a word of or from him till last Christmas.”  

“Did he write?”  

“No.

But he drew a check for fifteen thousand dollars on his bank account.

The bank manager is a friend of mine—one of my biggest shareholders. [shareholders - акционеры] 

He’d always promised me he’d let me know if Bernie drew any checks.

Bernie had fifty thousand there.

And he’d never touched a cent of it till last Christmas.

The check was made out to Aynsley’s, Toronto——”  “Aynsley’s?”

Valancy heard herself saying Aynsley’s!

Drawing that fifteen told me there was something in the wind.

The manager communicated with the Aynsleys—his wife was an Aynsley—and found out that Bernard Redfern had bought a pearl  [pearl - жемчужина] necklace there.

His address was given as Box 444, Port Lawrence, Muskoka, Ont.

First I thought I’d write.

Then I thought I’d wait till the open season for cars and come down myself.

Ain’t no hand at writing.

I’ve motored from Montreal.

Got to Port Lawrence yesterday.

Enquired at the post-office.

Told me they knew nothing of any Bernard Snaith Redfern, but there was a Barney Snaith had a P. O. box there.

Lived on an island out here, they said.

So here I am.

And where’s Barney?”  Valancy was fingering her necklace.

She was wearing fifteen thousand dollars around her neck.

And she had worried lest Barney had paid fifteen dollars for it and couldn’t afford it.

Suddenly she laughed in Dr. Redfern’s face.  

“Excuse me.

It’s so—amusing, [amusing - забавный] ” said poor Valancy.  

“Isn’t it?” said Dr. Redfern, seeing a joke—but not exactly hers.

“Now, you seem like a sensible  [sensible - разумный] young woman, and I dare  [dare - смеется] say you’ve lots of influence over Bernie.

Can’t you get him to come back to civilisation and live like other people?

I’ve a house up there.

Big as a castle.

Furnished like a palace.

I want company in it—Bernie’s wife—Bernie’s children.”  

“Did Ethel Traverse ever marry?” queried  [queried - вопрошает] Valancy irrelevantly.  

“Bless  [Bless - благослови] you, yes.

Two years after Bernie levanted.

But she’s a widow  [widow - вдова] now.

Pretty as ever.

To be frank, that was my special reason for wanting to find Bernie.

I thought they’d make it up, maybe.

But, of course, that’s all off now.

Doesn’t matter.

Bernie’s choice of a wife is good enough for me.

It’s my boy I want.

Think he’ll soon be back?”  

“I don’t know.

But I don’t think he’ll come before night.

Quite late, perhaps.

And perhaps not till tomorrow.

But I can put you up comfortably. [comfortably - спокойно] 

He’ll certainly be back tomorrow.”  

Dr. Redfern shook his head.  

“Too damp.

I’ll take no chances with rheumatism.”  

“Why suffer that ceaseless  [ceaseless - непрестанно] anguish? [anguish - тоска] 

Why not try Redfern’s Liniment?”

quoted  [quoted - цитируется] the imp  [imp - бес] in the back of Valancy’s mind.  

“I must get back to Port Lawrence before rain starts.

Henry goes quite mad when he gets mud on the car.

But I’ll come back tomorrow.

Meanwhile you talk Bernie into reason.”  

He shook her hand and patted her kindly on the shoulder.

He looked as if he would have kissed her, with a little encouragement, [encouragement - поощрение]  but Valancy did not give it.

Not that she would have minded. [minded - мыслящий] 

A score of times over.

And Barney was his son—and heir.  

She took him over in the motor boat and watched the lordly purple car roll away through the woods with Henry at the wheel looking things not lawful to be uttered. [uttered - изрек] 

Then she went back to the Blue Castle.

What she had to do must be done quickly.

Barney _

might_

return at any moment.

And it was certainly going to rain.

She was thankful she no longer felt very bad.

When you are bludgeoned on the head repeatedly, you naturally and mercifully become more or less insensible and stupid.  

She stood briefly  [briefly - кратко] like a faded  [faded - померк] flower bitten by frost, by the hearth, looking down on the white ashes of the last fire that had blazed  [blazed - пылал] in the Blue Castle.  

“At any rate,” she thought wearily, “Barney isn’t poor.

He will be able to afford a divorce. [divorce - развод] 

Quite nicely. [nicely - красиво] ”     

CHAPTER XXXIX   

She must write a note.

The imp  [imp - чертенок] in the back of her mind laughed.

In every story she had ever read when a runaway  [runaway - убежал] wife decamped from home she left a note, generally on the pin- [pin - булавка] cushion.

It was not a very original idea.

But one had to leave something intelligible.

What was there to do but write a note?

She looked vaguely about her for something to write with.

Ink? [Ink - чернила] 

There was none.

Valancy had never written anything since she had come to the Blue Castle, save memoranda of household necessaries for Barney.

A pencil sufficed  [sufficed - хватало] for them, but now the pencil was not to be found.

Valancy absently crossed to the door of Bluebeard’s Chamber and tried it.

She vaguely expected to find it locked, but it opened unresistingly.

She had never tried it before, and did not know whether Barney habitually kept it locked or not.

If he did, he must have been badly upset to leave it unlocked.

She did not realise that she was doing something he had told her not to do.

She was only looking for something to write with.

All her faculties  [faculties - способности] were concentrated on deciding just what she would say and how she would say it.

There was not the slightest  [slightest - малейшее] curiosity  [curiosity - любопытство] in her as she went into the lean-to.  

There were no beautiful women hanging by their hair on the walls.

Used no doubt by Barney in his smelly operations.

Chemical experiments, probably, she reflected dully.

At the other end was a big writing desk and swivel-chair.

The side walls were lined  [lined - подкладка] with books.  

Valancy went blindly  [blindly - вслепую] to the desk.

There she stood motionless for a few minutes, looking down at something that lay on it.

A bundle of galley-proofs.

The page on top bore the title _Wild Honey_, and under the title were the words “by John Foster.”  

The opening sentence—“Pines are the trees of myth  [myth - миф] and legend. [legend - легенда] 

They strike their roots deep into the traditions of an older world, but wind and star love their lofty tops.

What music when old Æolus draws his bow across the branches of the pines— [pines - сосны] ” She had heard Barney say that one day when they walked under them.  

So Barney was John Foster!  

Valancy was not excited.

She had absorbed  [absorbed - поглощенный] all the shocks and sensations  [sensations - ощущения] that she could compass for one day.

This affected her neither one way nor the other.

She only thought:  “So this explains it.”  

“It” was a small matter that had, somehow, stuck in her mind more persistently than its importance seemed to justify. [justify - оправдать] 

Soon after Barney had brought her John Foster’s latest book she had been in a Port Lawrence bookshop and heard a customer ask the proprietor  [proprietor - собственник] for John Foster’s new book.

The proprietor  [proprietor - собственник] had said curtly, “Not out yet.

Won’t be out till next week.”  

Valancy had opened her lips to say, “Oh, yes, it _is_ out,” but closed them again.

After all, it was none of her business.

She supposed the proprietor  [proprietor - собственник] wanted to cover up his negligence in not getting the book in promptly. [promptly - незамедлительно] 

Now she knew.

The book Barney had given her had been one of the author’s complimentary  [complimentary - бесплатно] copies, sent in advance. [advance - аванс]   

Well!

Valancy pushed the proofs indifferently aside  [aside - в сторону] and sat down in the swivel-chair.

She took up Barney’s pen—and a vile one it was—pulled a sheet of paper to her and began to write.

She could not think of anything to say except bald facts.   

“Dear Barney:—  I went to Dr. Trent this morning and found out he had sent me the wrong letter by mistake.

There never was anything serious the matter with my heart

and I am quite well now.  

Is desertion  [desertion - дезертирство] a ground for divorce  [divorce - развод] in Canada?

Of course if there is anything I can do to help or hasten it I will do it gladly, if your lawyer will let me know.  

I thank you for all your kindness to me.

I shall never forget it.

Think as kindly of me as you can, because I did not mean to trap  [trap - ловушка] you.

Good-bye.  Yours gratefully,  VALANCY.”   

It was very cold and stiff, [stiff - жесткая]  she knew.

But to try to say anything else would be dangerous—like tearing away a dam. [dam - плотина] 

She didn’t know what torrent of wild incoherences and passionate  [passionate - страстный] anguish  [anguish - тоска] might pour out.

In a postscript she added:   “Your father was here today.

He is coming back tomorrow.

He told me everything.

I think you should go back to him.

He is very lonely for you.”   

She put the letter in an envelope, [envelope - конверт]  wrote “Barney” across it, and left it on the desk.

On it she laid the string of pearls. [pearls - жемчуг] 

If they had been the beads she believed them she would have kept them in memory of that wonderful year.

But she could not keep the fifteen thousand dollar gift of a man who had married her out of pity  [pity - жалость] and whom she was now leaving.

It hurt her to give up her pretty bauble.

That was an odd  [odd - нечетный] thing, she reflected.

The fact that she was leaving Barney did not hurt her—yet.

It lay at her heart like a cold, insensible thing.

If it came to life—Valancy shuddered and went out——  She put on her hat and mechanically  [mechanically - механически] fed Good Luck and Banjo.

She locked the door and carefully hid the key in the old pine. [pine - сосна] 

Then she crossed to the mainland  [mainland - материк] in the disappearing propeller.

She stood for a moment on the bank, looking at her Blue Castle.

The rain had not yet come, but the sky was dark, and Mistawis grey and sullen.

She felt that she ought to knock like a stranger.

Her rosebush, she idly  [idly - лениво] noticed, was loaded  [loaded - загрузил] with buds. [buds - бутоны] 

The rubber-plant stood beside the prim door.

A momentary  [momentary - мимолетно] horror overcame  [overcame - преодолел] her—a horror of the existence to which she was returning.

Then she opened the door and walked in.  

“I wonder if the Prodigal Son ever felt really at home again,” she thought.  

Mrs. Frederick and Cousin Stickles were in the sitting- [sitting - сидя] room.

Uncle Benjamin was there, too.

They looked blankly  [blankly - тупо] at Valancy, realising at once that something was wrong.

This was not the saucy, impudent thing who had laughed at them in this very room last summer.

This was a grey-faced  [faced - лицо] woman with the eyes of a creature who had been stricken by a mortal  [mortal - смертельный] blow. [blow - удар]   

Valancy looked indifferently around the room.

She had changed so much—and it had changed so little.

The same pictures hung on the walls.

The little orphan who knelt  [knelt - преклонил колени] at her never-finished  [finished - кончил] prayer by the bed whereon reposed the black kitten that never grew up into a cat.

The grey “steel engraving” of Quatre Bras, where the British regiment  [regiment - полк] forever stood at bay. [bay - бухта] 

The crayon enlargement of the boyish  [boyish - мальчишеский] father she had never known.

There they all hung in the same places.

The same elaborate, [elaborate - вычурный]  never-used pitcher stood at the same angle on the sideboard shelf.

The blue and gilt vases that had been among her mother’s wedding-presents still primly adorned  [adorned - украшенный] the mantelpiece, flanking the china clock of berosed and besprayed ware that never went.

The chairs in exactly the same places.

Her mother and Cousin Stickles, likewise  [likewise - тоже] unchanged, [unchanged - неизменный]  regarding her with stony unwelcome.  

Valancy had to speak first.  

“I’ve come home, Mother,” she said tiredly.  

“So I see.”

Mrs. Frederick’s voice was very icy. [icy - ледяной] 

She had resigned  [resigned - смиренный] herself to Valancy’s desertion. [desertion - дезертирство] 

She had almost succeeded in forgetting there was a Valancy.

She had rearranged and organised her systematic  [systematic - систематический] life without any reference  [reference - ссылка] to an ungrateful, rebellious  [rebellious - бунтующий] child.

She had taken her place again in a society which ignored the fact that she had ever had a daughter and pitied her, if it pitied her at all, only in discreet whispers and asides.

The plain truth was that, by this time, Mrs. Frederick did not want Valancy to come back—did not want ever to see or hear of her again.  

And now, of course, Valancy was here.

With tragedy  [tragedy - трагедия] and disgrace  [disgrace - позор] and scandal  [scandal - скандал] trailing  [trailing - плетущийся] after her visibly. [visibly - видимо] 

“So I see,” said Mrs. Frederick.

“May I ask why?”  

“Because—I’m—not—going to die,” said Valancy huskily.  

Nothing else was worrying her; but all at once it seemed that she simply could not endure  [endure - терпеть] that thin, insistent rattle. [rattle - греметь]   

“My home, as I told you, is always open to you,” said Mrs. Frederick stonily, “but I can never forgive you.”  

Valancy gave a mirthless laugh.  

“I’d care very little for that if I could only forgive myself,” she said.  

“Come, come,” said Uncle Benjamin testily.

But rather enjoying himself.

He felt he had Valancy under his thumb  [thumb - большой палец] again.

“We’ve had enough of mystery.

What has happened?

Why have you left that fellow?

No doubt there’s reason enough—but what particular  [particular - особенно] reason is it?”  

Valancy began to speak mechanically. [mechanically - механически] 

She told her tale bluntly  [bluntly - прямо] and barely. [barely - едва]   

“A year ago Dr. Trent told me I had angina pectoris and could not live long.

I wanted to have some—life—before I died.

That’s why I went away.

Why I married Barney.

And now I’ve found it is all a mistake.

There is nothing wrong with my heart.

I’ve got to live—and Barney only married me out of pity. [pity - жалко] 

So I have to leave him—free.”  

“God bless  [bless - благословите] me!” said Uncle Benjamin.

Cousin Stickles began to cry.  

“Valancy, if you’d only had confidence in your own mother——”  “Yes, yes, I know,” said Valancy impatiently. [impatiently - нетерпеливо] 

“What’s the use of going into that now?

I can’t undo this year.

God knows I wish I could.

I’ve tricked  [tricked - обманул] Barney into marrying me—and he’s really Bernard Redfern.

Dr. Redfern’s son, of Montreal.

And his father wants him to go back to him.”  

Uncle Benjamin made a queer  [queer - чудак] sound.

Cousin Stickles took her black-bordered handkerchief away from her eyes and stared at Valancy.

A queer gleam  [gleam - блеск] suddenly shot into Mrs. Frederick’s stone-grey orbs.  

“Dr. Redfern—not the Purple Pill man?”

she said.  

Valancy nodded. [nodded - кивал] 

“He’s John Foster, too—the writer of those nature books.”  

“But—but—” Mrs. Frederick was visibly  [visibly - явно] agitated, though not over the thought that she was the mother-in-law of John Foster—“_Dr.

“Yes.

Barney left home years ago—because of—of some trouble—some—disappointment. [disappointment - разочарование] 

Now he will likely go back.

So you see—I had to come home.

He doesn’t love me.

I can’t hold him to a bond he was tricked  [tricked - обманули] into.”  

Uncle Benjamin looked incredibly sly.  

“Did he say so?

Does he want to get rid of you?”  

“No.

I haven’t seen him since I found out.

But I tell you—he only married me out of pity— [pity - жалость] because I asked him to—because he thought it would only be for a little while.”  

Mrs. Frederick and Cousin Stickles both tried to speak, but Uncle Benjamin waved a hand at them and frowned portentously.  

“Let _me_ handle this,” wave and frown  [frown - нахмурился] seemed to say.

To Valancy:  “Well, well, dear, [dear - дорогой]  we’ll talk it all over later.

You see, we don’t quite understand everything yet.

As Cousin Stickles says, you should have confided  [confided - доверился] in us before.

Later on—I dare  [dare - осмелился] say we can find a way out of this.”  

“You think Barney can easily get a divorce, [divorce - развод]  don’t you?” said Valancy eagerly. [eagerly - нетерпеливо]   

Uncle Benjamin silenced with another wave the exclamation  [exclamation - восклицание] of horror he knew was trembling on Mrs. Frederick’s lips.  

“Trust to me, Valancy.

Everything will arrange itself.

Tell me this, Dossie.

Have you been happy up back?

Was Sr.—Mr.

Redfern good to you?”  

“I have been very happy and Barney was very good to me,” said Valancy, as if reciting a lesson.

She remembered when she studied grammar at school she had disliked the past and perfect tenses.

They had always seemed so pathetic.

“I have been”—it was all over and done with.  

“Then don’t worry, little girl.”

How amazingly  [amazingly - удивительно] paternal  [paternal - по-отечески] Uncle Benjamin was!

“Your family will stand behind you.

We’ll see what can be done.”  

“Thank you,” said Valancy dully.

Really, it was quite decent  [decent - прилично] of Uncle Benjamin.

“Can I go and lie down a little while?

I’m—I’m—tired.”  

“Of course you’re tired.”

Uncle Benjamin patted her hand gently— [gently - нежно]  [gently - нежно] very gently.

“All worn out and nervous.

Valancy smiled wanly.

But she had come back to the old life—the old shackles.

“What?” she asked as meekly as of yore.  

“Not to return it,” said Uncle Benjamin with a chuckle.

He shut the door and rubbed  [rubbed - тер] his hands.

Nodded  [Nodded - кивал] and smiled mysteriously  [mysteriously - загадочно] round the room.  

“Poor little Doss!”

he said pathetically.  

“Do you really suppose that—Snaith—can actually be Dr. Redfern’s son?” gasped Mrs. Frederick.  

“I see no reason for doubting  [doubting - сомневаясь] it.

She says Dr. Redfern has been there; Why, the man is rich as wedding-cake.

Amelia, I’ve always believed there was more in Doss than most people thought.

You kept her down too much—repressed  [repressed - подавленный] her.

She never had a chance to show what was in her.

And now she’s landed a millionaire  [millionaire - миллионер] for a husband.”  

“But—” hesitated Mrs. Frederick, “he—he—they told terrible tales about him.”  

“All gossip and invention—all gossip and invention.

It’s always been a mystery to me why people should be so ready to invent and circulate  [circulate - циркулировать] slanders about other people they know absolutely nothing about.

I can’t understand why you paid so much attention to gossip and surmise.

Just because he didn’t choose to mix up with everybody, people resented  [resented - обиженный] it.

I was surprised to find what a decent  [decent - порядочный] fellow he seemed to be that time he came into my store with Valancy.

I discounted all the yarns then and there.”  

“But he was seen dead drunk in Port Lawrence once,” said Cousin Stickles.

Doubtfully, [Doubtfully - Сомнительно]  yet as one very willing  [willing - готовый] to be convinced to the contrary.  

“Who saw him?” demanded Uncle Benjamin truculently.

“Who saw him?

Old Jemmy Strang _said_

he saw him.

I wouldn’t take old Jemmy Strang’s word on oath. [oath - присяга] 

He’s too drunk himself half the time to see straight.

He said he saw him lying drunk on a bench  [bench - скамейка] in the Park.

Pshaw!

Redfern’s been asleep there.

Don’t worry over _that_.”  

“But his clothes—and that awful old car—” said Mrs. Frederick uncertainly. [uncertainly - неуверенно]   

“Eccentricities of genius, [genius - гениальный] ” declared Uncle Benjamin.

“You heard Doss say he was John Foster.

“I—suppose—we must forgive her,” yielded  [yielded - уступил] Mrs. Frederick.  

“Forgive her!”

Uncle Benjamin snorted.

Really, Amelia was an incredibly stupid woman.

No wonder poor Doss had gone sick and tired of living with her.

“Well, yes, I think you’d better forgive her!

The question is—will Snaith forgive _us_!”  

“What if she persists  [persists - сохраняется] in leaving him?

You’ve no idea how stubborn she can be,” said Mrs. Frederick.  

“Leave it all to me, Amelia.

Leave it all to me.

You women have muddled it enough.

This whole affair has been bungled from start to finish.

If you had put yourself to a little trouble years ago, Amelia, she would not have bolted  [bolted - болтается] over the traces  [traces - следы] as she did.

Just let her alone—don’t worry her with advice or questions till she’s ready to talk.

She’s evidently  [evidently - очевидно] run away in a panic  [panic - паника] because she’s afraid he’d be angry with her for fooling him.

Most extraordinary thing of Trent to tell her such a yarn!

That’s what comes of going to strange doctors.

Well, well, we mustn’t blame her too harshly, poor child.

Redfern will come after her.

If he doesn’t, I’ll hunt him up and talk to him as man to man.

He may be a millionaire, [millionaire - миллионер]  but Valancy is a Stirling.

He can’t repudiate her just because she was mistaken about her heart disease.

Not likely he’ll want to.

Doss is a little overstrung.

Bless  [Bless - благослови] me, I must get in the habit of calling her Valancy.

She isn’t a baby any longer.

Now, remember, Amelia.

Be very kind and sympathetic. [sympathetic - сочувствующий] ”  

It was something of a large order to expect Mrs. Frederick to be kind and sympathetic. [sympathetic - сочувствующий] 

But she did her best.

When supper was ready she went up and asked Valancy if she wouldn’t like a cup of tea.

Valancy, lying on her bed, declined. [declined - отказался] 

She just wanted to be left alone for a while.

Mrs. Frederick left her alone.

She did not even remind Valancy that her plight  [plight - бедственное положение] was the outcome  [outcome - исход] of her own lack of daughterly respect and obedience. [obedience - послушание] 

One could not—exactly—say things like that to the daughter-in-law of a millionaire. [millionaire - миллионер]      

CHAPTER XLI   Valancy looked dully about her old room.

It, too, was so exactly the same that it seemed almost impossible to believe in the changes that had come to her since she had last slept in it.

It seemed—somehow—indecent that it should be so much the same.

Outside, the old carriage- [carriage - карета] shop with its blatant advertisements.

Beyond it, the station with the same derelicts and flirtatious flappers.  

Here the old life waited for her, like some grim  [grim - мрачный] ogre that bided his time and licked  [licked - вылизанный] his chops.

A monstrous horror of it suddenly possessed her.

When night fell and she had undressed and got into bed, the merciful numbness passed away and she lay in anguish  [anguish - тоска] and thought of her island under the stars.

The camp-fires—all their little household jokes and phrases and catch words—their furry beautiful cats—the lights agleam on the fairy islands—canoes  [canoes - каноэ] skimming  [skimming - слив] over Mistawis in the magic of morning—white birches shining among the dark spruces like beautiful women’s bodies—winter snows and rose-red sunset fires—lakes drunken  [drunken - пьяный] with moonshine—all the delights of her lost paradise.

She would not let herself think of Barney.

Only of these lesser things.

She could not endure  [endure - терпеть] to think of Barney.  

Then she thought of him inescapably.

She ached  [ached - болеет] for him.

She wanted his arms around her—his face against hers—his whispers in her ear.

She recalled all his friendly  [friendly - дружеский] looks and quips  [quips - шутки] and jests—his little compliments—his caresses.

She counted them all over as a woman might count her jewels—not one did she miss from the first day they had met.

These memories were all she could have now.

She shut her eyes and prayed.  

“Let me remember every one, God!

Let me never forget one of them!”  

Yet it would be better to forget.

This agony  [agony - агония] of longing and loneliness  [loneliness - одиночество] would not be so terrible if one could forget.

And Ethel Traverse.

That shimmering witch woman with her white skin and black eyes and shining hair.

The woman Barney had loved.

The woman whom he still loved.

Hadn’t he told her he never changed his mind?

Who was waiting for him in Montreal.

Who was the right wife for a rich and famous man.

Barney would marry her, of course, when he got his divorce. [divorce - развод] 

How Valancy hated her!

And envied  [envied - завидовал] her!

Barney had said, “I love you,” to _her_.

Valancy had wondered what tone Barney would say “I love you” in—how his dark-blue eyes would look when he said it.

Ethel Traverse knew.

She would never come to the little Mistawis shack at all.  

What was Barney doing—thinking—feeling now?

Had he come home and found her letter?

Was he still angry with her?

Or a little pitiful.

Was he lying on their bed looking out on stormy Mistawis and listening to the rain streaming down on the roof?

Or was he still wandering  [wandering - блуждание] in the wilderness, [wilderness - пустыня]  raging at the predicament  [predicament - затруднительное положение] in which he found himself?

Hating her?

Pain took her and wrung her like some great pitiless giant.

She got up and walked the floor.

Would morning never come to end this hideous night?

And yet what could morning bring her?

The old life without the old stagnation that was at least bearable.

The old life with the new memories, the new longings, the new anguish. [anguish - тоска]   

“Oh, why can’t I die?” moaned Valancy.     

CHAPTER XLII   

It was not until early afternoon the next day that a dreadful  [dreadful - ужасный] old car clanked up Elm Street and stopped in front of the brick  [brick - кирпич] house.

A hatless man sprang from it and rushed up the steps.

The bell was rung as it had never been rung before—vehemently, intensely.

The ringer was demanding entrance, not asking it.

Uncle Benjamin chuckled as he hurried to the door.

Uncle Benjamin had “just dropped in” to enquire  [enquire - вопросить] how dear Doss—Valancy was.

Dear Doss—Valancy, he had been informed, was just the same.

She had come down for breakfast—which she didn’t eat—gone back to her room, come down for dinner—which she didn’t eat—gone back to her room.

That was all.

She had not talked.

And she had been let, kindly, considerately, alone.  

“Very good.

Redfern will be here today,” said Uncle Benjamin.

And now Uncle Benjamin’s reputation as a prophet  [prophet - пророк] was made.

Redfern was here—unmistakably  [unmistakably - безошибочно] so.  

“Is my wife here?”

he demanded of Uncle Benjamin without preface.  

Uncle Benjamin smiled expressively.  

“Mr. Redfern, I believe?

Very glad to meet you, sir.

Yes, that naughty  [naughty - непослушный] little girl of yours is here.

We have been——”  “I must see her,” Barney cut Uncle Benjamin ruthlessly short.  

“Certainly, Mr. Redfern.

Just step in here.

Valancy will be down in a minute.”  

He ushered  [ushered - ввел] Barney into the parlour  [parlour - гостиная] and betook himself to the sitting- [sitting - сидя] room and Mrs. Frederick.  

“Go up and tell Valancy to come down.

Her husband is here.”  

But so dubious  [dubious - сомнительный] was Uncle Benjamin as to whether Valancy could really come down in a minute—or at all—that he followed Mrs. Frederick on tiptoe up the stairs and listened in the hall.  

“Oh, Mother.”

Valancy got up from the window and wrung her hands.

“I cannot see him—I cannot!

Tell him to go away—_ask_ him to go away.

I can’t see him!”  

“Tell her,” hissed Uncle Benjamin through the keyhole, [keyhole - замочная скважина]  “that Redfern says he won’t go away until he _has_ seen her.”  

Redfern had not said anything of the kind, but Uncle Benjamin thought he was that sort of a fellow.

Valancy knew he was.

She understood that she might as well go down first as last.  

She did not even look at Uncle Benjamin as she passed him on the landing. [landing - посадка] 

Uncle Benjamin did not mind.

Rubbing  [Rubbing - Трёт] his hands and chuckling, he retreated  [retreated - отступил] to the kitchen, where he genially demanded of Cousin Stickles:  “Why are good husbands like bread?”  

Cousin Stickles asked why.  

“Because women need them,” beamed Uncle Benjamin.  

Valancy was looking anything but beautiful when she entered the parlour. [parlour - гостиная] 

Her white night had played fearful havoc with her face.

She wore an ugly old brown-and-blue gingham, having left all her pretty dresses in the Blue Castle.

But Barney dashed  [dashed - тире] across the room and caught her in his arms.  

“Valancy, darling— [darling - душка] oh, you darling little idiot! [idiot - идиотка] 

Whatever possessed you to run away like that?

When I came home last night and found your letter I went quite mad.

It was twelve o’clock—I knew it was too late to come here then.

I walked the floor all night.

Then this morning Dad came—I couldn’t get away till now.

Valancy, whatever got into you?

Divorce, [Divorce - Развод]  forsooth!

Don’t you know——”  “I know you only married me out of pity, [pity - жалость] ” said Valancy, brushing him away feebly.

“I know you don’t love me—I know——”  “You’ve been lying awake at three o’clock too long,” said Barney, shaking her.

“That’s all that’s the matter with you.

Love you!

Oh, don’t I love you!

My girl, when I saw that train coming down on you I knew whether I loved you or not!”  

“Oh, I was afraid you would try to make me think you cared,” cried Valancy passionately. [passionately - страстно] 

“Don’t—don’t!

I _know_.

I know all about Ethel Traverse—your father told me everything.

Oh, Barney, don’t torture  [torture - пытка] me!

I can never go back to you!”  

Barney released her and looked at her for a moment.

Something in her pallid, resolute face spoke more convincingly than words of her determination. [determination - решимость]   

“Valancy,” he said quietly, “Father couldn’t have told you everything because he didn’t know it.

Will you let _me_

tell you—everything?”  

“Yes,” said Valancy wearily.

Oh, how dear he was!

How she longed to throw herself into his arms!

As he put her gently  [gently - нежно] down in a chair, she could have kissed the slender, brown hands that touched her arms.

She could not look up as he stood before her.

She dared not meet his eyes.

For his sake, [sake - ради]  she must be brave.

Never!  “You’ve seen Dad and you know I’m Bernard Redfern.

And I suppose you’ve guessed that I’m John Foster—since you went into Bluebeard’s Chamber.”  

“Yes.

But I didn’t go in out of curiosity. [curiosity - любопытство] 

I forgot you had told me not to go in—I forgot——”  “Never mind.

I’m not going to kill you and hang you up on the wall, so there’s no need  [need - потребность] to call for Sister Anne.

I’m only going to tell you my story from the beginning.

I came back last night intending to do it.

Yes, I’m ‘old Doc.

Redfern’s son’—of Purple Pills and Bitters fame. [fame - слава] 

Oh, don’t I know it?

Wasn’t it rubbed  [rubbed - натирание] into me for years?”  

Barney laughed bitterly  [bitterly - горько] and strode  [strode - ходьба] up and down the room a few times.

Uncle Benjamin, tiptoeing  [tiptoeing - на цыпочках] through the hall, heard the laugh and frowned.

Surely Doss wasn’t going to be a stubborn little fool. [fool - дурак] 

Barney threw himself into a chair before Valancy.  

“Yes.

As long as I can remember I’ve been a millionaire’ [millionaire - миллионер] s son.

But when I was born Dad wasn’t a millionaire. [millionaire - миллионер] 

He wasn’t even a doctor—isn’t yet.

He was a veterinary and a failure at it.

He and Mother lived in a little village up in Quebec and were abominably poor.

I don’t remember Mother.

Haven’t even a picture of her.

She died when I was two years old.

She was fifteen years younger than Father—a little school teacher.

When she died Dad moved into Montreal and formed a company to sell his hair tonic.

He’d dreamed the prescription  [prescription - рецепт] one night, it seems.

Well, it caught on.

Money began to flow in.

Dad invented—or dreamed—the other things, too—Pills, Bitters, Liniment and so on.

He was a millionaire  [millionaire - миллионер] by the time I was ten, with a house so big a small chap  [chap - парень] like myself always felt lost in it.

I had every toy a boy could wish for—and I was the loneliest little devil  [devil - дьявол] in the world.

I remember only one happy day in my childhood, Valancy.

Only one.

Even you were better off than that.

Dad had gone out to see an old friend in the country and took me along.

I was turned loose in the barnyard and I spent the whole day hammering  [hammering - долбящий] nails in a block of wood.

And most things went deep with me.

I was a sensitive  [sensitive - чувствительный]  [sensitive - чувствительный] child and I was even more sensitive as a boy.

No one ever knew what I suffered.

Dad never dreamed of it.  

“When he sent me to a private school—I was only eleven—the boys ducked me in the swimming-tank until I stood on a table and read aloud all the advertisements of Father’s patent  [patent - патентный] abominations.

I did it—then”—Barney clinched his fists—“I was frightened and half drowned  [drowned - утонувший] and all my world was against me.

But when I went to college and the sophs tried the same stunt I didn’t do it.”

Barney smiled grimly.

“They couldn’t make me do it.

But they could—and did—make my life miserable. [miserable - жалкий] 

I never heard the last of the Pills and the Bitters and the Hair Tonic.

‘After using’ was my nickname—you see I’d always such a thick thatch.

My four college years were a nightmare. [nightmare - кошмар] 

You know—or you don’t know—what merciless beasts  [beasts - звери] boys can be when they get a victim like me.

I had few friends—there was always some barrier  [barrier - барьер] between me and the kind of people I cared for.

And the other kind—who would have been very willing  [willing - желающий] to be intimate  [intimate - интимный] with rich old Doc.

Redfern’s son—I didn’t care for.

But I had one friend—or thought I had.

A clever, bookish  [bookish - книжный] chap— [chap - парень] a bit of a writer.

That was a bond between us—I had some secret aspirations  [aspirations - стремления] along that line.

He was older than I was—I looked up to him and worshipped him.

For a year I was happier than I’d ever been.

Then—a burlesque sketch  [sketch - набросок] came out in the college magazine—a mordant thing, ridiculing Dad’s remedies. [remedies - средства] 

The names were changed, of course, but everybody knew what and who was meant.

I found out _he_ had written it.”  

“Oh, were you sure?”

Valancy’s dull  [dull - унылый] eyes flamed with indignation.  

“Yes.

He admitted it when I asked him.

Said a good idea was worth more to him than a friend, any time.

And he added a gratuitous thrust.

‘You know, Redfern, there are some things money won’t buy.

For instance—it won’t buy you a grandfather.’

Well, it was a nasty  [nasty - противный] slam.

I was young enough to feel cut up.

And it destroyed a lot of my ideals and illusions, [illusions - иллюзии]  which was the worst thing about it.

I was a young misanthrope after that.

Didn’t want to be friends with any one.

And then—the year after I left college—I met Ethel Traverse.”  

Valancy shivered.

Barney, his hands stuck in his pockets, was regarding the floor moodily and didn’t notice it.  

“Dad told you about her, I suppose.

She was very beautiful.

And I loved her.

Oh, yes, I loved her.

I won’t deny it or belittle it now.

It was a lonely, romantic boy’s first passionate  [passionate - страстный] love, and it was very real.

And I thought she loved me.

I was fool  [fool - дурак] enough to think that.

I was wildly happy when she promised to marry me.

For a few months.

Then—I found out she didn’t.

I was an involuntary eavesdropper on a certain occasion for a moment.

That moment was enough.

The proverbial fate  [fate - судьба] of the eavesdropper overtook  [overtook - настигла] me.

A girl friend of hers was asking her how she could stomach Doc.

Redfern’s son and the patent- [patent - патент] medicine background.  

“‘His money will gild the Pills and sweeten the Bitters,’ said Ethel, with a laugh.

‘Mother told me to catch him if I could.

We’re on the rocks.

But pah!

I smell turpentine whenever he comes near me.’”  

“Oh, Barney!” cried Valancy, wrung with pity  [pity - жалость] for him.

She had forgotten all about herself and was filled with compassion  [compassion - сострадание] for Barney and rage  [rage - ярость] against Ethel Traverse.

How dared  [dared - осмелился] she?  

“Well,”—Barney got up and began pacing  [pacing - шагать] round the room—“that finished me.

Completely.

I left civilisation and those accursed dopes behind me and went to the Yukon.

For five years I knocked about the world—in all sorts of outlandish places.

I earned enough to live on—I wouldn’t touch a cent of Dad’s money.

Then one day I woke up to the fact that I no longer cared a hang  [hang - повесить] about Ethel, one way or another.

She was somebody I’d known in another world—that was all.

But I had no hankering to go back to the old life.

None of that for me.

I was free and I meant to keep so.

I came to Mistawis—saw Tom MacMurray’s island.

My first book had been published the year before, and made a hit—I had a bit of money from my royalties. [royalties - гонорар] 

I bought my island.

But I kept away from people.

In fact, I’m afraid I suggested a few of them myself.

By mysterious remarks  [remarks - замечания] which people interpreted  [interpreted - интерпретированный] in the light of their own prepossessions.  

“Then—you came.
